THE BED PAGE.
THE WITTIEST WOMAN IN THE WORLD
'V'S'T'HO t France at least might say
WW Sophie Arnould, and it would
" * be difficult to justify greater
claims for another. “Beautiful,
gracious,” saysGaboriau, “charming enough
to make the Pompadour jealous ; of so bright
a spirit that she could lend wit to Beau-
marchais ; as sceptical as Diderot ; more
luxurious and lavish than Beauj on ; cynical
enough to make La Clos blush ; and finally,
by Garrick’s admission, an actress of genius ;
—such was Sophie Arnould. Hers was the
eighteenth century, and she has all the
seductions, all the licenses; graces, elegances,
attractions, audacities of her epoch.” Born in
1744, she. made her debut at the Opera before
she was fourteen jrnars old, and from her first
appearance had prodigious success. For
twenty years she remained a queen of the
stage, caressed and adored. Love and fame
she knew in fullest measure ; gold was poured
into her lap. Then, as she grew old, the
fickle public forsook her, and even (to its
disgrace) ridiculed her without pity. She
spent years of poverty and wretchedness,
forgotten by everyone. Then a pathetic
appeal to the Government was successful,
and her last days passed in comparative
peace. She died in LBO2. ‘Her last words
to her confessor were: “I am like the
Magdalene, much will be forgiven me be-
cause...! have loved much.”
J*
One may renew acquaintance with this
brilliant creature in Sophie Arnoidd, Actress
and Wit, by R. B. Douglas (Paris, Chas.
Carrington, 13 Faubourg Montmartre ; 215.).
In a handsome volume, adorned with fine
engravings by Lalauze, Arnould is worthily
treated apparently for the first time in Eng-
lish. Mr. Douglas’s work is good and sym-
pathetic ; and, despite lapse of time, his
volume holds no small share of the original
fascination of his subject. It is a book to be
treasured. Of its atmosphere of the de-
cadent French monarchy one can give no
idea. Sophie herself is shown scintillating
continually. Her caustic wit was exercised
at the expense of all, but plrhaps especially
against operatic rivals ; and it is no wonder
that she was hated and feared. One per-
former, Mile. Peslin, angrily told Sophie
never to mention her name again. “Say
nothing either good or bad about me ! ”
“ Oh, my dear,” cried Sophie, “I am afraid
I shall not be able to more than half obey
you.”
After ruining a host, Mile. Beau-
menard married one of the actors of the
theatre. Her charms by that time had faded,
and she led a tolerably regular life after her
marriage. Alluding to her early,career, Some-
one said that she was then like a weather-cock,
veering round to a fresh admirer every day.
“Yes,” answered the ever-ready Sophie,
“ and very like a weather-cock in this also,
that she did not become fixed till she was
rusty.” Mile. Guimard, another butt for
Sophie’s wit, was' very tall and thin,’ and
many jokes were made about her long, lean
figure. Sophie Arnould said that when she
saw Mile. Guimard dance a pas de trois
with two male' dancers, Gardel and Dauber-
val, it put her in mind of two dogs fighting
for a bone.
A writer named Chevrier was in the pay
of the police, to whom ‘he used to purvey all
the scandal of the theatre. Being threat-
ened by a person whom he had libelled, he
went to Holland, where he died, and the
rumour went- that he had been poisoned/
“Poisoned! ” cried Sophie Arnould when
she heard it, “ then he must have sucked his
own pen ! ” So reckless was she that she
did not scruple to jeer even at Madame de
Pompadour. A report reached the Lieu-
tenant of Police that at a supper party, given
at Sophie’s "house, some lampoons on the
Pompadour had been sung or recited. The
official called upon the actress one morning,
and the f- ' ing dialogue ensued :
“ Mademoiselle, where did you sup last night? ”
“ I don’t remember.”
“ You supped at home ? ”
“ Very possibly.”
“You had company ? ”
“ Most likely.”
“Aifiongst your guests were some persons of
high rank ? ”
“ That does happen sometimes.”
“ Who were those persons ? ”
“ I don’t remember.”
“ You don't remember the names of the persons
who supped with you last night ? ”
“No, sir.”
“But it seems to me that a woman like you
ought to remember things of that sort.”
“Yes,” replied Bophie, “but before a man like
you I am not a woman like me.”
Thereupon the Lieutenant of Police
walked away crest-fallen. This reply pro-
cured the actress a day’s imprisonment.
Mile. Clairon refused to play her part, in
an actress’s sulk. The Opera was under the
personal control of the monarch, and an
officer was sent to arrest Mile. Clairon.
“You are sent to arrest me?” said the lady.
“ Yes,” said the officer.
* ‘ By whose order ? ”
“ By the King’s order.”
“ I obey. The king may do what he pleases
with my person or with my property. My honour
he cannot touch.”
And, said Sophie, when she heard of this
speech : “Of course he can’t. Where there
are no effects, the King cannot seize.”
Jt
Mile. Allard, a pretty and young woman,
whose very numerous love affairs had as-
tonished and almost shocked even the
nymphs of the opera house, was once boast-
ing in the green room of the suceess she had
met with in a boy’s part she was then play-
ing. ‘‘l am sure half the audience thought
I was a man,” she declared. “Perhaps so,
dear,” said Sophie, maliciously, “but the
other half knew you were not.” It was
Sophie Arnould, by the way, who originally
said that the best way to support the opera
was to lengthen the ballets and shorten the
skirts.
Sophie met a doctor with whom she was
acquainted crossing a wood on his way to visit a
patient. An ardent sportsman, he was carrying
a gun in case he came across any game on his
road. Sophie asked him where he was going, and
he replied that he was bound for a neighbouring
village to see a sick man. The actress glanced at
the gun and said quietly, “Are you afraid of
missing him ? ”
These are some of the sayings of Sophie’s
prosperous period. “Unfortunately,” says,
Gaboriau, “success turned her head. One
could make a book of nothing but her
follies.” Ingenuously she herself admitted
that to Yoltaire when the great writer called
upon her. “Madame,” he said, “I am
eighty-four years old. I have committed
eighty-four foolish acts.” “Dear me,” said
Sophie, “that’s nothing. lam not yet
forty, and I have committed already more
than a thousand.” With such stories is
Douglas’s book filled, and the tithe of them
has not been gathered.
A REAL ANTHOLOGY
Dear Sir,—lt has reached my ears that several
Eersons, assuming names resembling my own,
ave commenced to compile anthologies of Aus-
tralian poetry. To prevent base and spurious
rivals from howling, I beg to state that I have been
engaged on this work for the past 500 years, and
have secured works hitherto unpublished by .the
poets in question.—Yours, etc., 1.0. Stiffuns.
P. S. —I enclose the f ollowing specimen anthology
for your perusal.
O’D— wd is etymological:
LANGUAGE.
I am the morbifuge of mind,
The alkahest of oaths ;
With picric phraseog rams,. Mankind
I separate from clothes ;
With verbal volts electrify
God’s guernseyed anthropoids,
Who, in arenas, avidly
Pursue prolate spheroids.
Archaic epithets I boom
In corybantic chords ;
Cacophonously I exhume
Lexicographic hoards.
Stentorian styptics I apply
To languages unstrung ;
I, Tarrawingee, fluxify
Each triturated tongue.
For euphony alone I live,
Combined with epigram;
For normal speech I do not give
A polyandrous dam.
Though Juggernaut of Speech ahead
May forge xvith Age’s ■weight,
Its petrol-perfumed 'path I spread
With torpid tacks of Hate.

THE BALLAD OF ZOPHAR THE NAAMATHITE.
The boils like Western sunset blazed and bled , .
Chrome rail the elemental matter’s tracks ;
Crepusculous, the hybrid Hebrew said,
“ Why bibber thus thy rutlish coronachs ? ”
Said Job, the blainey Jew : “ My neck is cursed
Carbunculously by my gutless gods ;
The nuances of kosher liebenvursts
Serve but to pungify my emera&s.”
Then Zophar spake : “ 0 carcinomous lout!
Take from der shynagogue thy Jrested hairs !
Vot, jettison thy Makers! Cast dem oudt
Undt breat’ to dem no more thy sclilenter prayers l ”
Poised Job himself upon his twingeing toes,
And dirtier than Sodom’s dust, a right
Swung he upon the figure-six-like nose,
And there was peace in Palestine that night.

THE GARDEN OF GIBLS.
Reach down the sky to make a quilt
To cover me at night,
As broad and blue as all the verse
That I in frenzy write.
Pluck me some stars before I give
The firmament a rest,
To wear when next I do the Block,
As buttons on my vest.
My garden's planted thick with girls
■ Ablaze with budding charms ;
Their blooms are scarlet glowing Kps,
Their branches shining arms.
The cultivator’s boundless love ;
The planter’s fervent bliss ;
Hark while I tell you of the tarts
That I, Gr —nt II — rv — y, kiss !

And bring some bosoms white,

There is no man in history like noble William Green,
The noblest best Australian lad who ever yet was seen ;

’Twas there the Melbourne populace his daring, deed did
meet,
Down at the awful fire at 34 M’Guiness-street,
Where a girl was shrieking wildly from the 27th floor,
And her parents wept they’d not see their darling daujhier

And in all the books of history no braver deed was seen

H.G.
“ The repudiation of demonstration in any but
immediate and verifiable cases that this Scepticism
of the Instrument amounts to, the abandonment of
any universal validity for moral and religious pro-
positions, brings ethical, social, and religious teach-
ing into the province of poetry, and does something
to correct the estrangement between knowledge and
beauty that is a feature of so much mental existence
at this time.’’ Clearly, a man who writes like that
doesn’t know his writer’s business. But it is H.
G. Wells ! in the appendix to A Modern
Utopia (Lond., Unwin; 2s. 6 d.). Then H. G. Wells
does n’t know his writer’s business. The sentence
is not typical, but it is not singular. A Modern
Utopia is difficult to read, and counts for nothing
because Wells has not force enough to handle
abstract ideas firmly. He wants the Concrete to
make his image clear. In Anticipations he had
it; and Anticipations was a good book. In
Mankind in the Making he was losing it ;
and Mankind was pretty fair. Now, in
A Modern Utopia, lie has lost it; and his abstrac-
tions remain unvitalised ghosts. As a philosophic
romancer Wells is brilliant, triumphant; because
he has people and a plot to collect and focus the
rays of his delicate imagination. His philosophic
speculations are too far diffused.
Noticing a German translation of George
Moore s Evelyn Innes and Sister Teresa, Jhe
Academy points out that German critics consider
Evelyn Innes and Owen Asher the artistic
types of psychological drawing since Flaubert,
and urge that “in them Moore has attained the
zenith of soul-analysis.” This is little more than
Moore’s due. As realistic writer he is the finest
modern artist in English, and the next English
generation may realise it.
In Waves of Fate (Lond., Blackwood; 2s. 6d..)
Edward Nobie writes a clever story that will
intensify an hour and leave a memory. (Inciden-
tally, he shows Katherine Thurston how the
doppel-ganger may be truthfully handled John
Chilcote” was a bright impossible.) The bea
looms large : Noble views it romantically, yet prac-
tically ; Conrad’s mystery is not in his mental fibre.
He makes excellent we'ar-a-day fiction and a little
better: one thinks he could make a lot better.
But "that would mean larger space, closer work,
and (possibly) a book less saleable. As current
reading his work serves well as it stands. It is
Mr. Noble’s ill or good luck that I laubert and
Meredith have preceded him.
“ I am sixty-five years of age,” said Sir Hiram
Maxim to an interviewer, “ and I remember dis-
tinctly that my father told me that his grand-
father had said that England was evidently going
to the dogs.” But in the fable, you remember,
the wolf really did come at last.
Edison believes that he invented the call
“ Hello ! ” fof the telephone. He has a habit of
saying “ Hello ! ” to everybody, and naturally
shouted it into the ’phone. But why do yo u say
“Hello”? As good as anything else, to be
sure; yet why not anything else ? Imitation
habit—law. One would not be surprised to learn
that all motion started from all rest precisely in
that way—by the variation of a single enterpris-
ing molecule.
A. H. Full wood writes to a friend in Australia :
The Academy, so far as a few of us are concerned, is of
no use this year. Some of the boys are hung; but
Streeton is the only one on the line—really on the line.
The curious thing is that Streeton’s subject is of figures—
and very beautiful, / think. Other ©pinions vary, but all
ao-ree to beautiful colour. He doesn’t pretend to draw the
figure in an academic way, but he gets something more
into his work than mere drawing. A pure technician, of
Bourse, condemns such an outlook ; but some of the finest
works of art have been so on account of a certain naive-
ness which, of course, the mechanical mind fails to
understand. , . , ...
Lambert is hung fairly high, but in a good position, in
good company ; Longstaff the same. Minns has a small
water-colour, his first in the Academy ; and I am very
glad for he has tried for a long time. Roberts is clean
biffed, also myself and Mahony—who, by the way, seems
to have left this earth, for no one ever sees him. Roberts,
Streeton, and myself are in the New Gallery (two to me),
so that there is some record for the year, and the New
Gallerv is as good as the R.A., but not such a large show,
more especially in the eyes of the public.
May MeCtyires gives space to a Harvard pro-
fessor’s encomium of a Scotch-American teacher
named Davidson, who spoke things heard in this
vicinity. “He said it was a fixed rule with him
to form no regular habits. When he found him-
self in danger of settling into even a good one, he
made a point of interrupting it. Habits and
methods make a prisoner, of a man, destroy his
readiness, keep him from answering the call of the
moment. Individualist d outrance. Davidson felt
that every hour was a unique entity, to whose
claims one should lie open.
A.G.B.

A CATALOGUE OF SYMPTOMS.
The Case of Mrs. M. WING.
(By a Sydney Reporter.)
Events which have evidently left an
impression upon the mind never to be
obliterated—events which at one time
were beheld with grief and dismay—
are here related by Mrs. Maria Wing,
of Dora-street, Hurstville, upon whom
a pressman recently called, and was
courteously accorded, an interview.
“It is twelve years ago now,” ex-
plained Mrs. Wing, “since I took Cle-
ments Tonic for indigestion and neu-
ralgia, but if it were twice that time I
should never forget how much I owe
to that remedy for its powerful cura-
tive i nil uence.”
“Had your afflictions been ini exis-
tence long ?”
•‘hjiute seven years, so you can ga-
ther from that what a wreck I was.
Even from childhood I had been a
great sullerer from nervousness, and
each year found that complaint more
fully developed then ever. When neu-
ralgia started, the pains in my jaws,
all about my head, behind my eyes,
and at the back of my neck and right
down my shoulders were unendurable.
Night after night passed by and not
an hour's sleep, with the result that,
by-and-bve, 1 felt almost dazed for the
want of rest. This, was not
the full extent of my adversities, for I
daresay you know what indigestion is
like? It meant that whenever I took
any food—and I did not have much at
a time, iny appetite being so poor—l
got a feeling m the chest like a tight-
ness, as if I had overeaten, and a
nasty, full, uncomfortable feeling also
affected me in the stomach. But the
bilious attacks I had were horrible.
Some days, from the time I got up in
the morning till the sun went down in
the evening, I could do nothing but
retch, and on those particular occa-
sions I had such a fearful sensation in
my head that I could not hold it up.
Talk about being depressed—why, that
was no name f t or it ; I was absolutely
melancholy, and what also' helped me
to be so was a continual presentiment
that something was going to happen.
Try how 1 would I could not get out
of my head the idea that some kind of
trouble was awaiting me, and if it
didn't come to-day I was sure it would
arrive to-morrow. Oh ! it was a life
that one should be pitied for leading,
and I'm sure I’ll do all I can to help
other poor women out of similar pre-
dicaments. Now I know that an infal-
lible cure is within the roach of every-
body, I consider it but right that I
should make known to the world what
that cure is, and for this reason alone
1 am actuated in going into full de-
tails. Yes, when those bilious attacks
were on I was so upset generally that
I could not oven take a drink of water.
Nothing whatever would stay on the
stomach, so it’s nothing to marvel at
that I got as thin as a shadow and as
weak as a kitten. And the giddiness
that I had to contend with. It used to
come on suddenly, and almost cause
me to fall sometimes, and it was
strange how my eyes felt so swollen
and the sight bad besides.” .
“Those are symptoms arising from
the disordered state of the liver,”- re-
marked the writer.
“Exactly, and further evidence in
support of that lay in the fact that
my mouth had a bitter taste, and 1
had a white coating over my tongue ;
and not until I had taken- some Cle-
ments Tonic could I get rid of those
symptoms cither.”
“\vhat medicine had you taken pre-
viously ?”
‘‘Nearly every kind of pills and me-
dicines ever heard of, and there was
not one of them, that was worth my
while to refer to. Such useless. trash 1
didn’t think possible could exist, and
it just shows you how advisable it is
to relate such experiences as mine so
that people's money should not be
thrown a-way. Doctors', too, they used
to attend me and prescribe medicine,
but it never came to anything. I felt
no better after taking it, and that is
why, to tell you the truth. I started
to lose hope, and think that i was
never going to get rid of my com-
plaints again. One thing that caused
me no small amount of alarm was pal-
itation of the heart, which used to
e particularly troublesome when lying
down at night, but Clements Tonic
soon set my mind at rest by making
the heart’s pulsation regular and
steady, and at the same time I ob-
served a great falling off in the bother
1 previously had with flatulence. My
chest became eased of its burdensome
feelings, and when I had been using
Clements Tonic a little while the dis-
comforts to the stomach after meals
was entirely absent. So far so good, L
thought ; but there was a lot for Cle-
ments Tonic to do still, if it w'as only
equal to the occasion.”
‘‘And was it equal to all your re-
quirements ?'
“Yes ; every one of them, and what
a lucky woman I was to be sure ! Just
fancy being almost mad with pain like
1 frequently used to be, to fruitlessly
spend money in all directions trying to
get relief, to become hopeless and de-
spondent, and then, at last, to get a
medicine hitherto overlooked which
was the very one that could cure me 1
Don’t you think I had cause to be
deeply grateful ? Ah, well 1 I sincerely
trust that my experience may rt ach
the ears of others, who will soon
know that I have spoken nothing- but
the truth about Clements Tonic. With-
out exaggeration, that remedy set me
entirely free from all the pains I have
mentioned, gave me such an appetite
that I could eat anything that was
going, and so invigorated my nervous
system that I could stand any kind of
noise without trembling, and as for
having ugly dreams or spending my
time in worrying over imaginary trou-
bles ahead, why, I never thought of
such things after being on Clements
Tonic a few weeks. And if anything
will make a person sleep soundly it is
Clements Tonic, for by using it it be-
came a regular thing for me to fall off
shortly after getting into bed, and
not till nearly breakfast time would I
wake again. Tt was a delightful change,
and it will give you an idea of the
perfect cure it was when I tell you
that I am now heavier than I have
ever been before.” .
“Well, that ought to be a good in-
dex as to your health.”
“And so it is, for I can now. work
well and enjoy the privilege of living,
whereas before taking Clements Tonic
life was only a drudgery that was not
worth enduring. I have advised dozens
of people to take Clements Tonic, and
shall always do so. You also have my
permission to publish this interview in
any way you like for the benefit of the
general community.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, MARIA WING, ot Dora-strcet,
HurstvMe, In the State ol New South
Wales, Commonwealth ol Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that It contains and
is a true and faithful account of my Illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish In anv
way my statements —which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Hurstvllle this
twenty-third day of September, one thousand
nine hundred and four, before me.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Hour of Dawn.
The great, slow brain of the earth is waking
O’er the solar skies ;
The white Dawn comes, and the light is breaking
From her half-shut eyes.
As slow she moves with her long skirts trailing
O’er the forest rims,
She lifts her robe of the grey nun’s veiling
From her gleaming limbs.
The praise of birds she has stayed to listen
Where the locked boughs meet;
Heroold cheek warms and the dewdrops glisten
On her bare white feet.
All sounds are still, and the soul is shaken
As it stands alone ;
The best and worst in the heart must waken
That the heart has known.
And darkness falls, by the day supplanted ;
With her eyes alight,
The Morning stands with her fair feet planted
On the prostrate Night.
And strong hearts glow, and weak ones sicken
At the sound of strife,
Of thoughts that leap and deeds that quicken
In the womb of life.
N.S.W.
LOLA RIDGE.
The Tracks that Lie by India.
Now this is not a dismal song, like some I’ve sung
of late,
When I’ve been brooding all day long about my
muddled fate;
For though I’ve had a rocky time I’ll never quite
forget,
And though I never was so deep in trouble and in
debt,
And though I never was so poor nor in a fix so
tight—
The tracks that run by India are shining in my
sight.
The roads that run by India, and all the ports of
call—
I’m going back to London first to raise the where-
withal.
I’ll call at Suez and Port Said as I am going past
(I was too worried to take notes when I was that
way last).
At Naples and at Genoa, and, if I get the chance,
I’ll view the vine-clad ridges of the pleasant land
of F ranee.
The track that runs by India goes up the hot Red
Sea—
The other side of Africa is far too dull for me.
(I fear that I have missed a chance I’ll never get
again
To see the land of chivalry and bide awhile in
Spain.)
I’ll graft a year in London, and if fortune smiles
on me
I’ll take the track to India by France and Italy.
’Tia sweet to court some foreign girl with eyes of
lustrous glow,
Who does nob know my language and whose
tongue I do not know;
To 101 l on gently-rolling decks beneath the soft’ning
skies,
While she sits knitting opposite—and make love
with our eyes.
The glance that says far more than words, the old
half-mystic smile —
The track that runs by India will wait for me
awhile.
The tracks that run by India to China and Japan,
The tracks where all the rovers go—the tracks
that call a Man !
I’m wearied of the formal lands of parson and of
priest,
Of dollars and of “ fashions,” and I’m drifting
towards the East;
I’m tired of cant and cackle, and of sordid
jobbery—
The misty way# of Asia are calling unto me.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
Two Flowers.
A slender lily fallen in the dust,
Its poor, wan fragrance wasted on the ground ;
Tarnished more sorely by each passing gust
And wayward footfall —Innocence discrowned.
She of the golden hair, the laughing eyes,
Pale-robed and garlanded with sweet perfume,
Strays in her walk where the sad lily lies,
And pities, ah 1 but passes the poor bloom.
The bitter afternoon fades into night.
The glow of many lamps along the pave
Shows, startled, halting in their searching light,
One of those passion-flowers He came to save.
She stood a painted girl, with flaming cheeks,
Great haunting eyes made vixen-bold with wine :
A feeble sinner of three wretched weeks,
Trembling, and broken-hearted at a sign ;
The broken lily, lying, on the road,
Spurned by the careless stream that pours along
And, passing, spurns her also with the goad
Of sneering looks, the falt’ring in the throng.
What matter that the press was curious?
She kissed the lily, and they laughed aloud ;
They held her kisses cheap and spurious.
But she had found a friend among the crowd.
A friend, ah me ! She hurried on again
To where the great waste waters flow and flow;
And when the lily touched her breast, the pain
Of loneliness-and longing seemed to go.
She kissed her friend again before she slept,
And, speaking softly, told her of it all.
“I loved him well,”'she whispered as she wept;
"But when he tired he mocked me for my fall.”
The river washed the lily’s stains away,
While Death explained to God a lost girl’s pain.
And when the sun rose on another day,
The far white beach held two flowers pure again.
n.s.w.
M.P.

An Object Lesson in Laying
RUBEROiD.
THIS Photograph serves to illustrate the important part RUBEROID
■ is playing in roofing the immense factories of the old world. A staff of
16 men are employed on a section of a roof, on which 600 squares of
RUBEROID were laid. The men may be seen seated on ladders, nailing
and tin-capping the RUBEROID - The courses are lapped two inches, and
the nails are driven two inches apart from the centres. At the left of photo,
two men are engaged in nailing the roofing where it has been tucked in under
the eaves. The fact that RUBEROID ROOFING may be bent into all
corners is only one of its minor merits. Durability is its main feature.
RUBEROID ROOFING
IS WORTH LAYING PROPERLY.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
tv be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
11 ow to A ddress Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ 'The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, ivhether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 8000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications ivill be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But. no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded ivithout stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient

any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good, idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-dr a,um—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JUNE 15, 1905.
THE “PARASITE.”
The Tariff Commission, among its other delays,
wasted some time trying to find out the cause of the
prejudice which exists in Australia against Aus-
tralian manufactured goods. It had already made
quite sure that such a prejudice existed —on that
point the evidence was overwnelming—and it was
Senator Clemons, of Tasmania, who desired to go
further and ascertain the why and wherefore of
the phenomenon. It might have dawned on
Senator Clemons —but apparently it didn’t—that
he was himself a very fair sample of one cause, for
all through the Tariff inquiry he has been so over-
bearing and so truculent towards witnesses who
spoke on behalf of Australian industry, that he
seems ready to represent every Australian manu-
facturer as an enemy. Mr. Clemons’ question
was addressed to piano-manufacturer Beale, of
Sydney, who replied vigorously :
The origin of it is the continual and ceaseless antagon-
ism of newspapers and members of Parliament in their
advocacy of what they consider to be Freetrade. There
is no term of opprobrium too severe to be applied to the
Australian producer of manufactured goods. Our indus-
tries are described year after year as “tottering,”
“coddled,” “bounty-fed,” and so on, and the favorite
phrase of all is “robber industries.” This, said witness,
was more particularly from the Melbourne Argus, and
similar phrases were also used in the local newspapers.
Manufacturers felt it keenly that they should be pro-
nounced “public thieves.” Criticism of this kind was
bound to arouse antagonism in the minds of the public in
regard to Australian industry. He had heard a lady say
that she would use no article of Australian manufacture
in her home if she could obtain an imported one. She
was a lady residing in this State, and born in this State.
He had heard similar statements repeatedly from others.
The Prime Minister had stated, in effect, in his place in
Parliament, that too many members of Parliament, in
their advocacy of Freetrade, denounced Australian indus-
tries, and, on being interrupted, he said, “At least, there
is too much of it from this side of the House.”
Then |there were alarms and incursions in the
Commission, and Eowleb, M.H.R., said weep-
fully :
To say that the Freetrade members of the Federal
Parliament had at any time slandered Australian manu-
facturers was to use language which—unintentionally, he
thought—misrepresented the position. He had been in
the Federal Parliament since its establishment, and he
could not recollect a single instance of this. . . . The terms
complained of were applied to parasitical industries.
* * * *
In this matter Fowler, M.H.R., has a short
memory. One of the most disgraceful scenes in
the House of Representatives took place during
the tariff debate, when Cameron of Tasmania
appeared with an Australian-made plough-share
and an inferior imported one, and violently
■denounced the superior local article as rubbjsh,
and demanded, in effect, that the Australian
industry should be discouraged under the tariff.
And one of the most disgraceful scenes that ever
happened outside the House was when a Melbourne
importer and Freetrader, as representing him-
self and other importers and Freetraders,
was sued by their joint and several agent
or jackal for services rendered in getting the
tariff so fixed as to irretrievably cripple the
Australian distilling industry. A third case arose
only the other day when a Freetrade member of
the Commission wanted to know whether the
conduct of ex-Premier Dibbs of N.S.W., in
speaking to a manufacturer on tariff matters, wasn’t
calculated to lead to corruption—the manufacturer
being regarded as naturally corrupt. Dibbs’
Freetrade predecessor in office had not only
consulted foreign importer M‘Millan about
the tariff, but had taken him into his
Ministry and made him his Treasurer—
but that is another story. And these are
only isolated incidents and slanders amid an
ocean of slander and hostility. The charge which
is laid by honest and intelligent Australians
against most of the “ great ” Freetrade dailies of
this country, and against most members of the
Freetrade or importers’ party, is that they have
laid themselves out, by lying, slander, misrepresen-
tation and a variety of meannesses, to destroy
Australian handicrafts, to deprive Australian
artisans of work and wages and the means of
supporting their families, to block Australian
progress, to vilify Australian work, to damage
Australia’s industrial reputation, to represent its
manufacturers as robbers, cormorants, and parasites
—all of this so that the importing business might
flourish. And as the natural corollary of
this, the bulk of the importers’ press is regarded
by decent and intelligent Australians as a public
enemy, a traitor in the camp, and a spy acting on
behalf of the foe outside our gates. Fowler,
M.H.R., who couldn’t even finish his defence
against the charge of hostility to Australian in-
dustries without relapsing into his old mood and
calling these industries “ parasites,” is a fair
sample of the Freetrader’s mental attitude. The
Freetrader only objects to the parasitical manufac-
turer —but then he regards all Australian manu-
facturers as parasites.
* * * •
New South Wales has always been the home of
the Freetrade craze in Australia. It is there that
the denunciation has been loudest, and that the
impression has been strongest that the manufac-
turer is always an evil-minded person, who wants
to live by subsidies, by aids and steals, and by
exploiting his fellows to their disadvantage. Now
at all times the simplest, handiest, and least
noticeable device whereby an industry can be
“coddled” is by carrying its goods below cost,
and at a cheaper rate than other goods, on
the railways. And, looking at last year’s N.S.W.
railway report (page 9), these remarkable figures
obtrude themselves: —
Average rate
Tons charged for
Carried. carrying 100
tons for one
mile.
Hay, Straw, Chaff .. 116,117 £0 3 2
Grain and Flour (from the
country to the coast, but
not the other way) .. 424,786 3 11
Coal, Coke, Shale.. .. 3,914,901 4 6
Timber, Bark, Seeds, &c... 723,719 6 2
Firewood 226,445 6 6
Vegetables, Agricultural
Implements, Copper,
Tallow, &c 538,606 9 5
Livestock ... ...... .. 154,525 12 3
~'WesLr'".. .. 74,096 16" o
All other goods, including
most manufactured
goods 202,754 1 12 2
These figures distinctly represent the farmer as
the parasite—if there is a parasite—not the manu-
facturer. And it may be mentioned that the
farmer doesn’t get his rebate because his stuff goes
an extra long distance ; the longest average haul-
ages are on wool and live stock—the shortest on
coal. Nor are his hay, corn and chaff carried for
next tonothing because there isalargetradeiri these
things; they constitute the smallest item, save one,
on the list. Next to the farmer, as “ coddled ” and
“subsidised” persons, come other producers of
raw materials, and persons who dig out the “great
natural resources of the country ” to send them
away in trucks. The country pays secret bonuses
to these people to a huge extent yearly by making
up the railway deficit, and helps them in many
other ways besides. And seeing that the Free-
trader never, by any chance, denounces the farmer
as a robber, a cormorant, and a person who wants
to be “ coddled ” and to live on the industry of
others, and yet aims all these and worse epithets
at the much-less-liberally-assisted manufacturer, it
becomes reasonably obvious that it isn’t parasites
but manufactures which the importers’ party
desires to suppress. Only the other day it almost
seemed as if Sheol had broken loose because an
Australian manufacturing industry—the Lithgow
Iron Works—was receiving a railway rebate,
though it turned out to be a much smaller rebate
than the farmer enjoys. And if Sheol didn’t break
loose altogether, it was probably because the
Lithgow Iron Works are not very prosperous at
present, and the importers’ party reckons that
they may be dead in course of time. To accelerate
their deadness a Sydney morning paper published
a leading article in which it denounced the
reduced rate charged to Lithgow as a case of “the
taxpayers, through the railway department, sub-
sidising the works to the extent of about £9OOO a
year in freight concessions.” In 1904, the hay and
chaff business was worth to the N.S.W. railways
only £34,787, and as compared with the wool busi-
ness this microscopic industry was subsidised to
the extent of about £140,000; as compared with
miscellaneous manufactured goods it was subsi-
dised to the extent of over £340,000. It was even
heavily subsidised as compared with Lithgow
rates. The Freetrade party sees nothing dis-
creditable or parasitic in this—neither does
The Bulletin. But because the Freetrade or
importing crowd professes to see something utterly
disgraceful and ruinous in a much smaller aid to a
manufacturing industry, which pays better wages
and works its employees shorter hours, the Free-
trade and importing party stands forth con-
spicuously as one which is hostile to Australian
factories, and would resort to any slander to crush
them out of existence.
■* ■* * *
But there is one thing to be said for the
Australian Freetrader. He is generally ready to
speak kindly even of an Australian manufacturing
industry—if it is owned by foreigners. Only last
week Sydney Telegraph and S.M. Herald both
enthused over an English co. which, so rumor has
it, proposes to start shale and oil works at Capertee
(N.S.W.), provided it gets a further reduction on
the very low railway rates already charged. The
Herald was also excited over a proposed foreign
company which is thinking of canning rabbits at
Forbes, and which will also, probably, want a re-
bate. And though it didn’t say a single good word
about the desirability of helping or encouraging
any Australian-owned manufacture, the Herald
almost wept with pathos in that same article
about the things that would happen if foreign
papital “were to pour in” and start foreign-
owned industries. Further, by way of doing
strict justice to the Freetrader and the importers’
jackal, it must be admitted (a point which was
nearly overlooked) that the locally-owned and
enormously-protected beer industry appears sacred
from their attacks. There is no visible reason for
this, but they never call Tooth a cormorant or
a robber, and they don’t even allude to Toohey as
a subsidised and coddled parasite who lives on the
toil and substance of others. The subsidised or
protected iron, steel, woollen, cotton or agricul-
tural implement business is unspeakably objec-
tionable in the Freetrader’s eyes, and he foamed
with rage about the very small Brotection (4s. per
gallon) formerly given to Joshua Brothers’
Melbourne distillery ; but the subsidised farmer
is the backbone of the country, and against the
protected beer-maker not a voice is raised.
Berhaps a careful inspection of the lists of
brewery shareholders would throw some light on
this anomaly.
THE ANGLO-JAPANESE TREATY.
Within two years or less the British Government
will be called upon to decide as to the renewal or
termination of its alliance with Japan. There is
not the shadow of a doubt as to the direction of that
decision. When Britain, whose traditional policy
in Europe had been for years an attitude of
“splendid isolation/’ affecting to scorn friendships
as “entanglements,” first departed from that
policy to become the ally of an Asiatic Bower,
there were grave doubts in the minds of many as
to the wisdom of abandoning what had passed as
a fixed principle ; in the minds of many others as
to making the first exception in favor, not of a
European but an Asiatic people. But though
those doubts have probably not been removed (the
arguments on which they were founded have
been in no way affected) the liberty to
entertain them has departed from the Britisher.
Japan has fought its way to so high a
position, that Britain—the Britain of present-day
conditions —cannot afford to do without its Asiatic
friend. Britain, because of Cobdenism, is alone
among the great civilised Powers in not being
able to feed itself, and must gather its bread from
the waters. Any war, therefore, that so much as
seriously threatened its mastery of the seas would
mean annihilation. For the same reason of Cob-
denism, Britain has become a land without a
yeomanry, the citizens of which, to an extent
greater than in any other land, are without the
capacity to fight for their homes, and without the
national spirit to face a universal militia system.
Britain, therefore, dare not risk a great war with
the Mikado hostile or neutral. To a very large
extent, Japan owes its present power to Britain’s
aid. Had it not been for British moral sup-
port, the military and naval power of Nippon,
after its first startling display in the Chinese
war, would have been checked by a European
coalition. But, however much Japan leaned on
Britain a decade ago, or five years ago, there is little
dependence now. By the best of all tests Japan
has attained to the dignity of being counted a
war-Power, to which, on land at all events, Britain
must confess equality—if not something else.
He * * *
When Britain accepted Japan as an ally it
was—according to general belief—after Japan had
received a humiliating rebuff in making a similar
request to Russia. Certainly at that time the
Asiatic country was in the position of a suitor,
and an uncertain suitor at that. But when, a
little while hence, Japan discusses the ques-
tion of having Britain as an ally for a future
term of years, it will be in London and
not in Tokio that there will be nervous
fears of a hitch. That Britain might, by a
giant effort, re-assert its old splendor and dare
again a policy of splendid isolation is, of course,
true. In that case London would not have to
wait on Tokio. But there is no practical use in
discussing what might be. What is—that alone
matters. And that shows Britain still wedded to
Freetrade, still dependent on the ocean for its
food, still without a citizen army and with no
indications of any resolution to reform. Under
such conditions Britain needs Japan, must have
the Japanese alliance as the price of national
security. If such an alliance were not entered
into, probably there would be other suitors for the
friendship of the powerful Yellow Man. Though
no other European people would in the past assist
Japan to become a Power, now that it is
a Power few would despise its alliance.
With Japan an enemy, Britain would almost find
its Asiatic possessions in danger, and in the
event of a general European war would have one
of its food-routes blocked. So the Britain of to-
day must have Japan as an ally. It has played a
Frankenstein freak in nation-politics by bringing
the Yellow Man to sit at the European council
table, and part of the price is a certain measure of
dependence on the monster it has created.
m * * *
Tokio knows that as well as London. Behind
the slant eyes of the Mongolian are brains as good
in the matter of diplomacy as any in Caucasian
skulls. As to whether the Jap is the super-man
of the daily press, a marvel of intellectual and
physical pre-eminence, the world, in spite of some
recent events, still entertains occasional doubts.
But certainly the Mikado’s men are not wanting in
appreciation of their own exploits, and are not dis-
posed to under-estimate their future. Therefore
London must expect to pay for the renewal of an
alliance, which is, under present conditions,
necessary, and only to be made unnecessary by a
national regeneration and re-organisation, as to
the beginning of which there is no sign. And
what London pays is of vital importance to Aus-
tralia, since it may be that Australia is put in the
pool when the bargain is being made. A harassed
Roman empire was willing, centuries ago, to leave
its colonies in Britain to be overwhelmed by the
barbarians because the legions were wanted in
Rome. A harassed British empire might one
day be prepared to secure its own safety
with the sacrifice of Australia. Already it is
suggested by one British newspaper, pro-
fessedly “on official authority,” that if Japan
were allowed to colonise Australia, then “Great
Britain and Japan might dominate the world
together,” and from another source comes the
intelligence that one of the first subjects to engage
the attention of the Mikado after the conclusion
of the war with Russia will be the exclusion of his
subjects from Australia, and a threatening demon-
stration by Admiral Togo’s fleet in these waters is
hinted at. Rumor speaks often with a lying
tongue, but a persistent rumor generally has some
foundation of truth, and there are many good
grounds of reason why we should believe that
there is some threat to the Commonwealth in the
growth of Japan’s might.
* * * *
The Australian territory, ever since it was
opened up by the white man, has been a sort of
El Dorado to the Asiatic. The earliest struggles
of this land were against a Chinese invasion, and
the Japanese, no doubt, would like to find an
outlet for their surplus population, if not a
dependency of the Empire they are building up,
in _ Australia. There is a further essentially
Oriental reason why Japan should exercise a new-
born power to strive to humble Australia. The
policy of excluding Asiatics from our territory has
undoubtedly given serious offence, and, even if
there were no material advantage in “taking it
out of Australia,” probably Japan would make
that a. dear object nevertheless. The Oriental
mind is astonishingly servile when oppressed, and
just as insolent when in power. For many, many
years the Asiatic has suffered under the
scorn of the European. Now Japan has set
itself the task of balancing the account. It
is, therefore, quite possible—almost probable—
that when the next treaty of alliance be-
tween Britain and Japan comes to be dis-
cussed, there will he conditions proposed
by Japan seriously affecting this country’s
interests. Australia must set its mind now to
seriously discuss that emergency. If Japan asks
and Britain grants concessions which interfere
with our Australian self-government and our
white-race ideals, what will be the answer of
Australia ?
* # * *
If it is a meek acquiescence, it will involve the
abandonment for ever of the idea of Australian
nationalism, of the dream of holding this island
fortress as a home, perhaps as a refuge, for the
white race. Should Australia consent to sacrifice
its self-government at the bidding of Japan, as it
had never yet done at the bidding of Britain, the
cherishing of national ideals would be absurd,
and we Australians might as well begin to resign
ourselves to finally becoming mean whites, hanging
to the heels of an Asiatic satrapy. Yet a refusal
would be the gravest of all actions. According to the
present temper of British rulers, if Australia were
set in the balance on one side and the friendship of
Japan on the other, Australia would kick the
beam. To refuse to be a pawn given away to
Japan in the war game might involve Australia in
a fight for its existence. If we had our gun fac-
tories, our iron mills and ship-building yards, our
own navy, our manhood trained to the last unit to
carry arms, there would be no reason to face that
prospect with anything but equanimity and forti-
tude. A white race capable of any sort of defence
would not be permitted to be smothered by Asia.
But Australia, as it is now, without even an
ammunition factory, would inevitably perish, going
to its grave shrouded in the calico, the worship of
which brought it to its ruin. It is not, however,
too late, even now at the eleventh hour, to start to
prepare. But the Event knocks at the door.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Butter Commission’s Report.
The final report of the Butter Commission is as
comprehensive and almost as drastic as the subject
demanded—also it is so Socialistic in its recom-
mendations that the wonder is that Premier Bent
and his friends have not ordered it to be burned
by the hangman. The Commission was composed
of a public servant, as chairman, and two strict
anti-Socialists— Henry Gyles Turner, late of the
Commercial Bank, and George Graham, M.L.A.
and farmer. Put briefly, it recommends that laws
be enacted putting the whole butter industry
under control of the State, through a Board to be
appointed. This Board would license all factories,
refuse licenses where factories are unnecessary,
cancel licenses where conditions and regulations
are not complied with, appoint supervisors over
factories and creameries, certify as to the compe-
tence of factory managers, &c., take over at a
valuation private factories for the benefit of the
suppliers, inspect balance-sheets, and call
for any returns it might think fit, prohibit
imitations and adulterations, insist on all
boxes, packages, &c., being correctly branded
with net weights ; insist on all milled or
blended butter being branded and sold as
“renovated butter”; inspect and pass, from a
sanitary point of view, all factories, creameries,
etc.; fix a standard for butter—said standard to
be not less than 80 per cent, of butter fat nor
more than 15 per cent, of moisture; administer
the Health Act as regards butter factories, etc,;
see that all butter for export is consigned to the
Government Cool Stores and is there graded by
Government experts, who shall further advise the
manufacturer as to any defects. If these recom-
mendations were fully carried out they would prac-
tically put the whole of the butter industry, from
its starting point at the under surface of the cow
right to the ship’s hold, under the direct control
of the State Export Department —which is a
pretty considerable instalment of Socialism.
The Commission further demands Federal
legislation as to standardising weights, con-
trolling brands and compelling the tricky
trader to put 16 ounces in his lib.
tins, etc. ; but the States will have to
take control of the primary and minor details of
the industry. The report, incidentally, points
out that, of the £103,599 paid by the State as a
butter export bonus, nearly half went into the
pockets of the agents; and that the whole
“ private enterprise ” system is rotten, inasmuch
as secret commissions, rebates, and direct bribes
have been given to factory managers, directors,
and contractors for supplies, &c., thus ruining the
honest dealer or manufacturer, and inducing a
system of trickery and roguery. Also it tells Pre-
mier Bent that the facts disclosed in the evidence
about the Victoria Creamery and Butter Co. anc.
the Fresh Food and Frozen Storage Co.—it
specially names seven commercial men in this con-
nection —should be brought under the notice of the
Crown Law authorities. Altogether it is a sudden
and refreshingly honest report, and, as neither
Bent, Carruthers, nor the two_ half-Premiers are
likely to take much action on it unless someone
prods them violently from the rear, it may be
advantageously pointed out to the dairy-farmer
generally—and to him of Victoria in particular—
that a large sum of money has been spent, much
hard work has been done, and a great de jv »
obloquy has been incurred by Australia m general,
for his benefit and advantage, and chiefly because
he would not take the straight and sensible roaa
that was pointed out to him when he started
butter exporting; and if he fails to force these
dilatory Ministers to push the Commission s work
to its logical conclusion, and prefers to continue
denouncing Socialism, this country will probably
take him at his word, and his last case will 6
worse than his first. And he won’t be the only
sufferer, by any means; the wheat-grower and
the orchardist are being exploited nearly as rutn-
lessly as the dairy-farmer.

Salvation by Polysyllables.
Brisbane Worker, which, one way and another,
continues to add to the difficulties of the " a .°£
party, and is frequently quoted with great glee
by its enemies, has lately had another viole
attack of “sinking the fiscal issue Senator
Stewart wrote to protest against the absurd
of being a political ostrich, and pretending to sink
something that can’t be sunk, and never is sunk ,
and the Worker replied in two columns ot poi)


syllables, of which the conclusion was that the
only hope for the world is through “ the growth
of class-consciousness towards a revolutionary
change in the basis of society.” The Bulletin
doesn’t know for sure what this farrago means,
but it does know for sure that Walker’s foundry
at Maryborough employed 1000 hands a few years
ago, under the comparatively high Queensland
State Protective tariff, whereas it employs only
125 now, under the Federal tariff, which G. H.
Reid and other Freetraders whittled away until
it gave no Protection worth mentioning. Yet
Australia has needed the things that Walker’s
foundry could supply; but, instead of getting
them from Maryborough, it has got them from
Belgium, Brummagem, or elsewhere ; instead of
putting wages into the pockets of Queensland
-rtisans, it has sent the money off to the foreigner,
and thereby thrown 875 Queenslanders out of a
job. And the Queensland artisan, crying for a
crust, is offered a quantity of long words. His
children, asking for food, are told not to be so
stupid, but to feed on the hope of “the growth
of class-consciousness towards a revolutionary
change in the basis of society.” What will happen
to them while the class-consciousness is growing
to edible size isn’t explained. The same beautiful
words of six syllables have been filling the same
weary columns for many years, and, presumably,
class-consciousness has been growing. But what
advantage has it been to the blacksmiths, the
foundrymen, the laborers who have been thrown
out of a job because the tariff isn’t high enough to
shut out the products of the cheap-labor foreigner?
As a matter of fact, the growth of class-conscious-
ness has, in many cases, simply made matters
worse for the Australian workman ; instead of fill-
ing his cupboard, it has emptied it ; instead of
warmly clothing his children, it has turned them out
in rags. For the trouble has been that, though there
has been a growth of class-consciousness here,
the blessed thing hasn't budged an inch in some
other places. While it has secured for the Aus-
tralian workman an eight-hour day, the Chinese
and the Jap still work 12 hours a day. While it
has, through the Arbitration Courts, obtained for
the Australian workmen an order that they shall
be paid, say, 9s. a day, it hasn’t stopped the
foreign employer getting the same class of work-
men for ss. a day. Class-consciousness has grown
so much in Australia that the workman isn’t
permitted, so to speak, to go more than three
miles an hour. But the foreign workman is
prodded till he goes six miles an hour ; and then
the Worker expects the Australian workman to
arrive at the same time as his rival. Of course he
doesn’t do anything of the sort; the foreigner gets
there first, empties the cupboard, and leaves
nothing but the sack and a number of polysyl-
lables for the Australian. The Worker’s beautiful
theory might pan out all right if the growth it
talks about were a uniform thing. If it would
leave off preaching in Australia, and transport
itself to China, and water the plant there, and
grow class-consciousness on the banks of the
Hoang-ho until Sing Fat would scorn to take less
than Bs. 4d. a day of eight hours; and if it then
brought every foreign country up to the level of
Australia in the matter of hours and conditions of
work, it might sink the fiscal issue. But so long
as the foreigner will work for less wages, or under
worse conditions, than the Australian, by consent-
ing to Freetrade the Worker simply drags the
Australian down to the conditions of the country
where there is least of that class-consciousness that
it talks about.
What the Freetrader is Thankful For.
Official figures for the Commonwealth, now
issued by N.S.W. Acting-Statist Hall, knock a
rather large hole in some statistics lately supplied
by the German Consulate relating to German
trade with Australasia; likewise, they knock a
somewhat larger hole in some elaborate Freetrade
arguments built upon the ricketty foundations of
the Consulate’s figures. These latter stated Aus-
tralasian exports to Germany for 1903 at
£6,001,050; imports, £2,238,000; excess of Aus-
tralasian exports over imports, £3,763,050. The
official Commonwealth statistics, however, show
that the Australian imports from Germany
amounted to £2,358,553, and the exports to
£3,134,638. A very small part of the £2,866,412
by which the two sets of export figures differ is
accounted for by Maoriland exports-; by far ther
greater part is made up of freight, &c., from Aus-
tralia to Germany, from which, of course, Aus-
tralia derives no advantage whatever. Most of
the exports consist of bulky products, practically
all of them raw articles, such as ores and concen-
trates, wools, hides, fat, &c., upon -which the
freight is exceedingly high in proportion to the
value of the goods. The S.M. Herald, waiting
when the German figures were published, pointed
to them as proof that Australia was doing very
nicely and needn’t worry itself about “ hot-house ”
manufactures; also that the British manufacturer
needn’t worry either. “ The balance of £3,763,050
is placed to our credit in London to pay for British
goods or for other purposes. ... If this trade
with Germany were not done, we should have
£3,763,050 less to spend among British
manufacturers.” And now we find that tb s
balance of £3,763,050 is largely a myth and
the Herald’s argument largely mud. But even
if it weren’t, what a contemptible attitude for
an Australian paper to take up to rejoice
in the exportation of millions of pounds’ worth of
skins, and crude ore. and raw wool (much of it
without even the dirt washed off it), and to be
glad over the fact that some foreign manufacturer
will send us goods for the money ! Our black
brother Bill knocks over the rabbit with his
stick and trades off the skin for tobacco. We are
the black brother Bill among the nations,
knocking over rabbits, cutting off the sheep’s
wool and crowding it into a bag without washing
it, rooting up the minerals and not even smelting
them, leaving all the jobs that keep highly
civilised nations employed for other nations to do,
content ourselves with merely banging the rodent
and rooting stones out of the ground. These are the
victories of Freetrade, and the Herald rejoices
over them, and is devoutly thankful that we can
keep “the British manufacturer” so well em-
ployed. As for the Australian manufacturer and
the Australian artisan—isn’t there the swift
rabbit to pursue, and the stupid sheep to shear,
and the tree to chop down, and the stone to dig
up, and what more can they want ?
A Little Far-off Revolution.
A certain interest attaches in a small way to the
fact that Norway has sent a post-card to Sweden,
explaining that it has set up for itself, that it has
no further use for the present monarch, who can,
therefore, have a rest, and that the joint kingdom
of Sweden and Norway has gone out of business.
Norway, which used to be the stormy petrel of
Europe, has been a very quiet place in recent cen-
turies. It burst its boiler with over-exertion
when very young, A thousand years or so ago
its people, in addition to running a pretty regular
civil war, raged with their long ships and their
long swords all round the Atlantic and beyond it.
They owned the Orkneys and Shetlands, the
Hebrides, Iceland, and the Isle of Man. They
pervaded part of the coast of North America, and
argued with the natives of those parts, and they
howled up and down the Mediterranean. For 200
years they were the maddest and drunkest fighters
on earth, and stole nearly everything that wasn’t
nailed down, and burned nearly everything that
wasn’t wet, from Greenland to Constantinople.
Then their star pegged out. The last Norse king
who invaded England— Harold “ Hard-Coun-
sel”—was defeated and killed at Stamford in
1066. Magnus “ Barefoot,” the last one who
invaded Ireland, left his bones and his large bare
feet there among the native Home Rulers in 1103.
Haco tried to annex Scotland later on, and died in
the course of his retreat after the disastrous battle
of Largs; and then Norway settled down to fishing,
and petty civil wars, and the growth of large snow-
balls. After a while it grew too tired for civil
war and only fished. The Danes got possession of
the country about 1380, through a confused series of
royal marriages, and ran things in a high-handed
way till 1814, when Europe was cleaning up after
the” Napoleonic wars, and then Norway was in-
formed that it was to become Swedish property. It
kicked a little, but it wasn’t the kick of Harold
“Hard-Counsel,” nor even of Magnus “Bare-
foot” and the transfer came off. Still things drifted
along easily. Norway had its own Parliament
and universal suffrage. It had its own tariff. It
didn’t even allow the king to veto its laws. It
abolished titles of nobility. It had its own army,
and its own navy of four ironclads and a raft and
some sundries, and it had its own Ibsen. But
the aristocratic Swede had a superior habit of
patting Norway on the back, and telling it to be
good and go to bed early, and somehow the union
was never popular. There was hardly a public-
house in any seaport on earth where one flaxen-
haired sailor could casually mention “Sweden and
Norway ” without another flaxen-haired seaman
yelling “Norway and Sweden,” and over that
question pewter beer-pots have been wrecked in
Valparaiso and Colon and New York and Odessa
and even up the Persian Gulf. Now, quite
suddenly, King Oscar has been dismissed without
even a week’s salary in advance, and if Sweden
doesn’t think the matter worth fighting about the
old arrangement is off. Norway starts business
as a kingdom 1160 miles long and in some places
only 20 miles wide, and with a tail at the north,
which tail sticks into Russia in an unaccountable
fashion. Also, it has 2j; million people, and
Kaiser William of Germany is understood to
have his eye on it. But then there are few things
on earth that Kaiser William hasn’t his eye upon,
and few people to whom he hasn’t sent a telegram.
Still Another Land Bill.
The newest scheme of N.S.W. Lands Minister
Ashton for “coddling” and assisting the farmer
is a rather curious affair. It is a mixture of a
Closer Settlement scheme and a State Land Bank
device, and the Minister describes it thus :
Take an example such as this : A large estate is owned
by A, who desires to subdivide it. He finds a sufficient
number of farmers, who are ready to purchase the estate
from him in small areas. A provisional agreement is
entered into between the vendor and the purchasers, and
a plan of subdivision is submitted to the Government
authority, whom, for the present, we may call the Com-
missioners. The owner asks, we will say, £3 an acre for
the land. The purchasers are in a position to find, say,
one-third of the purchase-money. The Commissioners,
provided they approve of the valuation submitted to
them, agree to advance the other two-thirds of the pur-
chase money. The vendor falls out of the transaction,
being paid one-third by the private purchasers and two-
thirds by the Commissioners, and the Government there-
after stands to the new settlers in the relation of mort-
gagee.
Then being asked how it was proposed to prevent
land thus cut up from aggregating into large
estates again, Minister Ashton explained :
That could be effectually guarded against, I think, by
giving the Commissioners power to approve or disapprove
of transfers of the equity of redemption, and power to
regulate the area which might be acquired by any indivi-
dual person by fixing a maximum to the advance that
might be made to any one settler.
There is something curiously futile about all this
business, but in that respect it is of a piece with
the general half-heartedness, unfinishedness and
compromise of the Carruthers Ministry. On at
least three points it appears fairly justifiable to
call the new—or allegedly new—lands device a
very poor apology for the thing that is really re-
quired :
(1) It puts no compulsion on the owner of the large
estate which blocks settlement. He isn’t directly com-
pelled to sell by a resumption process, nor indirectly com-
pelled by a graduated land-tax. Ever since big estates
began to block settlement, the owners have been abso-
lutely at liberty to sell if they wanted to. The Car-
ruthers Closer Settlement Act also allows them to sell if
they want to. The whole difficulty has lain in them not
wanting to.
(2) If the big owner wants to sell (which he generally
doesn’t), and the small man wants to buy, and the price
is satisfactory to both of them and to the Government
valuer, the State is to advance two-thirds of the money.
This is set forth as if it were a bright, fresh device, the
glitter of which would shine like a new pannikin by
moonlight. Yet Maoriland, Victoria, and S.A. have State
Land Banks which do just this kind of business ; and
N.S. \V. is supposed to have, somewhere or other, the
ruins or vestiges of a somewhat similar institution,
though it is a concern about which it is practically
impossible to get any information of value. Also the
Savings Bank does the same kind of work. Minister
Ashton’s proposal is, therefore, something like this :
N.S.W. has one State Land Bank, which seems to be
about dead. The Carruthers Government promises to
start a second, before the first one is cleaned up, by amal-
gamation of the Savings Banks. And seemingly the
Lands Department is going to found a third.
(3) Finally, the new proposal doesn’t seem to be any
definite Closer Settlement scheme at all. At least, there
is nothing said about any guarantee that the man who
buys, with the aid of the State, say 300 acres of a
large property that is being broken up, won’t also be, at
the same time, the owner of large areas elsewhere. The
new system may limit the amount it will lend to one man,
but that won’t prevent him being already an extensive
landow'ner, who is simply grabbing with the State’s
assistance a few' extra acres. The new system may also put
some kind of restriction on transfers while the mortgage
lasts, but again it may not; the matter is apparently to
be left to the discretion of the Commissioners. Certainly
it can do nothing to prevent the-re-aggregation of large
estates as soon as the mortgages are paid off. When they
are paid off the control of the Commissioners comes to an
end.
In short, until there is a heavy graduated land-
tax to make the holding or acquiring of large
estates unprofitable, all Closer Settlement schemes
are likely to be little more than sham and orna-
ment. They may have a temporary effect, but
nothing more. And all schemes for breaking up
the great estates that block settlement in the
coastal districts, so long as these schemes start
with the proviso that the owner may sell if he
feels so inclined, amount to very little. The
owner has always been at liberty to sell if he felt
inclined. He hasn’t sold because he didn’t feel
inclined. And there is only one reason why
State interference on the voluntary principle
should fill him with a voluntary inclination to sell
which he otherwise wouldn’t feel—that he hopes
to get more from the State than the land is worth.
If the State is willing to allow itself to be swin-
dled, then it may obviously, on the Ashton and
Carruthers principle, do a good deal of Closer
Settlement, though what it does will probably
be undone in future years. The State covered
Monaro years ago with small settlers who are gone
now almost to the last man—the squatting octopus
having devoured them.

The N.S.W. Arbitration Act.
The S. Australian election results should add some
weight to the protest of the N.S.W. Labor party
against the strangling of th 3 Arbitration Act.
Presuming any regard on Premier Carruthers’
part for the duties and responsibilities of his posi-
tion no such adventitious aid would be required ;
but, as his attitude is always moulded by oppor-
tunism. the reinforcement will not be needless.
It is not much of an argument with Carruthers
that an action is improper ; but if he can be con-
vinced that it is dangerous, he is at once influenced.
The S. Australia results show that there is
no virtue in Reid’s clowning to stem the tide
of Progress. The newly-elected leader and latest
pride of the Tories recently went to S. Australia to
preach the evangel of “ anti-Soeialism.” The
Tory leaders and newspapers there accepted his
lead and gave to the local Labor party the bogey
name of “Socialist,” though Price is probably
the most moderate and the least Socialistic of all
the Labor leaders. The issue was thus made one
of Socialism, as represented by the Labor party,
versus anti-Socialism, as represented by the hotch-
Eotch of Tories, Tory-Liberals, Liberal-Tories,
liberal - Conservatives, Progressive - Backsliders,
Yes-Noes, and politicians who walk crab-fashion.
And what the Tories defined as Socialism
scored most emphatically. It is the first electoral
judgment on Reid’s anti-Socialism campaign, and
it is so convincing as to make justifiable a forecast
of his utter defeat at any Federal general election.
It is, further, another proof that in State politics
Labor is a constantly advancing force. With
all its mistakes and its failures to grapple
obvious opportunities, the Labor party, as
compared with the other old parties, shines
brightly, and the electors see and follow
the light. Another election in N.S. Wales,
according to the hint given by S. Australia, would
put the Labor party into power, and in the present
Parliament that party has sufficient strength to
force an election almost whenever it chooses. So
there is very good reason for Carruthers to re-
consider his determination to destroy, by under-
hand means, the Arbitration Act. He must see
that it will be cheaper for him to part company
with his John Stinson following than to utterly
antagonise the Labor party. By falling in with
Reid’s anti-Socialism humbug, he is making his
own destruction sure, whilst, even if he lent his
shred of assistance to wipe Reid out of Federal
politics, a Eederally-unemployed Reid, who badly
wanted to recover his old State job, would be,
though a source of danger, not exactly certain ex-
tinction. Carruthers’ political success so far has
depended upon his capacity for seeing a hole—the
hole to get into and the hole to get out of. Very
little perception will show him now that in bowing
to the John Stinson ukase to smother the
Arbitration Act he has got into the wrong hole.
Even the Daily Telegraph tells him that.

An Unnecessary Adjective.
That “imported ” and “ shoddy ” are practically
the same adjective, is the lesson which almost
every witness before the Tariff Commission im-
presses. At last Wednesday week’s sitting:—
Mr. Robert Vicars, manufacturer, sworn : The Aus-
tralian wool blanket practically supplanted the other
blankets, except those of Maoriland, in the higher quali-
ties. The lower-grade blankets are imported. . . . The
local trade in genuine tweeds had been “smothered” by
the importation of shoddy cloth. . . . The history of the
decline of the local article began, he believed, when sam-
ples of Australian tweed were sent to England for the
special purpose of being copied in cheaper imitations.
We had to come down in price to compete with this
article, and -we have been down ever since.
John Blackman, manufacturer’s agent, sworn: The
great trouble was that when an article was produced
locally, patterns were forwarded immediately to English
buyers, and copies came out—very good imitations these
were, but they had not the wearing qualities of the stuff
made here. . . . When his mills took up the manufacture
of flannels the quality of the article produced was so good
that one warehouse firm offered to take the supply for
three years ; but patterns of the flannels were sent to
England and to Victoria, with the result that imitations
appeared here, and his mills had to abandon the trade.
He was a Freetrader himself, but under Freetrade the
mills did poorly, and were gradually closing up.
That is from the evidence of one day, and is typi-
cal of the evidence of many days. The story is
always the same. The local manufacturer can
produce a good honest article at a fair price, does
so, and is smothered by a flood of shoddy imitations
which a defective tariff allows in to compete
against him. “Imported ” means “shoddy ” and
“shoddy” means “imported.” Since the man
who makes only one adjective to grow where two
flourished before is a benefactor to his language,
The Bulletin suggests dropping “shoddy” as a
word. “ Imported ” carries its whole meaning.
