England Ltd.
Stonehenge, where the pre-historic Briton used
to worship the sun on the occasions of its rare
visits to his island, has, by judicial decision, been
closed to the British public. Stonehenge is part
of the nation, one of the few certain monuments
of the people who gave the British Isles their
name, and who showed themselves such sturdy
fighters against the Roman arms and such apt
pupils of the Roman civilisation. But the owner
of the ground on which it stands can barbed-wire-
fence it and charge the Britisher a pound a head
to gaze upon the altar of his race tradition. The
decision is a notable landmark in the descent
of England towards the ultimate ideal of Private
Property, when Commerce will be the one God,
and the British infant will be charged so much for
the privilege of beingborn in his own country—pay-
ment strictly in advance. Centuries ago, Shak-
speare put into the mouth of John o’ Gaunt, the
scorn :
—England, bound in by the triumphant seas,
Whose rocky shores beat back the envious siege of watery
Neptune,
Is now hound in with shame with inky blots and rotten
parchment bonds.
Whatever truth there was in the invective then,
there is much now. Britain for many years
has set its face away from idealism and towards
commercialism. On the altar of Cobden it
sacrificed the yeomanry, who “once had been
their country’s pride,” and gave over its memoried
acres to be the playgrounds of American million-
aires and Jewish plutocrats. Scotland now, for
miles and miles, is wire-fenced and stone-walled to
serve the whimful pleasure of the foreign rich.
That they may shoot, the Scotch peasants had to
emigrate. Historic England falls quickly to the
same fate, and the date when Westminster
Abbey will be put up for auction in New York or
Tokio might almost be fixed. The declaration
which makes Stonehenge private property is not
an isolated fact; it is one of many, all pointing to
the same moral, that the Britisher is losing his
pride of race and country, that in the chase for a
concession in Asia or a gold mine in Africa he has
lost the faculty to think of his own hearth. Soon
he will be leading “an hotel life” among the
nations.
A. L. Harrold, formerly head of the one-time
great firm of Harrold Bros., which came to a
smash that dragged him out of the S. Aus.
Assembly and landed him somewhere else, has
gone to England to push on a scheme in connec-
tion with the overland-railway land-grab. The
Jenkins-Butler crowd hasn’t missed many
chances lately of giving the northern elephant a
push along; it has been scattering literature
wholesale (pro-yellow literature, much of it), and
Jenkins, before he crept into the nice, dry office
of Agent-General, dotted most of his speeches
with remarks upon the necessity of mongrelising
Australia. Now “Geology” Brown is up there
to report on another branch of the Territory’s
“ great natural resources.” What Brown has to say
on the labor question isn’t likely, though, to help
on the treachery very much. When the yellow
man has worked the N.T. mines “on his own”
he has simply rooted out the easily-worked gold,
and made it almost impossible for anybody else to
go in after him; and when he has been an
employee for English or Australian co.’s the end
has always been disaster. It is a hard fact for the
cheap yellow-labor crowd to think over that the one
part of Australia where mining has been a dismal
failure is also the one part where abundance of
tbis cheap yellow labor has been available all the
time. It is also a hard fact that, with a far
greater proportion of Asiatics than any other
part—the proportion runs to four Asiatics to one
European—the N.T. is the one part that has made
no progress whatever in any direction. Spruce
Myth and the rest of them, who tell Australia
that the way to get jolly and fat is to let in the
Asiatic or any other colored person, ought to be
invited to explain how it is that the N.T., with its
four Asiatics to every one European, and with its
alleged enormous natural resources to work on,
has heen simply a drag on the State that has to
carry it —has a huge deficit —can’t even collect
enough revenue to pay interest—and hasn’t one-
third the white population that it used to have
before the flood of colored laborers arrived.
So far the statements laid before the N.S.W.
Lands Inquiry Commission regarding Nicholas
Willis’ brother-in-law may be condensed as fol-
lows : —He is, or was, a letter-carrier at Granville—
just a plain, mild, harmless letter-carrier. There
were a number of applications for the lease of
certain lands, and the tender of the letter-carrier
was the lowest. The Board recommended that
the highest tender should be accepted. Instead,
Willis’ brother-in-law, the letter-carrier, was in-
formed of the amount of the highest tender, and
told that if he liked to raise his offer to that figure
he could have the land. This was said to be because
he was the “original applicant, ’’ which apparently
he wasn't. The letter-carrier raised his offer, and
got the land. About four months afterwards
the letter-carrier applied for a reduction
of rent, and got it; his rent was reduced
from an average of about per acre to 41d.
Consequently the increased offer, whereby the
applicant got the land, was hardly any sooner
increased than it was reduced again. The letter-
carrier, it is said, never even saw the land, and
never went near it. It seems to have been
promptly mortgaged to the Willis family, which
foreclosed on it. The commission called the letter-
carrier as a witness last week, but he didn’t turn
up. As the Sydney dailies enthusiastically put
it : “We must settle the people on the land,” and
certainly it appears that something has been done
that way. The success achieved in settling
Nicholas Willis on the land seems to have been
almost phenomenal.
Queensland Treasurer Kidston is accused of
fathering the agitation to form a new Labor party
with a “moderate democratic” platform. The
trouble has arisen over the Crown land sales
which the Morgan Government is persisting in.
Undoubtedly there is a split, a new central
organisation having been formed, in addition
to several provincial ones. If the scheme goes
on to successful accomplishment, it is calculated
that the members of the present Labor party
likely to go over will give Morgan a majority
of about eight in the event of a split. The more
radical Labor members complain bitterly that a
g reference to unionists clause is not contained in the
lompulsory Arbitration Bill to be introduced next
session. The Bulletin would be sorry to see a
division which would send a most valuable section
of the Queensland Labor party into a camp where
Liberalism would be very much watered. A
“ Labor” party believing in the further alienation
of the people’s land and in an emasculated Arbi-
tration Act would be a very curious Labor party
indeed.
The Vanguard.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
While the crippled cruisers stagger where the
blind horizon dips,
And the ocean ooze is rising round the sunken
battleships,
While the battered wrecks, unnoticed, with their
mangled crews, drift past—
Let me fire one gun for Russia, though that gun
should be the last.
’Tis a struggle of the Ages, and the White Man’s
star is dim,
There is little jubilation, for the game has got too
grim ;
But though Russia’s hope seems shattered, and
the Russian star seems set,
It may mean the Dawn for Russia —and my hope’s
in Ivan yet!
Let the Jingo, in his blindness, cant and cackle
as he will;
But across the path of Asia run the Russian
trenches still !
And the sahib in his rickshaw may 101 l back and
smoke at ease,
While the haggard, ragged heroes man the battered
batteries.
’Tis the first round of the struggle of the East
against the West,
Of the fearful war of races—for the White Man
could not rest.
Hold them, Ivan ! staggering bravely underneath
your gloomy sky;
Hold them, Ivan ! we shall want you pretty badly
by-and-bye !
Fighting for the Indian empire, when the British
pay their debt;
Never Briton watched for Blucher as he’ll watch
for Ivan yet !
It means all to young Australia—it means life or
death to us,
For the vanguard of the White Man is the van-
guard of the Russ!
N.s.w.
HENRY LAWSON.

♦
Two years ago, when Mr. Edward Brans-
eombe’s Westminster Glee Party first showed
the quality of the voices that echo round the
bones in the great English charnel-house,
there were four men and four boys; now there
are six of one and seven of the other. But the
rare qualities of pure enunciation and true intona-
tion and nice balance have not been sacrificed to
volume. There isn’t anything particularly
striking in the way of solo-singing ; and there is no
bright and not very particular star who is deter-
mined to show the size and beauty of the bell in
his own throat or crack it in the attempt. General
Effect is aimed at; and the old warrior is
hit every time. Saturday night’s audience
at Sydney Town Hall was obviously a
congregation. It was even expected to
bring a thipny-bit to put on the plate
and get a programme in return. Most of them
brought the thripny from force of habit; but the
rest who thought they were going to a concert
kicked. Everybody had his revenge, however,
by encoring every number except one. It was
the best item on the programme, but apparently
was reckoned of too sacred a character for the
worldly encore, so the programme instructed the
congregation to receive it in silence. But, by the
way, why is it that part-singing should only,
these times, appeal to the congregation ? Fluff
and feathers will turn up at a concert where 27
mostly-bad solo numbers are listed ; but if part-
singing is mentioned the same crowd either doesn’t
go at all, or leaves off its frills and puts on
the same clothes that it goes to funerals in.
And (apart from the before-mentioned thripny-
bit) there isn’t anything particularly ecclesiastic
about the Gleesters’ performance, which, on the
whole, is much more cheerful than the concert
with the eight pianoforte solos and the 15 soulful
ballads. Of the newcomers, taking them
individually, basso Bennett proves the most
popular ; and soloist Madame Hooten uses her
contralto with the same good effect as hitherto.
Dudley Causton, of the old co., contributes his
humorous musical sketches to the new co., and
gets great applause—which only shows that the
Branscombe congregation doesn’t frequent Aus-
tralian music-halls. If it did it would have seen
a dozen other performers in this same played-out
line of mimic business, and it would have been
much less enthusiastic.
#* * *
Nothing much has happened to “The
Oingalee ” this week except that it has been raised
individually and collectively to the dignity of
being carried through the post on a card for Id.
Nobody is truly great these times till Pumpkin
Smith carries him for that small sum in a post-bag ;
and the greatness has come to our brown fellow-
subject in his last week in Sydney. Meantime,
the tea-girl has been taking the moth-balls out of
her travelling clothes, and making sure that she has
enough hair-pins to see her through to Melbourne ;
and on Saturday night Mack will depose Boo-
bhambah at Her Majesty’s in Sydney, and Ireland
will reign for a short season of drama with musical
interruptions. “Tom Moore,” with which the
new season opens, is said to have been written
expressly for Andrew Mack, and The Bulletin
knows nothing to the contrary.
** * *
Miss Eugenie Duggan must find this world
in very truth a vale of tears. Next Saturday night
she will enact, at Sydney Palace, the part of that
dampest heroine, Lady Isabel-Madame Vine, in
“East Lynne.” But up to next Friday night all
those who love melodrama at its drammiest will
have opportunities to thrill over “ Two Little
Drummer Boys.”
*# * *
An American humorist, Frank Pixley by
name (we don’t know him, but that’s no reason
why he isn’t a large buck-wheat cake in his own
country), wrote the libretto of “King Dodo,”
produced for the first time in Australia on Satur-
day night, at Sydney Criterion. Pixley may
have got a suggestion of his theme from a set of
verses called “Ponce de Leon,” which may be
found in most “ books of recitation for the
young.” In the poem a haughty Spaniard, who
had gone seafaring with Columbus in that eminent
navigator’s ferry-boat, is told by an Indian
slave of the wondrous land where the streets are
beaten gold, and where all the Beautiful Works
of Nature are more beautiful, having been greatly
improved upon since the time when Baby Genesis
was cutting his first tooth and amusing himself with
the serpent’s tail in Mesopotamia—long before he
grew up and became the father of Exodus
and that wild youth Deuteronomy. In the midst
of fragrant flowers and singing birds with jewel-
like plumage, there bubbles a spring, and whoso
shall drink its waters shall regain his lost youth
and live for ever. Ponce sets sail and runs up
against Florida. It is quite beautiful enough for
him, but his after - fate has nothing to do
with this gospel—nor with that of “King
Dodo,” according to Pixley. The humorist
puts “Dodo” in a gorgeous Florida background,
with palaces, and the iron gates that suggest
property worth stealing on the other side.
Spoojuland is the name of this phantom country,
and it is ruled by Queen Lilli, the most Gibson-
esque of all beautiful Amazons. She is white,
and dressed in superb, up-to-date raiment; but
her army is black, and its tailoring doesn’t cost
anything. To this fair land come King Dodo (the
very antithesis of the Spaniard) and his court to
hunt for the magic spring. No man knoweth to
what age, race or country the potentate belongs.
He wears, in crimson brocade, the stuffed
trunks and hose of Elizabethan days, and
a crown no bigger than a wart. The Dodo
coat of arms looks like a crazed ornitho-
rhynchus or a cow with a beak. Dodo makes
allusions to Australian burning questions and
wants to invest another tuppence in Tattersall’s.
He is an amusing, doddering little old king—a
buffoon even when he’s gloomily wondering how
he can get back his lost youth. To give him as
husband to the beautiful Amazonian monarch was
a piece of mad extravaganza on the part of the
Pixley person, but then “ King Dodo” may come
under the index of musical burlesque, and that is
irresponsible lunacy trimmed with song and night-
mare. Miss Maud Amber plays Lilli, the Queen
of Spoojuland, and the composers have given
her some attractive songs in one of their lucid in-
tervals. “ The Moon and the Star ” was composed
in quite a sane interval. Mr. Sheridan, of Widow
O’Brien fame, makes a diverting, old, silly-faced
King Dodo, and Mr. Winfield Blake, the only
heroic male character in the piece, is Piola, Cap-
tain of the King’s guard. He has some good
musical opportunities. The co. had only a fort-
night to rehearse the piece, so first night’s perform-
ance was rather crude in the matter of chorus and
minor details.
-# # # *
At Sydney Royal, “ Pygmalion and Gala-
tea ” was to have been produced on Wednesday
night, after this paper had gone to press to cope
with the demands of an enormous circulation. That
dainty statue story is only billed to survive
until Saturday, when “Monsieur Beaucaire”
will be staged with Mr. Maud Osborne in the
title role. There is no reasonable ground for sus-
picion that the gentleman in question is a
star actor, but he may be, and so as to
be on the side of safety the management is
keeping the fact of his appearance in a favored
beauty rdle from the convalescent Julius Knight.
Mr. Knight is getting better, but it is not advis-
able to take risks with his health as yet. Whether
Mr. Osborne’s Beaucaire is to be taken as a joke
or not, its first night on Saturday will be a great
social event, with Lord and Lady Northcote
present.
#* # *
G. F. Golden’s yarns continue to be the
bright spot on an otherwise rather drab pro-
gramme at the Tivoli. Also, his mimicry creates
a more favorable impression as he goes along.
He has a theory that you can always tell what an
actor did for a crust before he went crazy and
took to the stage for a living, and develops it by
explaining how ex-pug. Jeffries makes love (you
can almost see the uppercut coming), and how
various other people tell the heroine that they’ll
protect her to their last gasp. It’s a novel show,
and worth the money. Mdlle. Guerra continues
dancing, and when you have seen her dance you
wonder why she sings ; M'Ginty continues to be
rude to J. W. Winton ; and other things mostly
continue as they were this time last week.
*# * #
It were hardly correct to say that “Bill
Adams” was produced at Melbourne Princess’s
last Saturday. “Bill” was produced at Sydney
Criterion earlier in the month, and then he moved
to Melbourne for a change of domicile. He belongs
to the very restless order of musical creations, does
Adams. A fortnight ago this journal remarked
in other words, that a critic who explored Wil-
liam’s bumps with a view to discovering an organ
of common sense would draw a blank. Also he
would be flying in the face of the advt., where the
word “extravaganza” is expected to make its
meaning clear. “Bill Adams” has no common
sense, but he is full of song and dance, and rapid
conversation, the dialogue consisting mainly
of strange questions and irrelevant replies.
A part-author of the “ book” —if Bill can be said
to have a book—is described on the programme as
Bacchus, and doubtless Bacchus was Bacchus when
he went to work. The result is brighter than
many a comparatively sober musical comedy.
George Stephenson’s show avoids the paths that
lead to unfavorable comparison as regards the
numerousness of the company and the weight of
the spectacle. One can laugh at the absurdities
on their merits without mistaking them for bril-
liant flashes of humor. The fun, as a rule, looks
rather cheap, but perhaps it would appear more
valuable if it were not plentiful—like fruit out of
season.
* * * *
The co. has taken in some new members
—Miss Roland Watts-Phillips and Lissant—since
the previous Melbourne season, whilst the old
members have been sharpening their familiar
methods and, perhaps, profiting by experience.
Miss May Beatty has even sharpened her figure.
Instead of the bouncing breadthsome charmer of
yore, we find a quite slim young May, who has
lost the bulk of her singing voice, but is, in other
respects, improved by modification. Edward
Lauri is as quick and lively as ever, but
a little less aggressive, and M'Naughfcon is
original, and sufficiently a mimic of George
Huntley to make a good deal of a good part.
The Bovis Bros., Miss Garstang, Miss Nixon,
and the chorus, are equal to the occasion, and
the fact that the Stephenson Co. can dance in
all directions is put beyond dispute. There is
dance movement —more than enough—in the
frivolous music. A dance in the first act intro-
duces an effect of electric lights which is remark-
able for being artistic. As a rule, the lights that
gleam from the dresses or headgear of ballet
nymphs are the merest freaks of decoration.
* * *
Hugo Heermann, the violinist who is said
to be adored in Berlin and belauded in every
other large musical city of the world beyond Aus-
tralia, will give his firsc concert at Melbourne
Town Hall next Saturday afternoon (17th), and
his second concert on the following Tuesday
night. The matinee event is Beethoven’s con-
certo, for which a full orchestra is in active train-
ing under W. A. Laver’s direction.
“ Sporting Life,” at Melbourne Royal, is
one of the few Blandoltian melodramas which pro-
claim their meaning in their titles. The first scene
is the spacious yard of Lord Walter Baker’s train-
ing stables j the second act closes with an auc-
tioneer in the background, preparing to knock
down his lordship’s crocks to the highest bidders.
The third act brings professional pugs, and
wrestlers into play, and the feast of sport culmin-
ates in a Derby race, whereby the busted Bak. r is
restored to affluence and happiness. Like all other
stories of betting transactions, this reckless narra-
tive takes no trouble to explain how a bank-
rupt owner is able to back the first favorite
to win a fortune, considering that never an Ikey
in the ring would book a wager with him. But
Baker works the old miracle once again. Fortunes
come easy to punters on Bland Holt’s racecourses,
where the bad men have unlimited money to lose,
and the virtuous men have no means of paying
their debts if they don’t win. It happens, for a
change, that the jew money-lender in “Sporting
Life” is not a bad man as far as the audience
know. He wears a wonderful light overcoat and
performs little deeds of charity in public. Pos-
sibly he bleeds the widow and the fatherless
behind the scenes, but his victims hold, their
tongues, anyhow. Discreet silence is commonly
practised in “ Sporting Life.” The girl who was
betrayed doesn’t go around and talk about
her troubles, neither does the Law shriek for ven-
geance when young man Maxwell throttles Miss
Harrie Ireland to death in a sitting-room at the
Hotel Cecil. Darkness falls upon the corpse, and
nothing further is heard about the melancholy
circumstance. If the Bland Holt co. had been
making its first appearance in Australia last
Saturday night, The Bulletin would utter here-
with a number of admiring comments instead of
leaving them unsaid. It is only necessary to men-
tion that the co. does itself justice. The members
run up to their usual form, and they carry the
confidence of the public. In short, they are all
there. Bland Holt, as the benevolent usurer, is
so favorably placed from the start that he comes
in an easy winner by a long nose; whilst Miss
Jennie Pollock, who rarely gets a sorrow-stricken
part to play, reveals an unsuspected aptitude for
putting tenderness into her voice.
* * * -#
At Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), this
(Thursday) is the last night of Andrew Mack,
who has kept the green lamp of Irish drama
burning brightly for nine weeks. The warbling
comedian farewells in “The Way to Kenmare,”
and nothing less than an earthquake can prevent
the theatre being crowded when he breathes his
soft adieu. On Saturday the business in hand
will be the re-appearance of Miss Nance O’Neil,
supported by her own co. Melbourne remembers
the tall lady as an actress with an impressive
personality and a fine raking stride. Magda was
Miss O’Neil’s inaugural heroine four year ago ;
and as Magda, the stormy, impenitent young
woman with a Past, she will appear again on
Saturday night.
* * *
Those well-coached amateurs, the Players,
drew a full house to Melbourne Princess’s last
Friday, when they repeated their intelligent, un-
stagey treatment of H. A. Jones’ “Physician.”
The representation, of course, lacked the polish of
experience. Such details as exits and entrances,
as dealt with by novices, are generally mere crude
acts of necessity. In the hands of artists, they
expand into subtle manifestations of perfect train-
ing. An exit, for instance, may be Art, or it may
be j ust so much meat and clothes getting through
an opening regardless of consequences. Much,
however, may be forgiven to amateurs like the
Players, who dare to be quiet and natural, instead
of feverishly reproductive of the by-play and
mannerisms of last week’s “star.” Lawrence
Hardinge was manly and impressive, if a shade
unemotional, as the Physician ; D. M. Beere,
as the alcoholic maniac, and J. R. Castling, as
the charming old parson, were neither of them
unworthy of the fine parts allotted to them.
Among the ladies, Miss Georgina O’Meara, as
the heroine, showed distinct improvement on her
previous stage form. Miss Katherine Hesper
was bright, and Miss Grace Millar achieved a
cheerful picture of a comely village charmer.
Most of the minor characters covered themselves
and director Gregan M‘Mahon with credit.
* * *
“ 0.K.” throws a well-deserved brick :
The saddest shilling’s worth of entertainment I have
ever struck was a “gymkhana” perpetrated at the Mel-
bourne Exhibition Oval last week. It was a bank holiday
show. The unsuspecting public had been loudly exhorted
to roll up, and the grand stand reserve was a shilling
extra. I remember with horror and indignation that I
nearly paid a second * bob ’ into the gymkhana fund.
True, it was for an excellent charity—the St. Vincent’s
Hospital. But I had gone to the bleak show-place for
entertainment —bright, heart-stirring entertainment, not
for cold charitable purposes. As far as the hospital is
concerned I wish the gymkhana had charged me two
shillings to stop away, and had kept me out by main force
after taking the money. The star item at this alfresco
matinee was a “ musical ride.” Several pairs of nice red-
coated gentlemen and white-robed ladies went through a
mild series of evolutions on horseback, whilst the band
played. They processed in single file, or two abreast,
or three abreast; then back to single file again, and so on
for about 20 minutes. The next item was a puerile display
of jumping over hurdles by two of the nice gentlemen,
one of whom didn’t ride verj' charmingly. Later on the
gentlemen and ladies jumped the hurdles in straggling
couples at a slow pace. And oh ! the “ low-comedy ” dis-
play of “ ridiculous turn-outs.” I think it was the com-
petition of comic vehicles and funny occupants that drove
me from the scene marvelling at the audacity of such
circus performers. A gymkhana, seemingly, is a society
circus, and judged by the fair comparison of an amateur
theatrical performance, or a non-professional nigger
minstrel entertainment, it is undoubtedly Wicked. Put
into Fitzgeralds’ Circus building or Wirth’s tent it would
look less worth a shilling than an amateur theatrical show
is worth a pound. The faces of the assembled multitude
bore witness to this truth. I had never before seen so
many sad and weary people mourning over a wasted bank
holiday, and wondering what they had done to deserve a
gymkhana.

The Brough-Flemming Co. will have opened
in “ Beauty and the Barge,” at Adelaide Royal,
before this issue sees the light, which is the best
dramatic news that Tortoise Town has heard for a
long time. Meanwhile, W. Anderson has been
profitably filling in a long gap with melodrama,

sharp and their horrors thick and slab. The
Avalon-Collard five have played a short return
season at the Tivoli, with a new programme that
was as gentlemanly and ladylike (and also as
bright and clever) as the old one.
The Wild Cat Column.
The Butter Commission, after a full knowledge
■of the agents’ tricks, recommends legislation which will
allow the suppliers of any private factory to take over the
concern on a co-operative basis. This proposal is de-
nounced by the Argus as “ suggested confiscation,”
although provision is made, of course, for payment in full
for the business. But the Argus omits to mention the
“ confiscation ” practised on the farmers’ butter by the
agents, prior to the Commission. A second recommenda-
tion is, that suppliers shall be compelled to send their
milk to their own district factories. On this point a
lamenting agent says ingenuously, “If any factory
cannot compete and pay the same price as other
factories, it must be against the interests of producers
to supply their milk or cream to such factory.”
But one of the most successful tricks of the Melbourne
agents, whose palatial “ butter factories” are situated in
the city, was to send round touts to those districts where
co-operative factories were struggling for a foothold, and
offer prices for cream which were beyond the power of
the local factories to pay—if they wanted to remain sol-
vent. As the Commission drily observes, however, “this
was not philanthropy.” The undisguised aim of the
Melbourne sharks was to break up the co-operative fac-
tories ; then they were able, afterwards, to buy the cream
on their own terms. By this means several excellent
factories, particularly in the Gippsland and north-eastern
districts, were ruined, w’hile the co-operative movement
received a severe set-back. If each dairyman were, as the
Commission proposes, compelled to supply his own fac-
tory, the co-operative principle would receive a much-
needed help, while in the end the dairymen would really
be the gainers.

Of the profit made in 1904-5, £lOOO was handed over as a
contribution to the Town Fund; to that extent the
undertaking aided the general revenues of the city, and
thus reduced ordinary rates on property. This contribu-
tion is defended, or explained, by the electric supply
committee in this way:—“ Inasmuch as the elec-
tric supply undertaking is directly chargeable upon the
Town Fund, and the responsibility of any deficiency would
have to be borne by the citizens, it is the usual practice,
when funds will permit thereof, for a portion of the surplus
revenue to be appropriated for grants to the Town Fund.”
Whose “usual practice”? If the electric supply com-
mittee wants to guard the Town Fund against having to
make good a deficiency, its best plan would seem to be to
build up its owm reserves and reduce its loan liabilities.
To make the city some recompense for taking a risk when
that risk can be abolished is very like leaving a hole in a
boat and paying a man to pump w'hen the man’s wages
would more than pay for stopping the hole. If the £lOOO
were put to reserves, it would be there when it was
wanted ; if it is handed over to the Town Fund, it will be
used as revenue, and won’t be available anymore.
In the foregoing the amount available for sink-
ing fund and other reserves is shown ; hereunder is shown
the total capital expenditure year by year and the amount
required as sinking fund (1 per cent, sinking fund proper,
2 per cent, depreciation and renewal reserve): —

Operations began in 1894, but complete figures for the
early years are not available. Down to 1889 the sinking-
fund charges were 2Jper cent. ; after that 3 per cent.; and
on that basis £16,185 was called for down to Feb., 1900,
instead of which, as shown above, £22,332 was accumu-
lated. Down to Feb., 1905, £59,076 was called for, and
£73,784 had been accumulated. This is satisfactory ;it
means that, after paying full interest, very nearly 4 per
cent, was earned, and was available for sinking fund and
other reserves. On the average capital invested
last year the earnings (including, of course, interest
on sinking fund, &e.) amounted to £lO 7s. 3d. per cent.
In the previous year it was £9 4s. lOd. Meantime, also,
reductions have been made in the charges for light and
power; these have, however, been more than balanced by
a reduction in costs. The average charge per unit sold in
1903-4 was 2’410d. ; last year 2’094d. Last year’s progress
was most marked in connection with power-supply ; the
increase was from 351,472 units to 621,829 units. Part of
this increase was due to demand for current for elevators ;
the number of machines driven increased from 26 to 39.
These figures are not large ; but the length of the jump
can’t be too pleasant a subject for the hydraulic people to
think about.

# » * *
As expected, the trouble in connection with
the Equitable Life Assurance Co. of. New York is mainly
with regard to investments. It is alleged, and not
denied, that a syndicate, including some of the directors,
has acted a 3 middleman, buying stock and passing it on
to the co.; and a refund of the commissions thus drawn is
talked of. The co. has a huge surplus of £16,582,672 over
and above the capital (£20,550, which is restricted to a
7 p.c. div.) and assurance and annuity fund (£67,943,052);
and the handling of this surplus is also a cause of much
trouble. But, as might be judged from the size of the
surplus, the Investigation Committee has reported that,
so far as policy-holders are concerned, chey are most
amply covered. The whole row is largely promoted by
the agents, who find themselves handicapped when com-
peting with mutual offices; and one almost certain
development will be the organisation of a new and
vigorous office.
• • • •
The season just closed has been disastrous for
Che “ shrewd business man ” who generally makes a heavy
profit by selling other persons’ wheat in England. The
number of wheat-laden ships lost daring the past year
has been a record ; while of those which reached port, a
large percentage were forced to jettison a portion of their
cargo. Several Sydney brokers are reported to be either
broke or in difficulties. On so large a scale has disaster
overtaken the wheat ship of late, that it is plainly essen-
tial, when the Navigation Bill gets before Parliament, to
review the existing State legislation as to the load-line on
grain carriers. Grain has long been recognised as the
most dangerous of all cargo, except possibly coal, and the
present State regulations, especially for ships arriving in
Europe during the winter season, seem quite inadequate.

Another bank which shows a slight slackening-off of
profits. Still, considering the figures, profits are better
than might have been expected. At May, 1903, the bank
showed interest-bearing deposits of only £216,886 and
advances of £594,914. Now it is paying interest on
£15,322 more deposits, while the advances have shrunk
£50,224, and yet £383 more profit is shown. On present
figures the bank is earning more than it did in 1903, though,
given similar conditions, it ought to be earning less ; and
the chances are that a decline is in view. Capital is
£152,040; on this and reserves combined the earnings
have recently been (per cent, per annum) :

Nov., 1902 .. 418 7 „ May, 1905 .. 514 5 „
The return is by no means high, considering that, even
now, in spite of increased deposits and reduced advances,
•only 28 per cent, of the liabilities to the public are repre-
sented by liquid assets. But expenses are comparatively
high, though, in view of the number of its small
branches, the National of Tasmania does its work
very cheaply indeed. Take for comparison the
well-managed Colonial of Australasia. Of its gross
profits, £5O os. 7d. per cent, goes in expenses; of the
National’s, £34 18s. per cent, including income-tax. Or
while £lO6 of advances in the Tas. National cost in manage-
ment £1 per half-year, £74 in the Colonial cost £l. It
•doesn’t, therefore, look as though it is through paying
princely salaries that the National Bank can’t show more
•than £5 14s. sd. per cent, on its funds, notwithstanding
That it keeps comparatively small reserves. It looks more
as though the State’s Tory legislation had blocked pro-
gress and prevented a brisk demand for money.

(the yield of sugar (147,688 tons) was the largest save the

Sydney papers have been talking about a
mysterious English co. which has taken up large shale
and oil deposits in N.S. Wales, and now is pulling the
Government’s ear for railway and other concessions.
Capital mentioned is £400,000; and various people have
expressed great joy at such an influx of British money.
But the joy is premature ; for N.S.W. Mines Department,
if it doesn’t want N.S.W. mining to smell a lot worse
in England than it does at present, ought to say first
what it thinks of the chances of a huge co. making a
“do’’out of N.S.W. shale and oil. If it thinks the co.
has wretched chances, then the occasion isn’t one for joy
at all. The deposits are said to be at Capertee, where
the N.S.W. Shale and Oil Co. has been at work for
about 30 years, present co. being a reconstruction of
older co.’s. It has had every kind of experience,
has always been on the look-out for properties,
and presumably wouldn’t touch these “vast deposits”
that J. Bull is said to have bought at a huge price, simply
because it didn’t think them good enough. In 1898 this
co. paid one poor little div. of 2J per cent., amounting to
£833; since then it has not paid a cent, though it has been
grafting hard all the time. During 1904 it certainly did
make a profit of £5391, as the result of some extra-good
contracts; but all this was absorbed in writing-down
values of machinery which had been worn out in the
weary struggle during the years when it was making
losses. And only last year, when £25,000 of 5J per cent,
debentures were falling due, co. had to agree to pay 6 per
cent, to get them renewed for four years.
Facts with regard to this mysterious English
float seem to be that the eye of J. Bull has been caught
by the richness of the comparatively small quantity of
picked stuff which has been sent away lately ; though, in
spite of its richness, the N.S.W. Shale Co.’s profit, as
shown, has been very small. Who J. Bull is in this partic-
ular case, can’t be said for certain, but he is alleged to be
mostly Newnes, the newspaperman ; and if Newnes loses
his money, as he has a first-rate chance of doing, Aus-
tralia will probably get some kind attention from the
Newnes papers, as well as the Harmsworth papers, when
next it has a loan falling due. Still Newnes can’t say that
Australia incited him to this deaL Alleged that he put a
solid £lOO,OOO into the new concern, that another £lOO,OOO
was raised otherwise, and that the promoters get £lOO,OOO
and 200,000 shares. If so, they are mighty lucky promo-
ters. Possibly the promoters reckoned that they had a
big thing to offer Bull; all the same they were either
lucky or wise in not offering it to Australia. If they had,
and had asked £400,000 for it, or £200,000, or even
£lOO,OOO, the proposal would have been received as one of
the jokes of a dull season. And it seems to be N.S. Wales'
duty to tell Newnes & Co. so at the earliest possible
moment.
N.S.W. Registrar of Friendly Societies (Statist
Coghlan) wrote his last report with gall; and gall was the
proper ink to use. Friendly societies are the poor man’s
banks and insurance offices; and the older ones were
mostly heaped together by amateurs, who reckoned that
it was quite easy to throw four three-penny bits into a
till and take out half-a-crown. J. B. Trivett, the
actuary, made a complete valuation of assets and
liabilities as at the end of 1899, and found that there
wasn’t one single solvent society in the State. Even then,
though the actuary assumed that the funds would
earn at least 3 per cent, for the future, the average
earnings for six years had been only 271 per cent.
Lumped together, the liabilities for sick and funeral
benefits amounted to £3,795,013; the assets (including
present value of all future contributions), only £2,541,182.
Liabilities thus exceeded assets by £1,253,831. The older
the societies get, the bigger the drain is on the funds.
By 1901 there were already 53 societies which were
admittedly “broke,” and oouldn’t pay the full benefits
agreed on ; while there were 184 which were living on
their scanty reserves, and rushing fast towards eventual
repudiation. One old and big society has paid £7213. out
of the central funds in 10 years, and has only received
£3902; so that the old branches have loafed to the
extent of £3311 on the branches which haven’t yet
got gout to any great extent, and haven’t so far
accumulated any large number of widows. An
Act was passed in 1899 calling on the societies to leave
off making an ass of themselves, and deluding their mem-
bers into the belief that they could get 31 quarts out of a
pint pot; but they mostly kicked, and when they didn’t
kick they dtdged, and put up the “ good local member ”
to propose amendments. One result was that societies
were given till '903 to start being honest. Thereupon
some of the societies, instead of shutting down hard upon
the admission of members on the old bankruptcy basis,
whipped up every derelict they could lay hands on, and
decorated him with millinery, and undertook to give him
4s. 9d. worth for 25., though they had nothing but a
big deficit to pay the balance of 2s. 9d. with. The
new members have now to pay 4s. 9d. ; but
through the rush of members on the two shilling
basis, even the 4s. 9d. won’t be sufficient. But
the chances are that, when the new member sees that
the money he is paying in to provide for his own
gouty days, and for his own rheumatism and his own
widow, is being collared to pay for somebody else’s gout
and rheumatism and widow, he will kick. At least he
will kick if he isn’t a fool ; though it won’t be any use
kicking unless he forms a brand new society, or insists on
the old society keeping his money separate from the
funds of the old insolvent period.

For purposes of comparison, Broken Hill co.s’
statements of costs are not worth much. Block 10 quotes
15s. lljd. per ton of ore for mining and ore-dressing ; but
total costs seem to have been about 18s. Id., and then
apparently nothing is charged for wear and tear. Sulphide
Corporation’s last figures were 16s. Id. ; but that covered
everything. South quotes lowest, 13s. 6Jd. ; that covers
mining, refilling, developing, and milling, but, it seems,
nothing else. North costs are given at 14s. 3‘6d., which
again seem to include nothing for anything beyond mining
and milling. There is not much to choose in the matter
of costs between the South and its neighbor and rival,
the Sulphide ; yet one quotes 13s. 6£d., the other 16s. Id.
The managers surely might come to some agreement to
present these returns on the same basis.
• • * *
Study of balance-sheets of principal Broken
Hill co.’s, and comparison of metallurgical figures of the
co.’s which smelt, with those of the co.’s which sell their
concentrates, indicate that there is but little difference
in the respective gross profits, calculated, of course, at
per ton of ore raised. The B.H. Prop, and the Sulphide
Corporation naturally experience their best times when
the metal market is with them; but that would not
necessarily be the case with such co.’s as the South,
British, and Block 10, which have to depend entirely
upon the requirements of the foreign smelters. At
present moment returning charges are low; conse-
quently profits of non-smelting co.’s are above normal,
for lead and silver markets are, according to good advices,
quite high enough, and it is safe to assume that, at
expiration of present contracts, returning charges will be
raised, and profits of selling co.’s correspondingly re-
duced. It is a point not to be overlooked in estimating
values of these cos.’ shares, and the probable course of
the market.

So far, spite of all the talk about zinc, there’s
very little margin in the business at Broken Hill. Block
14 treated last half 19,000 tons tailings for £l9ll profit,
just about 2s. per ton. Stuff assayed 15'6 p.c. zinc. But
for the last half-year the mill worked (in 1900) the ore
ran only iO'l p.c. zinc, and the tailings would probably
be poorer than that, on the average. If 15’6 p.c. stuff can
only give 2s. profit, what about the 10 p.c. (and less)
stuff ? There isn’t much room for a market movement
even if the grade kept up. Certainly costs (over 11s. per
ton) should be capable of a heavy reduction.
• * * •
People who were responsible for introducing it
have to own up at last that magnetic concentration won’t
so far successfully handle B.H. Junction North ore.
Attention now has to be given to the old wet system of
concentration. Manager Stockdale has stuck to the latter
all along, and alleges that if he can’t make the mine pay
on that system he won’t mind being kicked out of Broken
Hill. Local people still have heaps of confidence in the
show. Of the two Barrier men who were lately put on
the board, one, who in a general way isn’t a big speculator,
still hangs on to 7500 shares. It wouldn’t be surprising if
an offer for the whole concern were to come shortly
from a foreign syndicate.

Dudley colliery, Newcastle (N.S.W.), was full of
rats before the big explosion in 1897. Since then not a
rat has been seen in the mine, although adjoining col-
lieries swarm with them.

Transpired at the London meeting of share-
holders in the Lloyd Copper Co., Burraga (N.S.W.), that

• • * *
Mt. Boppy North, adjoining Mt. Boppy
(N.S.W.), and, like it, Bull-owned, still shoves along
grittily. To date nearly £3OOO has been sunk in pros-
pecting ; and beyond the channel, which may or may not
be the right thing, there isn't anything to show for it.
Co. has now decided to sink main shaft, which is in reef
stuff, from 200 ft. to 400 ft. Mine is to be equipped with
necessary gear, including rock-drills; and, as parent
mine has proved the life of the reef beyond the fault,
chances are that depth is all that is required to bring the
North into prominence.
• • • *
North Cobar (N.S.W.), mentioned last week as
having been sold (subject to shareholders’ consent) to
“ the Syndicate,” has, it is now stated, been sold to a
syndicate—which isn’t quite the same thing, so far as
Cobar is concerned. Price mentioned is £12,500. This is
understood to be another of the “good things” into
which newspaper-man Newnes. of England, has been put.
Said that an electrical ore-finder was tried on the pro-
perty, with results which made all the years of fruitless
prospecting done by the co. blush a deep crimson. There-
upon £12,500 was offered to the owners, which was a heap
more than the market had judged the show worth ; and
forthwith poor old J. Bull was asked to put his money
into a £lOO,OOO co. First proposal was 25,000 shares
to the public (Newnes being most, if not all, the public),
£12,500 going to the owners, and £12,500 and 75,000
shares to the people who had found out wliat an amaz-
ingly good thing the North Cobar is. When, however, it
was pointed out that that would leave the co. without
working capital, it was proposed that £12,500 should go
to the co., instead of to the promoters, and that the
people who put up the money should also get a few of the
paid shares ; and it looks as though the thing was fixed
at that. Even now capital available is utterly inade-
quate ; and, in spite of the electrical ore-finder, prospects
are poor. Anyhow, the dead weight and ballast of paid-
up shares is absurdly heavy for a prospecting show.

• * * •
Although Ravenswood (N.Q.) goldfield returns
for May show an increase of 6260 z. over those for May,
1904, field is at present not looking so well as could be
wished. New Ravenswood, Ltd., a Bull-owned property,
is making strenuous efforts to keep up appearances, but
the stone is apparently getting poorer. Management is
now not booming the new treatment plant so much, but
it’s about time that it commenced to tell a tale.
The London Co. is producing slightly better
material, and gained some local notoriety' lately
by what the press described as a “big deal” in its
shares. Said “ big deal ” consisted of about 3000 shares
changing hands at something under 45., and the pur-
chasers are regarded as being “in the know.” As with
many other Ravenswood properties, it would not take
long to know everything concerning the London. But
Co. is gradually creeping out of its kindergarten methods,
although it’s doubtful if the finances will permit it to
grow into a healthy child. Ravenswood Deeps are now
looking—successfully—for more money. W. Phillpotts,
an Englishman with American mining experience, has
just been sent out from England to assist Lawrence
Wilson in his grope for the Sunset reef.
* * * •
Ravenswood Deep Mines (N.Q.) are now en-
deavoring to find the money wherewith to locate Sunset
reef. Local shareholders have fallen in with Bull’s pro-
posal that said wherewithal be loaned by shareholders to
co. at 7£ per cent, per annum. In return for this
holders are given the option of taking up additional
shares at par. Exactly' what co. will raise by this means
is not yet known ; but unless the Sunset reef is tapped
pretty quickly present indications are that Ravenswood
will slump almost beyond recall.
Queensland gold returns continue to drop back.
Figures for May are 49,8890 z. compared with 60,3300 z. for
May, 1904. Decreases are—Charters Towers, 63440 z. ;
Gyinpie, 21370 z.: Croydon, 11450 z. ; Mt. Morgan, 1138 oz. ;
Hodgkinson, 4920 z. ; Etheridge, 2470 z. ; and sundry
minor falls. Ravenswood alone shows an increase—36l7oz.
as against 29910 z. for May 1904. Notable that Croydon is
not on the same side as Ravenswood, though for some time
past Queensland has been relying upon Croy'don to dis-
count the falls of the other fields.
* • • *
Report of E. A. Weinberg on the Mount
Molloy (N.Q.) copper mine is published. Very optimistic ;
sets down the value of the present known ore reserves at
precisely £152,522 9s. Method by which this result is
arrived at is, however, not beyond question. First doubt
arises in estimation of ore reserves. “ Ore actually
broken,” and doubtless assayed, goes 9'5 per cent, copper.
That seems right. Ore blocked out, 24,215 tons; assay,
13'5 per cent, copper. That may be right. “Standing
partially blocked out, 5168 tons; assay, 10 per cent.”
This may be wholly wrong. Ore which is only “par-
tially blocked out ” can neither be set down in tons nor
in metallic contents, and certainly should not figure in
“ Ore Reserves.” Other lines come under the heading of
“ Reasonably Expected ” —viz., 6500 tons of 10 per cent,
sulphides and 3800 tons of 8 per cent, oxidised ore. These
“ reasonably expected ” items are also valued in the ore
reserves. Second doubtful point in Weinberg’s estimates
is that the value of copper has been taken on a basis of
£65. For ordinary purposes this is quite £5 above what
some careful men would take, and in some circles £6O is
considered to be higher than the average of the next
12 months will prove to be. Third doubtful point in the
report is that only 50s. per ton is allowed for smelting and
realisation. This in view of the position of the mine
appears insufficient. Mount Molloy will require abso-
lutely nothing to go wrong if it is to realise the dreams of
its promoters.
* » * *
Brilliant Extended, the mine Charters Towers
(Q.) depends unon to prove the deep ground, for the half-
year treated 12,951 tons, worth £2 Bs. Id. per ton. Pre-
vious term, 11,388 tons, valued at £2 18s. a ton. Costs pan
out—mining, 325. 9d.; milling, 6s. Id.; cyaniding, ss. Id. ;
total £2 3s. lid. ; administration, Is. 7d., not included.
Costs of development cannot be arrived at, as part is
boxed up with stoping. Decline of ldwt. in value of stone
will place the co. in a tight position. Management is
moving in the dark, as no sampling has been done.
Battery seems to be run for a time ; then there’s a clean-
up. If returns are low the mill is started again on better
stuff, and an average is thus maintained. Evidently
there is no stone worth mentioning in reserve. Manager
Macleod says an estimate of ore reserves and value
cannot be made without a large expenditure, on account
of the stopes varying from day to day. “ Such an expen-
diture Ido not consider warranted.” Yet 1180 tons were
put through as a trial from Nos. 6 and 7 west for 7dwt.
to 4dwt. a ton. This is rather expensive sampling when
costs are £2 3s. lid. a ton. Stopes are at present small,
notably No. 9 west, from which 2992 tons have been taken
for the six months. No. 2 supplied 5173 tons, nearly
half the quantity crushed. This stope still con-
tains a block, 200 ft. x 100 ft., with 2ft. to sft. of mineral
stone. Costs are so high as to call for attention
at once. Mine is looking well in the deep ground,
Five feet of mineral stone is showing in the hanging wall
at 11 east, 2300 feet vertical depth. A trial parcel is to be
put through; should it come up to expectations, and
there is no reason why it shouldn’t, it will be the deepest
payable stone worked on the field. A hundred feet further
down the underlie, on the west side, a level has been
started on 2ft. of stone. Balance sheet shows a credit of
£7013 ; from this the second half-yearly instalment of
£1250 is to be paid towards the purchase of the battery.
In three months a lump sum of £lO,OOO is to be paid.
Profits for the next couple of years are ear-marked for
purchase money and improvements.

and Wyndham, £12,460 ; Brilliant Central, £2500. Total

* » * •
O.K.’s dividend, paid this month, is the first
to come out of a N.Q. copper mine, in spite of the
Chillagoe and half-a-dozen other expensive booms. Half-
year’s profit and loss showed output worth £15,231,
expenses £9623, profit £5608. Financial position on
March 31st wasn’t, however, particularly strong. Co.
owed the bank net £3994 and other creditors £2498;
against that it had matte on hand £7600, and a few small
items —mostly stores, &c. There has certainly been two
months’ production since then, making on May 24 an
estimated balance available of £15,000. Manager Gibb
andconsulting engineerßrander estimate reserves of 22 p.c
ore at about 63,000 tons ; but of this it does not appear that
more than 29,000 tons are in sight; and N.Q. experience
in estimating reserves that are not in sight has been disas-
trous. “ Assuming the indications to hold good over the
whole area of the ore bodies,” says Allan Gibb, there
should be 33,800 tons below the level explored ; and that
is a large assumption. There are also 23,000 tons of 8 per
cent, ore ; but this cannot be worked at present. To get
out the proposed 300 tons of copper a month will call for
1600 tons of ore, and on basis of past figures should give
nearly £lO,OOO profit; but copper is steadily falling, and
estimates have been made on what looks like the top of
the market. In Jan.-Feb.-March of last year average
London quotation was £57 Is.; same months this year,
£6B Is. ; and now it is barely over £65. Whatever its
speculative value, £160,000 seems to cover the visible
profit in the mine, which, nevertheless, is valued by the
market at just upon £300,000. Any satisfactory develop-
ments are thus heavily discounted already.

South Dempsey’s, Gaffney’s Creek (Vic.), whose
start was to sink a shaft in the bed of the creek, immedi-
ately south of New Dempsey’s, and equip with a tin-pot
plant, has left that portion of the lease and begun to ex-
plore an old tunnel on the opposite side of the range,
known in the early days as the Golden Belt. Co. is now
gathering up battery paraphernalia from defunct district
mines. Large body of low-grade ore has been exposed
in South Dempsey’s present workings, and_ trial crushing
gave Bdwt. per ton. With water as a motive power, this
shouldn’t be too bad.
* • * *
Mining at Wood’s Point (Vic.) wants a little
more than the winter's rain to make it profitable. Victor
is unable to get on to decent stone; and South Victor,
after years of scratching, is to be snuffed out. It is cer-
tain that the Victor will take the lease, as the extension
of the shoot of gold from which it got its gold
in the past is in the South’s ground, if it is
anywhere. Said that Jamieson, once manager of the
New Loch Fyne, at Matlock, is back at the last-named
mine. Battery is going up at the New Loch Fyne, and
soon we may be able to tell whether the big dyke forma-
tion carries enough gold to make it a second Alaska*
Treadwell.
* * * *
Gt. Southern No. 1, Rutherglen (Vic.), is the
lame duck of the field, and it is diagnosed that it will
never regain its strength. Co.'has been struggling on,
year in and year out, to make decent money out of its
wash-dirt, but it always just fell short of being a good
mine. Its drives are now close to the boundary, its lease
of life is short, and so there isn’t much chance of any
money going to anyone unless co. has a streak of luck
when it tries to mine the small block of Great Southern
lead in its property, drained by the Prentice and Southern.
* # # •
The crowd that took up the Star of the East,
Ballarat, is having its reward—on the Stock Exchange.
A little flutter prefaced the striking of the Consols lode at
2290 ft., but the real rally came when a few specks of gold
were seen in the stone on the Guiding Star lode in a rise
from the bottom level. Of course, there was a
half-column puff in one of the Melbourne dailies, which
the man who has had Ballarat fever in the past read with
a dengue-fever face. None the less, Stars are so well held
since the days when divs. dropped from the Consols lode at
1400 ft. level like manna, that even a whisper of gold sets
the market dancing. It is no use to say that nothing
worth counting up has come from the mine since then,
save an odd shilling or two paid from the Guiding Star
lode at shallow depths. People all want to strike a mining
double, and so they buy the scrip. The final little move
was over 3ft. of stone showing beyond a slide on the
Consols lode at 2260 ft. Now everyone wants a trial crush-
ing to see if the egg has any
The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 600 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The Wilfley Slime Table which has been in
operation on the Ivanhoe Gold Mines for some months
past has been giving extremely good results. Mr. H. T
Brett, Metallurgist of the Ivanhoe Gold Corporation, in an
article contributed to the March number of the Chamber
of Mines Journal, W.A., gives particulars of the savings
made, and states that the introduction of the Wilfley
Slime Table will be of great service in treating the low-
grade sulphide ores of the field. Since the future of
Kalgoorlie is so intimately bound up with the most
economical solution of this sulphide problem, this
assurance from Mr. Brett is a most important matter.
The Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne, are
sole manufacturers for Australasia of this table.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discription of cast or wrought iron work.*

Is it that Ararat (Vic.) folk have lost all
aggressiveness ? For a quarter of a century the Langi
Looan, one of the finest alluvial claims ever prospected in
Victoria, has been left idle, and no one seems game to
bell the cat. In the old days Gray, of Maldon, was the
holder, and to-da>, if report does not lie, the lease is in
the hands of a iittle crowd that patronises Peacock s
office. Minister M’Leod talks a lot about tribute agree-
ments, but why does he silently permit ground like that
at Ararat to be left idle more than a month?

Melbourne’s Board of Works spent three
hours the other day discussing the alleged irregu-
lar appointment to a good billet of the nephei/of
one of the Commissioners. One of the remarkable
things about Fitz Gibbon’s Board is the regularity
with which candidates who happen to be related
auab'ficnT- ISS1 ° n r S deV< i lop super ior capacity and
qualifications when applying for positions. They’re
an exceptionally brainy lot, the young relatives of
the Commissioners. In fact, they seem to hnvt
rektiyes 7 ° f the brainS ° f their elderly
Waxellency Madden, presiding at Mel-
bourne Criminal Court, the other day :
There is no sitting of the Criminal Court, and few of the
other courts, from which the Judges do not go home be-
wildered by the effort to defeat justice by the expedient
of lying. Every man at the Bar and on the bench is aware
of that. People who formerly were particularly careful
are now resorting to perjury on the slightest provocation,
in utter defiance of the law, and time and again justice is
notoriously defeated by false-swearing,
It s all part of a growing and general tendency.
Commercial morality weakens under the influence
of fraud. Adulteration of articles of food is
common where it formerly was rare. That the
evidence given in law courts should be also shoddy
is natural enough. In word and deed, the expedi-
ency of lying is recognised, and, if other forms of
dishonesty are exempt from punishment, it stands
to reason that perjury should be now accepted as a
mere matter of course. Perjury starts with the
dishonest baker, the sugar-sanding grocer, and the
shoddy importer. For society to make head
against it, it will be necessary for society to make
an organised campaign against the general tend-
ency to adulterate everything from the beer to the
truth.
* * * *
A diminutive Sairey Gamp, down South
Melbourne way, took the law into her own hands
lately, and for a space defied the authorities to do
their worst. She had recently superintended the
arrival of a new baby, and part of her bill was
still owing—17s. 6d. to wit. Learning that the
mother was about to join her husband in “ the
West,” Gamp determined on action, and, while
the lady was getting her haberdashery and
the other children aboard the boat, she raided
the house, seized the new infant, and “planted”
it with a friend. Then she went round to
the watch-house, told the sergeant her troubles,
and asked him if she had done right!
That official assured her she was liable to 10 years
and 40 days’ dark cells for kidnapping—but he
Was, nevertheless, very pleased to see her, for the
infant s mother was in the next room and
also in hysterics, and was demanding her baby
immediately, as the boat was just about to
sail. The nurse stolidly demanded her 17s. fid.
Ihe mother had only 12s. fid., and the boat was
even then getting the gangway in. Then the Law
threatened to arrest the kidnapper, but she
replied that in that case the baby would stay
where it was, and she was quite prepared to go to
gaol, even for ten years, but she was going to have
her 17s. 6d, By this time the boat had let the
rope go. Finally, after much argument, the
sergeant persuaded Gamp to accept 10s. on
account, and th,e baby was rescued, and rushed
dovyn to the steamer with its mother, and they
bofh, jumped like kangaroos. It was. ignominious,
but considering how little time the law had to!
turn round in, it is difficult to see that it could
have, donp any more. A week’s solitary might
have broken down the nurse’s resolution, but there
was no timp for that, and there wasn’t a thumb-
screw handy.
* * * *
Chief Justice Madden, of Victoria, spoke
firmly to.certain lawyers at Ballarat (Vic.) sessions
lately. The. court, had no, sooner opened than
pase after case had to be adjourned. One barrister
faadn t had time to study his brief; another, the
Crown, Prosecutor, was in the same state of ignor-
ance • a third had missed, one of Bent’s casual
trains ; in a fourth case the papers had been sent
to the wrong lawyer, and, so on. Then the Crown
had failed to provide an interpreter for a Hindu
charged a capital offence, whpreupon his
counsel withdrew, and left the case at a standstill.
Madden adjourned most of these matters to the
following sessions, saying bitterly that they might,
as well drop the assizes altogether. In several of
the cases there is a suspicion that the collapse was
carefully arranged. Nobody with, any previous
convictions wants to.be tried by Madden sipce he
devised the, mechanical idea of sentencing a
prisoner to the sum of all his previous sen-,
tences added together. If a man has done t.en
years for bushranging and eight years for highway'
robbery, the Madden system gives, him 18 years.
(it the law will allow it) when he comes up a third
time for sfealing a shirt off a line, and prisoners
would just as soon see the Chief Justice wholly
unemployed nowadays.

The. Melbourne, caretakers of the Queen
Victoria Memorial Fund have decided to let!
a local professor of geology choose between
Gaioola (N.S.W.) and imported Carrara marble
for the statue. Sculptor James White has carried,
his point as to the substitution of white marble
for bronze, and he considers that the Caloola
stone should prove as good as the best for statuary.
Anyhow, it deserves to be put to the proposed
Pour emblematical figures are included in
Mr. White s design, and these are now being
carved in Italy from Carrara marble. If Queen
Victoria stands for an advt. of the Australian
product she will serve the useful purpose of com-
parison in the distant future. We’d like to know
how her Caloolan constitution will bear up against
the ravages of time.
* * * *
Persistent bad luck appears to attend
Maorilanders who served in that South African
war. In addition to those who have drifted into
gaol, and others who are chronic invalids, and
those who are “stony ” and thirsty, quite a number
have committed suicide or met sudden deaths.
Last disaster chronicled was that of a farm-burner
who was himself burned to death in a pub. at
Gore—of all places in the land.
UNFAIR DIVISION.
SUE: “ That’s your cake. Tom; I ate a piece."
TOM: “ Been better for me if you’d eaten the
cake and left the piece.”
Ever the landlord !: Victoria has just
spent some £50,000 in closing 37 unnecessary
hotels in North Melbourne, and so far as is visible
the only persons to benefit thereby have been the
landlords of the 20 unclosed pubs.. Last week one
of these got an ejectment order against one of his
tenants, who had demurred to the rent being
violently raised as soon a,s opposition was reduced.
That is the sort of Socialism that Eat cordially
approves; the State pays all the costs and
Private Enterprise reaps all the profits.

perspiringly, B. Go.su.

A prisoner in Albury (N.S.W.) gaol
recently tried to commit suicide by swallowing 11
inches of wire. Six inches were down the
unfortunate man’s throat before he was noticed,
and two days elapsed before it was extracted.
The would-be suicide is serving a life sentence.
He is practically blind and was convicted of
attempting to burn his wife (also nearly blind)
alive. Doubts as his sanity were raised at the
trial, and the desperate attempt to finish his
miserable existence seems to strengthen those
doubts.
* * * *
A remarkably smart man called recently
on a farmer near Lilimur (Vic.) and told him off-
handedly that there was a Socialistic law by
which he wasn’t allowed to have more
than a certain number of sheep on his
farm, and a certain amount of money in
the bank. And right there he collected
the sheep and money that the farmer possessed
over and above the alleged legal margin. As an
instance of the effects of the Argus’s lies about
Socialism, acting on the weak intellect of the ig-
norant class which supports Bent and the Kyabram
movement, this episode is unique.

A woman’s vanity gives the show away
once more. The other day, three Melbourne
detectives were walking down Hwanston-street,
when a gorgeously-dressed woman, in a very large,
bright, red hat, smote their six eyes all at once.
The red hat was waiting for somebody, and it was
such a remarkable red hat that the detectives
quietly watched. Two men came out of a neigh-
boring hotel, and as they walked towards the
buggy were at once recognised by the ’tecs as old
acquaintances who were wanted to explain away
a little matter of gilded farthings. One of the
great wants of the undeserving criminal classes in
these days is a donah who will not turn the pro-
ceeds of dishonest labor into a gorgeous sealskin
coat or a startling red cart-wheel hat. A lady
of quiet brown tastes would be a great source of
comfort to many a fraudulent man.

MEMORY.
[For The Bullett:*. }
She calls from dim hills far away,
Her fair face flits the leaves between ;
Her robes are decked with garlands gay,
And fashioned all of fairy green.
And “ follow, follow me,” she cries,
'‘Recall the joys thou hast forgot,”
So lays upon my dreaming eyes
Her sceptre of Forget-me-not.
Then, glad at heart, I follow her,
The queen of an enchanted land,
The buds of happy years that were,
Break into bloom beneath her hand.
Alight the boughs with wattle gold,
Uplifts the sweet epacris spire,
And in the bushland grasses old
The scarlet runner’s trailing fire.
Belovfcd, when the hour-glass wanes,
And all the ways of earth are brown,
When sunset tints thy window-panes,
And twilight flutters slowly down ;
While waitest thou thy heart’s sunrise,
I pray the Queen of whom we wot
May lay upon thy dreaming eyes
Her sceptre of Forget-me-not.
S..A,
A. M. BOWYER-ROSMAN.
ETAY 47.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sun was once a sight divine
As. he rose from, the deep,
When Youth and Hope and Love and Wine
Spared little time for sleep.
What were those things that used to seem
So pleasant to my mind ?
Delilah’s kisses in a dream,
The shadow of the wind.
But now with the true hearts I krow
I am not all forlorn,
The sunset has a rosier glow
Than ever had the morn.
The past had less of joy than pain,
And age brings vision clear—
I would not live the past again,
And yet—Delilah—dear !
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALRY.

* * * *
Sydney divorce case, Crocker v. Crocker,
in which judgment was delivered tor the peti-
tioner, exhibits the amazing daring and resource-
fulness of an excitable female. The respondent,
Mrs. Crocker, some time in 1902, made the ac-
quaintance of a musician named Bedford, the co-
respondent, through answering a matrimonial
advertisement. To him she represented herself
as a single woman —although living with her hus-
band and two children. She explained away her
incumbrances to Bedford by declaring that her
husband was her brother-in-law; that the infants
were his, and that he had engaged her to look
after them while their mother was absent in
Greece, of all places on earth. It would have
been a graceful touch of fancy if she had said that
the mother was disguised in male attire and
serving with the Japanese army, but perhaps she
didn’t think of that. Then she converted Bedford
into a sort of myth, by informing her husband
that a man was haunting her, peering in at the
windows at all hours, and in a general way myste-
riously misbehaving himself. _ She being under
medical treatment for hysteria at the time, the
husband regarded these ideas as mere hallucina-
tions, and the fact of his wife being in such a wild
state induced him to permit her to go away several
times for a change of scene. She went with Bed-
ford as his wife, and for two years she prevented
the men from meeting, and ran her dual existence
successfully. Her little show burst up at last, but
it burst very gracefully, and the respondent was
ready to explain everything to the last.
» * * *
A Melbourne firm was robbed of a number
of brass engine-fittings some time ago, bought
them back again, not recognising them, and was
subsequently prosecuted for receiving stolen
property. The firm escaped with its life, but it
reckons this is a hard world.
In Sydney, a few days ago, a man named
Everingham was tried on a charge of criminal
assault, but as the chief witness against him, a
girl of 16, admitted that, when she swore at the
preliminary inquiry to having seen the assault,
she had been lying, the case broke down. Being
pressed as to why she had endeavored to get an
innocent man into gaol, she said she did it because
Blank Blank (or, as the newspaper report put it,
“ ”) told her to do so. The case
wandered to its final breakdown without the real
name of Blank Blank being published, and there
was no mention, nor has there since been any
mention, so far as The Bulletin is aware, of any
intention to make further inquiry concerning
either the perjured girl witness or her alleged
instigator, Blank Blank. There is something
curiously lop-sided about a system which prints at
full length the two front names and the surname
of the person wrongfully accused of a very bad
offence, and which so carefully pours the ink-blot
of anonymity over the name of the person who is
said to have instigated a much worse offence. It
isn’t the blot itself that is objectionable, but the
tendency to blot one person and not another.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Lands Commission struck a
desperately cautious witness when it called on
MacFarlane, Under-Secretary for Lands. No
bygone MacFarlane, with his kilt streaming out
in the cold breeze of the 15th century, was more
careful in looking round a damp rock for a hostile
MTherson than was this Under-Secretary. In
fact, even before he appeared cautiously on the
horizon there were signs that he was a careful
kind of man. When it was decided that, instead
of the highest tenderer for a certain block getting
the land, the lowest one—a letter-carrier who
was closely related to a member of Parliament-
should be informed of the highest offer and
allowed to raise his bid accordingly, the Under-
secretary had to make a memo, on the subject,
and it read :
In these cases, as in others which have been dealt with
during the last two years, there is no objection to
allowing the applicants for the offer to bid up to the
highest tender.
After that no one could say that the practice had
been initiated by the cautious Mac. He was
completely hidden behind a precedent The same
wary man, while giving evidence, wouldn’t even
refer to the Commission’s copy of the Land Act,
but sent to his office for his own copy. He knew
his own copy wouldn’t bite, and he didn’t know
exactly how wild the Commission’s copy might
be.

The N.S.W. Lands Commission is begin-
ning to get on the community’s nerves. Even in
its sleep now it hears a voice like a large devil
crying out: “ Who drew that bill for £l5O V ”
The 86 miserable and long-haired Christian
Israelites, who recently left Victoria under
prophets “Mary 5 and “Benjamin, 5 have arrived
in Benton Harbor, U.S.A. They were met by
brass bands and a procession, and escorted to a
banquet of 16 courses—of vegetables. Having
dined heartily on cabbage, peanut and water, they
were lined up to drop their aggregate £37,000 into
the “common fund,” from which it will never
emerge, and after that the married couples were
separated, and told that they were never to asso-
ciate again until the “Second Coming,” which is
expected about 1916. It seems to be a cheerless
religion of loneliness, vegetables, cold water, long
hair, and hope —that hope deferred that makes
the peanut lie heavy on the stomach.

A reminiscence, by “ The Mussel ” :
Vampire Day was probably the most dismal patriotic
observance that ever struck this walloped and knocked-
out earth. Only one ray of light pierced the gloom. In
a restaurant where I am still trusted, a waitress, whose
taking ways, etc., are more fully developed than her
intelligence, asked this question of a sad-eyed cus-
tomer : “ Wot are all the flags flying for to-
day ? Is it the Prince of Wales’ Birthday? „
The depressed client looked hard at her. “No,
said he, “this is his grandmother’s birthday. Then
the girl pondered with the p ate of sausages if) her
hand “ Why do you sav ms ? Isn’t the Prince of Wales
a woman?" she asked. The miserable-looking man nearly
choked himself with a lump of tripe. “ No,” he said,
“ the Prince of Wales is a man.” “Now, don t pull my
le«%”said the fair one. “Ain’t the Prince of Wales a
woman?” turning inquiringly to a rather youthful cus-
tomer. “ Ah, well —er that is to say —er ap-
pearances seems to be against —er —that sort of theory.
You see ‘ Prince ’ stands for the man and ‘ Princess for
the woman.” Then a great dawn of intelligence climbed
slowly over her peach-like features. “Ah ! now I know i
the difference between prince and princess is just the
same as the difference between waiter and waitress.

41 # *
Melb. Argus has taken up the question of
the secret remission of prisoners’ sentences. It
has transpired that the Government has been
sheltering itself behind a provision of the Vic-
torian Crimes Act, under which any prisoner may
be released after serving two years, provided he
leaves the State for good. This provision, which
m i<rht be called a Scheme for Filling Other Aus-
tralian States with Yic. Criminals has been
availed of in many instances during the past ten
years, mostly for the benefit of convicts with influ-
ential relatives in Toorak or friends on the Cham-
ber of Commerce. Another sensational revelation
has been made regarding a number of homi-
cides ordered to be detained during the Gover-
nor’s'pleasure, having been set at liberty within a
year or two of their crimes, indicating that the
Governor is a gentleman very easily pleased. All
these releases have been kept secret, and in some
cases the intimation has even been omitted from
the published lists of discharged prisoners supplied
to the police.

A beautiful instance of simplicity in a
pawnbroker. At South Melbourne Court, the
other day, a 13-year-old girl, whose mother had
been wanting food, was charged with the theft of
a five-stone diamond ring, valued at £35. trie
child’s idea in stealing this first-class piece of pro-
perty—she stole it from the bedroom of a lady
who gave her employment—was to pawn
it for 10s. The kind pawnbroker “lent
her ss. on the ring, because he thought the dia-
monds were paste, he told the magistrates, and
thinking they were paste, he valued them at lus.
Whether he also mistook the gold setting for
brass was not made clear in the newspaper report
of the transaction. If he had troubled to “ clean
it up and examine it” he would have known the
ring was a genuine article. The dirtiness deceived
him, therefore he risked five “bob” on the ring
for the dear child’s sake, finding comfort in the
thought that he who giveth to the poor lendeth to
the Lord. Pawnbrokers are whales for lending to
the Lord.

The other day the Norman Hotel, Woollon-
gabba (Q.), was entered by two men. They were
surprised by a constable, whom they attacked
with an iron bar. The constable was found lying
insensible, with a fractured skull, on the footpath,
with the iron bar, broken in two pieces, beside
him. The doctors say that the policeman will re-
cover.
A CAMEO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fashioned for a lover ;
Little shining head,
Breasts that laces cover,
Whence my reason’s fled.
Eyes—wet violets sleeping ;
Lashes—curling silk ;
Ears like pink shells peeping ;
Dimpled throat, like milk.
Cheeks, just baby roses,
Flushed with swift, shy pink ;
Red mouth just supposes
How a man might drink.
Flowery hands of whiteness,
Most divinely sweet,
Like white doves in lightness—
Little childish feet.
Just a sweet, shy woman,
Where God did His best.
As I’m man and human,
You can guess the rest.
N.S.W.
HELEN JEROME.
THE DRAGON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The watchers slept, and then we crept—
All though Care followed as we stept—
Gray-eyes, as softly as a mouse
Until we entered Love his house.
Then were we ware the Dragon Care
Had checked, and could not enter there ;
He threatened, in a fiery din,
Of no avail to enter in.
Now from Love’s latticed spying-pane
We watch the sullen Dragon wane :
And fade, as still we watch him pine,
Our troubles with him, Rose of mine.
VIC.

It sounds like the mosquito which stole a horse

A great favorite and old military and society
vetman, Captain Daveney, died at Auckland
(M.L.) last week. He had been captain in a
crack Irish regiment, and was at the time of his
death paymaster of Imperial pensions. Mrs
Arthur MacArthur, of Sydney, was a daughter of
the deceased officer. Mrs. Todd, of Mosman
was another.

Dr John Mildred Creed, M.L.C. (N.S.W.) in
May last, wedded, as the announcement tells us
the widow of the late ’’ James Williams, of
Queensland. Dr. Creed has been before the N.S. W.
public as a legislator for 33 years. In 1872 he
represented the Upper Hunter in the Legislative
Assembly, and in August, 1885, Alexander Stuart
made him an M.L.C. This bride and groom were
much complimented at the opening of Parliament.

presently to the U.S. A. to try her luck. At the

theatricals. Eighty-six members of the Royal
Comic Opera Co. will train to Adelaide, en route

take his typhoid full strength, has so far recovered
