Cruy Booth by. He will in less than a fortnight’s
time go to Adelaide, to stay with friends at Mount
.Lofty, and he hopes to be ready for the July
season, starting on the 16th. Miss Rose Mus-
grove, who, I am told, attributes her illness to
eating oysters fresh off the rock ” down in some
sunlit cove of peace ” (as Roderic Quinn has it),
is still within the danger zone. The fresh rock
was evidently unhealthy, and her attack is a
virulent one. Miss Alexia Bassian has Sydney’s
sympathy. She is again out of the cast of “ The
Cingalee,” through a recurrence of throat trouble.

and had to be operated on.
Miss Nance O’Neil, considerably slighter and
more youthful-looking than she was four years
ago, came into our blue-and-gold weather on her
way to Melbourne. A huge, black-feathered hat,
navy-blue frock and grey coat formed her stepping-
ashore outfit; and her former companion, Miss
Rucoa Allen, and the white cat, Magda, were not
tar behind. Ihe uncanny-looking black cockatoo
that formed part of her menagerie last trip was
bought by London Zoo.
The grass-green tie—shrill, emerald green—is
being adopted by the smart young man with all
the eagerness with which the old bachelor or
widower adopts the 17-year-old baby-girl. Private
Secretary Share is wearing one; Mr. “Glen-
da 11 Kelly, M.P., has the like ; baritone Haigh
Jackson loses his heart nightly to the Cingalee
with the same seventeenth-of-Ireland color round
his collar. These three blades of grass recently
pervaded the pavement near Jeans’, at a moment
when Mr. Owen Cox’s brougham—his horses
wearing the same tone of green in ear-rosettes
was at the kerb. If the horses had been hungry
they would have come over and eaten the ties for
certain. It is to be hoped that P.S. Share won’t
encounter any starving stock when he is in W.A.
When a flock of sheep cast themselves on the
bosom of a private secretary somebody is almost
certain to be trodden on.
Dental Hospital celebrated its third birthday
with a smart ball last week. It might be called
for short the Tooth Ball. Mrs. W. T. Beckett,
Mrs. C. C Marshall, and Mrs. E. R. Magnus
who are always to the front in the matter of
working for trie bantling institution, surveyed
with satisfaction a gathering of some 300 guests.
Mrs. Marshall brought a snowy-robed debutante
daughter. This free dental institution, like most
charities, wants funds. It has done an enormous
amount of work during the past year, but a great
proportion of the patients have been old persons
who require sets of teeth. And these demand
an actual outlay of money—which the Hospital
hasn t got. Dental experts give their time will-
ingly and unstintedly to the relieving of pain and
to other forms of tooth work, but they can hardly
be expected to buy Darby and Joan’s complete new
apparatus. Someone has been suggesting that the
bloated Dental Board, which has a fund (anything
that has a fund these days lays itself open to the
adjective bloated’ )should assist, so thatprovision
could be made for students at Hospital and School.
Since it hyphenated itself with the Uni. Dental
School twenty chairs instead of only six have been
made available.
The newest acquisition to Rose Bay peacock
society is Dr. Piers-Hatton and his pretty wife-
said to be much his junior. They come from Japan,
are nearly as rich as the Mikado was before the war
and have motor cars, steam launches, and queer
little dogs. Mervyn,” Mr. “Ted” Simpson’s
house, has been secured by them for 12 months.

“ Mrs. S. Rowan (daughter of Surgeon O’Hara)
gave a farewell party last week, preparatory to
steaming home to Bananaland’s wool areas. The
guests were recruited from Toorak, the theatrical
quarter, and the smart set which is rather
Bohemian. Mrs. Rowan is a large breezy matron,
young in years and manner.
“It's just now a close season for river suicides.
Ihe Yarra really ought to be heated to aid the
world-weary, the love-lorn, or the debt-haunted
to a quiet end. The other evening, after sunset,
when grim shadows were stretching over the
Alexandra drive and the city side of Prince’s
Bridge, a wet, shivering mortal crept to the
nearest policeman and said that he didn’t want to
die any more. After a neat dive from the grassy
bank of the Yarra and twice sounding the bottom
of the stream, drowning proved none of the things
it was cracked up to be, and the second time of
rising the wet and greatly chilled suicide clam-
bered out and bolted, urged on by cold and con-
science, to the shelter of the blue arm of the
Law. He was taken into custody to be dried and
tried according to the Act.
“Society has suddenly developed a literary
craving. Mrs. Maudsley, the tall dame of the
Loll ins-street medico who has the beautiful bed-
side manner, runs the ’Varsity Extension Lectures,
and rounds-up all the Toorakites to the harangues.
Moreover, she converts herself into a doorkeeper,
and takes the shillings of the public with a smile
that glorifies the post. On Tuesday last, the little
Independent Hall wasn’t nearly big enough for
the crowd that paid its silver coin for the enter-
tainment. Scattered through a most fashionable
audience were many spectacled ’Varsity grads and
sad intellectual spinsters. Why is there no bright-
ness in the face of the woman who affects literary
taste ?

Pillow-patters and pill-administrators are
close up to the frolicsome chorus chick as the
rival of the society flutterer. Within the last
month, two of the city’s Nightingales have cap-
tured wealthy patients for better or worse. Toorak
meanwhile sits at home like the Beatrice of the
Bard, and cries ‘ Heigh-ho! for a husband.’
bociety girls have tried hard to beat the chorus

in the captivating nurse. She represents the
domestic ideal.
“Velvet dresses are in vogue. Long, slim
dreamy-eyed gazelles are rushing them. Romance
and terrace dust always cling to trailing velvet,
and the leaves of (an alleged) memory seem to
make a mournful rustling in that fabric. Silk is
up-to-date and shallow. Satin is bridal. Brocade
is elderly. Velvet alone is heavy with red rapture
or grey grief.

“Melb. Nat. Gallery haunters are gazing these
days on a new large canvas, signed Hare. Two
Christian martyrs to-be (a white beauty and a
brown slave) are lying in a loose, slumbrous
embrace-hands flung over the hips—on a faggot
of straw. A shaft of sunlight (presumably sun-
light) throws the upper parts ®f the two bodies
into brilliant relief. From the darker recess of
the cell the cold, hungry gleam of a lion’s eyes
make a point of light. Two of the hairy monsters
contemplate from behind their bars the toothsome
bodies. The white beauty is big-hipped, long-
limbed somewhat of a young Juno. The expres-
sion of soft and easy sleep is good. Her long
tawny locks ripple from the quiet brows right
over the straw in a cloud. She represents cold,
chaste, splendid strength in an easy dream. The
brown girl is smaller, younger, and lacks the
spiritual air. She sleeps with lips apart in the
Asiatic manner. It’s a striking canvas and might
have been a masterpiece but for the over-emphasis
of shade and the drawing of the white girl’s arm.
As it is, with two or three imperfections, it makes
an attractive and interesting addition to the
Stawell Gallery. And the crowd adores it for its
suggestion of dear Mercia and ‘ Sign of the Cross.’

Stonington, and begged to be let off all junketing.
Only once her charity, or her curiosity, moved her
to pay a visit with the local Governor’s dame to
an institution of the blind or maimed, I forget
which. At any rate, that was her one dissipation.

" A violent wind struck across Moonee Valley
and Saturday’s Oaklands Hunt muster, and
thrashed the sides of the straining tents. But the
Valley remained where it was, the tents stood to
their pins, and the crowd stuck to its clothes. The
little Toorak clan that is coaxed to the Valley
meetings by the Miller proprietors had made
themselves trim and taut against the stormy hour,
none with more effect than Miss Jean Langtree,
who was as neat a clipper as ever faced a blus-
tering wind.
“ The Miller idea of separating the sheep from
the goats (I say nothing of the kid section, always
a factor at these meetings) when tea-time comes
round, fails every time. A tremendous effort is
made to round up the Cream and hurry it into the
scones before the Skimmed Milk learns that the
billy’s boiling. But somehow the Skimmed Sort
gets along to the tent early and gains the enclosure
in good time for the first brew of tea. Fancy the
every-day race-goer and his missus lapping up the
first brew, while a remnant of the Toorak clans
stands around the reserve waiting for its turn
after the Early Savages !

"An awkward thing has occurred in connection
with an art competition in a country town.
-f he landscape painting which was awarded
by Melbourne Art judges the first prize was
found, after the exhibition, to closely resemble
a sketch in a British magazine. The local gal-
committee in haste corresponded with the
Melbourne critics. The result was that all the
other pictures in that class of the exhibition were
conveyed to town for a second review, and the
prize-winner lost the landscape medal.
“The movement organised in Melbourne to
stop the war may now be regarded as a failure.
Nine people in all formed themselves into a com-
mittee to appeal to the two combatants to cease
hitting each other, and those nine attempted to
organise a demonstration at the Town Hall.
If the event had come off, and resolutions
had been passed to the effect that Mr. Ivan and
Mr. Yamagata ought to go home again at once,
R probable that neither of those angry and
combustible persons would even have heard of it.
But the event didn’t come off, and the committee
had to pass round the headgear and take up a
collection to pay for the gas.

CURED OF NEURALGIA
After Years of Torture by Or. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“ nV'TO one under Heaven ever had Neuralgia
IX] worse than I had. It wore me down to
1 ll a wreck, and drove me to the very
verge of madness. When Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills cured me, they can cure the
worst case of Neuralgia in this world. There’s
no doubt about that.” This is the solemn, sworn
statement of Mrs. Margaret Purkis, Thorne and
Forsyth streets, Newtown, Wagga Wagga.
“I used to be a plump, healthy woman till
my blood got out of order,” said Mrs. Purkis.
“Before long my nerves broke down—and then
the Neuralgia started. Sharp, burning pains
nagged and dragged at my nerves till I writhed
and screamed with the agony. The pains in my
head were just like stabs from red-hot table-
knives. For weeks I could get no rest. One bad
attack lasted for four months. All this time I lay
paralysed with pain. I could not move from my bed.
“The agony was so terrible that I nearly
lost my reason,” Mrs. Purkis continued. “It
was only from sheer exhaustion that I ever
slept. I could not eat solid food at all.
Every time I opened my mouth, a piercing
pam tore through my head and face till
every nerve seemed on fire. Even when the
neuralgia was not nearly as bad as it often was,
there were days when I could not leave my bed
through weakness and pain. People would come
in and go out of the house and I would never
know they were there. At times I seemed lo
be unconscious of everything but my terrible
fiendish sufferings. At other times I was so
irritable that I could not bear the children near
me. If they walked across the floor every foot-
step sent a fresh spasm of pain through me.
“All my housework had to be left undone,”
Mrs. Purkis added. “ I had to depend upon the
kindness of the neighbors to do whatever was
absolutely necessary. When I was well enough
to leave my bed, the neuralgia had so drawn up
the nerves of my arm that I could not raise it to
comb inv hair. I used to rave up and down the
room like a maniac, frantic with pain. For a year
I hardly ever had my head out of flannels. I tried
all kinds of medicines, but nothing was of any use.
Ordinary treatment failed even to ease the pains.
My case baffled doctors and everyone else.
“I was worn to a wreck when one day my little
girl brought me home a book telling how Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People cured
Neuralgia. It said they actually made new blood
—and that good red blood was really the food of
the nerves." The cases they had really cured were
just like my own, only not so bad—so I picked up
heart, and sent to Williams’ the chemist in Fitz-
maurice-streefi*for a supply of these blood-building
pills.
‘ ‘ The very first box gave me a better appetite
than I had had for months,” Mrs. Purkis con-
cluded. “Soon I noticed my nerves were a bit
steadier. Gradually the color came back to my
cheeks and new strength to my legs. I began to
biighten up and look after my house again. The
children’s noise no longer worried me and I gained
in weight. Before long two or three days would
pass and I wouldn’t have a twinge of Neuralgia.
At last I was completely cured. Now I tell
everyone that when Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
cured me, they can cure anybody.”
Mrs. Purkis’ case is only one of hundreds.
Among your own neighbors you will find cases
just as wonderful, in which Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills have struck at the root of other blood
diseases* like anaemia, indigestion, rheumatism,
sciatica, backache, kidney diseasd, liver complaint,
headaches, and the special-secret sufferings of
growing girls and women when the regularity of
their blood-supply is deranged. Kemember,
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills do not act on the bowels...
Thev do one thing only, but they do it well—they
actually make new blood. If you are not sure
whether your special trouble is caused by bad
blood or not, write for free medical advice to the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney. From.the
same address you can order the genuine Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills by mail—2s. 9d. a box, s;x
boxes 15s. 3d., post free.

became Gil-lott. Present Mayor Pleasance is now
Pleas-owicc. Seems to be a fatality about it.
Randolph Bedford has sold English pub-
lisher Heinemann an Australian novel, “ Strength
and the Snare.”
H. L. Ayers’ will has been lodged at
Adelaide for probate, value £86,000. The dead
man was son of Henry Ayers, who was seven
•times Premier and ten times Chief Secretary of
South Australia, and subsequently President of
■the Legislative Council for 12 years.
S. Australia, which is vigorously firing out
from its public service everybody suspected of
being 65, sent three septuagenarians to Parliament
the other day—Burgoyne,,7B ; Gilbert, “father of
the House,” 76; and Sellar, 74.
The reodntly-deceased Captain Charles G.
Clark (he died aged 77), who acted as shipping
reporter .for Melbourne Age from ’54 to 90, was
the son df a naval officer who was on board the
Northumberland during her voyage from England
to St. Helena with Napoleon Bonaparte.
Some of (those law-writers were funny
■dogs. Thus Pollock on the “Law of Torts”
■(fifth edition, page 236) :
It is actionable to charge a barrister witli being a dunce,
or being ignorant of the law ; but not a justice of the
peace, for he need not be learned.
Queensland Lands Minister Bell is re-
garded by all parties as the drone of the Govern-
ment. He takes country trips on the slightest
pretext while departmental work accumulates.
The pile of papers on his desk grows daily, and
will soon obscure him from view altogether.
Archdeacon Giinther, of Parramatta (N.S.
Wales) is said to have declined more bishoprics
than any other Anglican preacher in Australia.
Hd was an old Parramatta schoolboy, and has
spent most of his life as incumbent in the sleepy
town. He often says he will close his days at his
present post. The Parramattans call him the
Citizen Bishop.
CHIEF JUSTICE WAY, OF S.A.
An attempt to dig up some of the half-
forgotten remains of ancient Groperdom is likely
to be the next move in Westralian politics.
Already voices from the remote past are heard
insinuating that Forrest and Throssell. and Lefroy
and Illingworth, and Venn are ready to come to
the rescue of their country with a very small
amount of encouragement.
James Norton, M.L.C., the Nestor of
Sydney solicitors, is son of the James Norton,
also M.L.C., who published a remarkable book in
London in 1857. In that volume he described
the aldermen of Sydney as “low-minded, and
unprincipled persons.” And even that wasn’t the
most violent remark in the book.
Archdeacon Tyrrell, who died in a private
hospital the other day at 78 years of age, was
nephew, and not brother, as stated, of Dr. Tyrrell,
first Bishop of Newcastle. The Bishop left a
quarter of a million to the church, but most of it
melted in bad speculations, and droughts. Nephew
Tyrrell never tried to stack up money like uncle.
The great Haynes family doesn’t lack
confidence. Brother Dick, K.C., in Perth the
other day, before Justice Parker began his
summing-up in a big case, cheerfully handed up■ a
paper containing “a few points of law” that he
would like him to remember. And Justice
Parker contented himself with rebuking Richard
with a very mild rebuke.
Joe Darling will only receive £2OOO under
the will of his father, who left £67,000, but he has
had the Tasmanian sheep-station already. Brother
John, who wouldn’t be Premier of S.A. when he
had the chance, and now hasn’t the chance, is to
get £lO,OOO. There were eight childreu. John
and Joseph are joint trustees.
Book-Arcade Cole boasts that he has
issued over 1,000,000 pamphlets, etc., against the
White Australian “ fallacy. One would think
that a man who had done his best to make his
country a nation of half-castes would want to
keep the fact quiet. But there’s no accounting
for the taste of the person who looks forward to a
Heaven modelled on the plan of Little Bourke*
street.
Thos. H. Thwaites, father of the engineer-
in-chief of Melbourne Board of Works, has just
celebrated his 80th birthday. He arrived in Mel-
bourne in March, ’42, and carried on business with
his father as a cabinet-maker. The first furniture
manufactured by the firm was made in John Bat-
man’s kitchen. Now the trade has shifted from
the white man’s kitchen to the Chinaman s bed-
room.
Captain Dumaresq, of Longford (l as.), on
Saturday last passed into his 104th year. The
captain, whose pedigree would equal a Gippsland
tree if stood on end, was originally in the Bombay
Army, but was appointed Surveyor-General of
Van Diemen’s Land in 1826, also Commissioner of
the Court of Requests.
Royalism in Europe promises to die
naturally of social scandals without waiting to be
decently buried alive. The latest Princess to get
into discreditable print is a Bourbon lady,
daughter of Don Carlos. She was “keeping
house ” with an Italian artist, and the incident of
a bullet being lodged in the artist has attracted
attention to the circumstance.
Died at Adelaide, recently, Rev. James
Gordon, aged 88, father of Justice Gordon. He
was born in Dumfries, Scotland, and studied for
the Presbyterian Church at Edinburgh Univer-
sity. In 1858 he came to S.A. as Presbyterian
minister for Mount Barker, and three years later
heard a voice calling him to Gawler, where he
remained until his retirement from the preaching
industry in 1898.
Parson Lorimer Fison lias been awarded a
pension of £l5O per year by the British Govern-
ment in recognition of his services to the science
of anthropology. Mr. Fison joined the Wesleyan
Church in the very early 70’s, and spent 20 years
missionising in Fiji. He has published many
articles on the Fijians and their customs, and has
been for some years editor of the Methodist
newspaper.
Some of the recent large army of candidates
for the Victorian police force were candid. One
wrote to an M.L.A. for a testimonial, urging, as a
reason therefor, “ If I don’t get into the force this
time I see nothing but hard work before me for
the rest of my life.” He got his reference, but it
didn’t pass him. Another confessed having put
down his religion as “ Church of England,” be-
cause he couldn’t spell “ ‘ Presbyterian ’ on the
spur of the moment.”
James Marshall, M.L.C., one of Seddou’s
fossils in the Upper House, is alluded to by a
Maoriland writer as a lineal descendant of Kenneth
M‘Alpine, first King of Scotland. But Kenneth
M‘Alpine is only a shade less legendary than the
40 Scotch kings from Fergus MacEarc to Fergus
MacFerchard, also Kings Selvach, Drostan,
Angus Mac Fergus, Constantine, Grig, and other
kilted specks and trifles of the dim, dim past.
James Gormly, M.L.C. (N.S.W.), testi-
monialised by his old Murrumbidgee constituents,
the other day, brought a beautiful brogue to Aus
tralia 64 years ago, and still keeps it. He was
three years old then, and was droving before he
was 10. He is a teetotaller and a non-smoker. In
the Gundagai flood of ’52, when 100 lives were
lost, all the Gormly family, save two, were
drowned. At one time, when carrying the mails
between Riverina towns, Mr. Gormly employed
the Tichborne Claimant to drive one of his coaches.
British Prime Minister Balfour’s grand-
father was an army contractor in India, and was
for a time suspended for taking tips from the
rajahs. He made £300,000 in four years, and then
returned to Scotland, where he married a daughter
of the Earl of Lauderdale. He is still remembered
in the Highlands with hatred because of the
farmers he evicted to make the deer forest of
Strathconan in Ross-shire—something after the
fashion of the execrated William the Conqueror,
when he planned out his New Forest.
The first solicitor admitted in Victoria—
then Port Phillip—was Frederick Lord Clay, who
arrived at Melbourne in 1840, and was admitted to
practise on May 8, 1841. On the same day Henry
Field Gurner was admitted ; he was a Sydney
native who had served his articles as a clerk in the
N.S.W. Supreme Court, and who subsequently
became Crown Solicitor. Mr. Clay returned to
England in 1877, intending to settle in Derbyshire,
where the Derbyshire neck comes from.
“Tip”: The late J. J. Shillinglaw lost
his position as Government Shipping Master in
Melbourne through not being able to control his
temper when the autocratic Jas. M'Culloch was
dressed in a little sudden and violent authority.
For a while J.J.S. was reduced to living in the
Immigrant’s Home. Speaking of this period, he
once said to me : “ I descended into hell.” It was
the late Chief Justice Stawell (when Acting-
Governor) who secured Shillinglaw’s return to the
Government service after seven years’ absence, by
obtaining for him a position in the Health
Department.
A very old Sydney name, Rundle, was
changed into Perkins at St. Mary Abbotts, Ken-
sington, London, on April 29. The bride is grand-
daughter of the late Jeremiah Brice Rundle,
M.L.C. The first Rundle (Nehemiah) was an
auctioneer in Pitt-street and King-street in the
early 40’s, and J. B. Rundle, senr., was, in the
late 40’s, a merchant in Queen’s Place with a
private residence in Elizabeth-street. This gentle-
man, who was the M.L.C., was subsequently in
partnership with F. H. Dangar in Clarence-street.
The Rundles have a big interest in station property
and in hrieks and mortar in Sydney, and a strange
habit of being christened something ending in
“’miah” or “’kiah.”

Mackey, one of the “ honorables ” in the
Bent Ministry, is chief wire-puller in that shaky
concern. His functions are to supply Thomas
with facts, jokes, and impromptu epigrams, out of
which the bulky Bent sometimes evolves a genuine
bit of unconscious humor. Mackey, a lawyer who
used to be a type-setter in the Government Print-
ing Office, began his political career with an
attempt at the South Melbourne seat, where his
strongest advocacy was for a land-tax. When he
stood for West Gippsland the land-tax was thrown
overboard and woman’s suffrage, or some other
mild thing, was substituted.
At last Sydney University examination for
M. degree two women sat, both of whom had
married since they took their 8.A.. and both of
whom had successfully read up for the higher pass
in the intervals between darning socks. One, Mrs.
Armitage, had also acquired two children in the
interval; the other, Mrs. Austin, a grand daughter
of Henry Clarke, for years the “father” of
N. Assembly, travelled across from Adelaide
for the exam. She, these times, helps her husband
run a little up-country Congregational church in
S. Australia.
Anti-Socialistic Melbourne recently pur-
chased a huge metal casket to be presented to
Malcolm M‘Eacharn, as a token of sympathy in
the matter of his defeat by Labor member Dr.
Maloney. But M'Eacharn went to England, and
he was so long away that a message was sent
at last asking whether the metal trunk should
be posted to him. Now a glad cable has come
saying, “Retain casket.” This evidently means
that Malcolm will be back soon tef view things
with alarm, and announce that the time is now
ripe. Malcolm has evidently had it forced upon him
that he is only one of the multitude in England.
In that country knights grow on .trees, and the
papers hardly think it worth while chronicling
their deaths when they are run over by omnibuses.
S. Aus. Assembly will re-elect Speaker
Jenkin Coles as a matter of course —it has been
doing it as a matter of course for nearly 15 years
now. There have only been five Speakers m S.
Aus. altogether. C. C. Kingston’s father sat in
the chair for 18 years ; his longest term was
years, and Coles will only have to stay there till
the beginning of next year to beat that record.
Coles used to be a police-trooper, and 20 years ago
he was a member of the famous 36ft. Ministry. It
was a hardy Ministry as well as a long one, for the
only member of it who isn’t still alive and in poli-
tics was John Colton, the Premier. Kingston
was Attorney-General, Senate President Baker
Minister of Education, and Senator Tom Playford
Commissioner of Works; and the Treasurer, Ben
Rounsevell, won a State seat the other day.
A Vic. pressman on Bent:—
It would appear that Bent of Victoria is anxious to out-
do George Reid for pride of place as Australia’s champion
heavy-weight humorist. Bent’s oratory is of the playful
sort that suggests a Clydesdale carthorse in its lighter
moments, and its grotesqueness always excited merri-
ment but of late Thomas has gathered in the flattering
conviction that it is his wit that fills the halls with mad
law-liter, and he laboriously spreads himself to be more
so. ° The' resulting exhibition must be painful to Bent’s
friends, if he really has any ; it is certainly meat and
drink to his enemies. As a humorist, Bent travels on his
stomach even to a larger extent than G. H. Reid. He is
always thrusting it forward, and dragging it into conver-
sation, and his pride in it is obvious, although it is really
something in the nature of an ailment. For the rest, his
idea of humor is a coarse remark about intestines and
bears, delivered in a coarse voice, but with such a
sprightlj air on the speaker’s part of having said a neat
thin"- that the audience always laughs long and loud.
There is some real danger of Bent retaining a position in
public favor, as Reid has, by reason of being an “ amoosin’
cuss ” Australians love to have their politics relieved
with comic spots, and they like the spots to be large-
something in the nature of grease spots, in fact.
Apropos “General” Booth.
Judge Dowling, of more or less
humorous memory, presiding at
a Court of Quarter Sessions —
which are never held quarterly—
at Parramatta (N.S.W.), was once
presented with a pair of white
gloves, there being no cases on the
list. He inquired to what the clean
record was attributable, and the
Crown Prosecutor ascribed it to the
Salvation Army. “I don’t see that
the Army has made much improve-
ment in the Crown Prosecutor, at all
events,” said Dowling'. It lias never
been decided whether this was a brilliant
joke or not. The Crown Prosecutor
thought it over all the rest of his life,
and his executors are still considering
it.
From a Vic. up-country correspondent :
An old bachelor named George Stephenson, of an
appropriately mechanical turn of mind, who a few months
ago was arrested at Heyfield, Gippsland (Vic.), on a
charge of lunacy, because he refused to eat anything,
died lately in Kew Asylum. He left £441 ; and in his old
district they tell with a wink that he got some of it for
making the armor of bushranger Ned Kelly. Ned Kelly's
mother, brother, and married sister and her children still
live at Greta, in the north-east of Vic. Kate Kelly has
been dead seven or eight years; she married, and one of
her children goes to the local school.
“The Mussel” writes about a man of
girth :
Every public man has to suffer the penalty of great-
ness—the penalty being the irresponsible criticism of
minds nurtured in a very narrow cradle. For example,
take the case of Thomas Bent, Premier of Victoria.
Tommy has never addressed a hostile meeting yet where
he has not been anxiously cross-examined as to the where-
abouts of a certain missing mace, lie has been ques-
tioned re this implement so often that he must almost
believe himself that there is something in the story,,
though, as a matter of fact, a mace would be no earthly
good to plant vegetables with. The other day I overheard
a man reading aloud the newspaper account of the
Waranga murder (the case where a man was chopped up,
placed in two sacks, and thrown into an irrigation
channel). He remarked: “This looks like Bent’s work.
I wouldn’t put it past him. He was up that way a little-
while ago.” But I think I reached the climax of calumny
the other day in regard to the said T.B. It was the usual
bar-room discussion re Japs and Russians. One man said :
the Russian strike was not nearly' such a serious matter
as the Victorian maritime strike. “Look what
Bent did,” said he; “he said ‘fire low and
lay’em out.’” The other man said it was Tom Price.
Then the trouble started. The disputants were
forcibly ejected into the dimly-lit right-of-way, and for
some minutes the air was over-charged with muffled
curses, gloomy, whirling figures, and bits of human
meat. After the affair was over, the conqueror and the
vanquished drank amicably together, the vanquished
apologising for supposing for one instant that it was Tom
Price who gave that famous order. Bent came out very
badly in this “discussion.”
N.S.W. Lands Minister Ashton, speaking
at Gorowa, stated that he had been pondering
deeply of late over some of the difficulties attend-
ing closer settlement, with the result that “he
had evolved a scheme whereby the project could
be promoted on a satisfactory basis without the
Government taking an active part in it.” His
novel idea was that “the people should deal
directly with owners of large estates, and the
Government would find the capital and become
the mortgagors.” Ashton really didn’t seem to
know that his brilliant scheme already existed as
a provision of the Victorian Act, which empowers
the Closer Settlement Board to finance private
purchasers where it is satisfied that transactions
are bona fide.

Aboriginalities.
Ballarat is troubled about an ancient of
82, named John Alpin, a mining pioneer of the
early ’so’s. Recently the police found him camped
dangerously close to a flooded creek near Black
Hill, East Ballarat, and advised him to move. He
explained that he had come from the Colac dis-
trict to search for several bags of gold left behind
at that place by diggers in the early days, and de-
clined to shift. Later on some of his friends
arrived from Beech Forest, and forced him
to go home with them, but in a few days he
was back again at his camp by the creek, which
was still flooded and liable to engulf him at any
time, and when the police tried to coax him to go
home, and stay there, he ended the discussion
cheerfully but firmly: “Why shouldn’t I be al-
lowed to work if I want to ? There’s years of work
in me yet, and I want that ton of gold.”

“C.X.G.”: There died at Innamincka
Station (S.A.), some two months ago, an old
nigger named Pompey, who must have been nearly
a hundred years old ; also a gin who was reckoned
by the natives old enough to have been Pompey’s
mother. I reckon she was all of 120 years. Mr.
Walker, the manager of the station, has an intelli-
gent nigger now between 50 and 60 years old.
This fellow was a lad of 20 at the time King (of
the Burke and Wills party) was camped at Tnna-
minclca, and he says Pompey used to carry King
about when the latter was too feeble to walk.
According to him, Pompey was then a “ flour-
bag”—i.e., white-headed. Now niggers do not go
properly “flour-bag” until between 50 and 60
years old. King was found in 1863 ; that’s 42
years ago, so that nigger must have been close on
the century, and the female considerably over it.
A sure sign of extreme old age with the nig., is
that his teeth get worn down almost level with
the gums through eating nardoo and ether rough
grain foods.

“Barchain”: “ Falder ” (B. l/6/’O5) is
wrong in saying that one of Tyson’s bullock-
teams drew 22 tons at Narrabri. The heaviest
load drawn by Tyson’s teams was 10 tons. But
Terbutt’s drew 18 tons 2 cwt. from Glenroy to
Narrabri, 40 miles, with 22 bullocks (102 bales
greasy wool); Rolling’s drew over 17 tons 18cwt.
from Burren to Narrabri, 70 miles, with 18 horses
(101 bales greasy). These weights are nett, and
are the heaviest loads that ever came to Narrabri.
The biggest loads drawn to Narrabri were 107, 110
and 115 bales of scoured undumped wool, all from
120 and 140 miles out. They were drawn by
Lambkin, with a horse team, the weight of the 115
bales being only 12 tons 12 cwt nett. These
loads were all drawn over heavy black-soil plains.

“Janor”: He divining-rod. Some years
ago mate and self had done much futile loaming
and trenching for the reef at the head of a likely
gully, in which some nice slugs had been got.
The quest had us fairly beaten, when my mate (a
superstitious Cornishman) tramped in to the
nearest mining camp to bring out a local rod-
expert. I was sceptical, and, to give the diviner
a show, I planted three sovereigns in different
parts of the surface. The rod-man cut a forked
twig and, holding it in the orthodox manner,
went marching around. I took care that he
passed over the hidden coins, but the rod gave no
sign. He pronounced the ground not auriferous,
pocketed his fee, and departed. Mate wanted to
break camp and try elsewhere, but when I
showed him the sovs. lie thought he’d interview
the seer instead. He did, and it cost him a fiver
next court-day for assault. We gave the gully
another trial, and inside a month found a reef
carrying good gold in the area that had been
rodded.
» * * *
“ Ratatat ” : During the drought there
was much talk of the “silent bush ” out west, but
there is no silence now. Birds of every variety
abound, and in parts of the Barcoo galahs are
causing trouble by perching in flocks of thousands
on the telegraph wires, and bearing them down in
places to within nine feet of the ground. The
birds don’t merely perch ; they swing, hang head
downwards, and cut all sorts of capers, causing
crosses ” and other disarrangements. Two wires
out of four on the same poles have been useless for
some time, and the Department is puzzled to find
a remedy for this state of things. The experiment
is to be tried of setting the wires on the same cross-
arm for a distance of nine miles, but already the
trouble has shown in other places, so that galah
parrots may cause a radical change in the method
of building telegraph lines in the backblocks.
Fred Kinred, of Tumut (N.S.W.), is
voyaging by boat down the Murrumbidgee and
the Murray to South Australia, starting from
Tumut. The trip will cover 1900 miles, and if he
gets through it will show that there is sometimes
at least a trickle of water for that distance in Aus-
tralia.
* * * *
“ C.C.H.”: This happened when I was
putting in a few days with my old friend, Jim
Bradley, who keeps a wayside pub. The first
night I was there Jim and I talked over old times
and skited a bit, no doubt; also we
sampled some of Jim’s whisky. Next
morning ! awoke with a head that
was trying to jump four ways at
once. I only mention this that it
may be understood that Jim’s whisky
was none of your milk-and-water
stuff. About 10 o’clock, when my
head was beginning to ease up a bit,
a waggon attached to a team of bul-
locks, their driver, and a man on
horseback pulled up in front of Jim’s.
They came in (the bullock-driver and
horseman), and immediately got to
work on whisky. They drank pretty
consistently till about noon, then went
out, drove round to a small paddock
at the rear of Jim’s, and unyoked.
They came back and settled down
again to whisky. As the afternoon
wore ©n they began to eye each other
suspiciously. At last Tom (that was
the horseman) said Joe (the bullocky)
was drunk. Then Joe bet Tom that
he was good enough to yoke up his
bullocks and drive to Barker’s that
night. _ Barker’s is another bush pub,
four miles further on the road—a road
constantly intersected by old roads
made by the early splitters. Tom took
the bet up. Joe went away, a trifle
unsteadily, but he had the bullocks
yoked and standing in front of the
door in half-an-hour. He came into
the bar, where Tom had sunk quietly
to sleep. Joe had another whisky,
picked Tom up, and dropped him in the
bottom of the waggon, tied Toni’s horse
on behind, and started for Barker’s.
About an hour after dark a waggon stopped at the
front door, and a voice we knew came in to us :
“Get up, you drunken swine! Here’s Barker’s!
I’m dry—dry as ’ell with a north wind blowin’—
and that quid’s got ter be changed pretty quick ! ”
I am not going to try and describe the look of dis-
gust on Joe’s face when Jim and I fell out of the
door—we had been having some more of Jim’s
whisky—and he recognised us.

“Civ” : Can any Bulletin reader “place ”
herewith-described bird, which was caught in a
rabbit-trap near Chiltern (Vic.)? Side view like
eaglehawlc, both in shape and color, but front-face
like an owl’s, with a covering of brown, hair-like
feathers and a thin line of black leathers encircling
it. Breast-feathers, yellow, with black spots;
back, dark brown and black. Talons like hawk.
About 12in. high and 3ft. across extended wings.
Lives on rabbits and parrots, which it tears to
pieces and devours greedily. Don’t break out
into any frivolity and suggest that the thing is a
cross between an emu and a money-lender, for it
isn’t.
* * * *
“Helen”: I once saw a strange, com-
pound-looking object on the limb of a tree, and
on inspection found it to be a jackass with half
the body and both legs of a half-grown minah
sticking out of his mouth. He seemed to be in
difficulties and was breathing heavily. For two
hours he remained in the tree and wrestled with
the too-vast orb of his dinner, and at the end of
that time the minah’s legs were still visible. In
about another hour Jack began to laugh in an apo-
plectic voice; he had swallowed the legs and feet
and was trying to brighten up.
A THING THAT MIGHT HAVE BEEN
SAID DIFFERENTLY.
HE : “Whisky and soda, please.”
SHE: “ What whisky would you like?”
HE: “I think I'd like to try some of your bulk.”
“San Remo”: Should like some Bul-
letin reader to place our venomous snakes in
their order of deadliness. Common bush belief
gives the black snake next place to the death-
adder. Yet the brown snake is deadlier than the
black, and the “ tiger,” in my opinion, ranks next
to the adder. Again, does any Bullet n reader
know of an adult dying from Australian snake-
bite within an hour ? I have witnessed three adult
deaths from bite. One (adder) in Ihr. 30min. ;
another (brown snake) jin three hours ; the third
(black snake) in about five hours. The two litter
cases wouldn’t have been fatal if proper treatment
had been available. Marvellous is the ignorance
on this point that still exists in the bush. To fill
the patient with whisky and run him up and down
is about all that is ever thought of.

Billy Murphy was a little sweet on Kitty
Caiey ; so when Mrs. Casey came upon him lean-
iug over the yard rails, watching Kitty
milk, and cordially invited him in to tea,
he gladly accepted. At tea the Casey
family sat around the table eating nothing,
but watching carefully as Mrs. Casey
pressed scones on Billy. “ Ate a bit, Billy ;
do ate a bit,” she said. Billy felt shy
at eating alone, but could not refuse the
hospitality. Presently Mrs. Casey in-
quired earnestly: “Don’t ye feel bad,
Billy ? ” “ No,” was the wondering reply.
“Then it must a bin crame o’ tarther,”
she said ; and the family looked visibly
relieved. Then Kitty explained. “We
got the arsenic and crame o’ tarther tins
mixed,” she said ; “ we didn’t know which
one we used for ther scones” “So
we arsked yer ter tea, Billy,” said a
younger Casey. “It muster bin the
crame o’ tarther,” added Kitty, with satis-
faction. “I ’ope ter Cord it wuz,” cried
Billy, as he bolted home for an emetic,
while the Caseys with the force of a burst-
ing dam started into the scones.

Sydney Centennial Park has a col-
lection of hybrids from the wild black
duck and several species of domestic duck.
Noticeable features of the cross are that
the hybrids lose the buff head-marks of
their wild parent, and have yellowish
red feet; and the males have the curly
tail feathers of the house duck. Recently
at a Riverina show hybrids of common
goose and black swan were shown.
Extract from a horse-stealing case in a
N.S.W. district that is very proud of having sent
soldiers to assist Tommy Atkins in burning the
homes of the “hignorant Boers”
John Farrell, residing at Goba Creek, on Dunlop sta-
tion, near Burrovva, deposed that the mare outside the
court was his property ; he lost her three years ago from
the Burrowa common ; some time afterwards he received
the mare from the police.
To his Honor : He did not know when he lost the mare ;
he did not know what year this was, or how many months
there were in a year, or the names of the months, or when
January was ; he knew that he lost the mare in the sum-
mer j he thought that the mare was lost about a month ;
he did not know how long a month was.
“The Prooshan”: Know an
old prospector who once owned
a pup that went strong on
novelties in the way of vic-
tuals and drink. One night
after work, a few ounces of
quicksilver were lying un-
der the table, in the pro-
spectingdish, with a little
water. Tobias came in
thirsty and made for the
dish, and, after lapping
up the water, lie tried
to polish off the mer-
cury —with poor re-
sults, of course. Re-
tiring a step, the pup
cocked his eye on one
side, in the manner
of Mark Twain’s
blue jay, and, after
quizzing the silver
for fully a minute,
hetackled it again,
lapping harder
than ever. This
went on for a
quarter of an
hour, when the
pup found his
tongue worn
out and began
to whine, and
finally to bark
at the dish.
till it was put
out of his
reach. About
a week later
wash - up oc-
curred ; and
the diggers
panned off the
rough, bringing home
about an ounce and a half
of gold in alittle black sand.
This was put under the bunk in the dish.
While the men were out of the hut, that pup
came in and scoffed the gold. A council of war
decided on certain strong medicines, which were
administered in large doses, and Toby was tied
up for assay purposes. Next day they gave
Tobias to the baker, and the camp knew him no
more. It hopes to continue that way.

“ Ramotli Gilead”: The yarn which
“Wethos” (B. l/G/’Oo) tells about drovers using
the hike to follow their flocks has been defunct
and buried this good while. “ Wethos ” must be
a body-snatcher. In the outer-back, where, if it
does rain at all, it rains every other day, it would
be impossible for anyone to ride a bike after stock ;
not to mention the details of getting through grass,
from 3ft. to Bft. high, over uneven ground and
across creeks where there are no bridges, etc.
And fancy scurrying on a bike, on a pitch-dark
night, through a belar scrub, too thick to hear a
dog bark in, over to Maloney’s to report; or
running down into a bend thick with dead timber,
to explore for a camp. The only time when bikes
were employed at all by drovers (and then very
little) was during the recent severe drought, when
there wasn’t a blade of grass for a thousand miles,
and all the gullies and hills had blown away.

THE OPTIMIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When things go wrong, don’t swear an’ cuss,
An’ rush about an’ make a fuss —
Creation doesn’t end with us.
Don’t flop straight down an’ start to pray,
Because yer luck is out to-day ;
come your way.
There’s other folk as well as you
’As got ter git their livin’ too,
An’ God ’as got enough to do.
Perhaps you feel a trifle sore
At missin’ what you’ve grafted for—
You’ll get what you deserve—p’r’aps more !
Although you’d like to run the show,
There’s lots o’ things as you don't know,
You’ll find out when you go below.
I’ve ’eard a lot of people curse
This ’ere old-fashioned universe ;
But it might be a darned sight worse.
vie.

“Paul Cupid”: About snake-charming.
Once, when a boy, I saw a black snake crawl
under the slab flooring of a shed. An old man
was working for us—a man whom some evil genius
had prompted to play the concertina. I asked
him to come and shift some slabs. “Don’t want
ter shift no slabs t’ get ’itn,” he said. “ Bring me
th’ concertiner, an’ I’ll fetch him out quick. You
get a stick an’ look out on th’ side what I’m
playin’at.” I didn’t think the snake would be
foolish enough to come out, for the aged incubus
was a barbarous player, and I had often seen our
cat spring up from the fire and rush out with its
tail bristling like the Emperor William’s mous-
tache when he started. Ha always used to shut
his eyes when tearing shrieks out of the instru-
ment. He sat on a box on the slabs, closed his
eyes, as usual, and started “The Rocky Road to
Dublin.” I went to the opposite end of the shed
and expected every moment to see the snake fly
for its life. The music attracted the dog. He
came up, sat on his tail behind the player, and,
pointing his nose to the skv, howled like a lost
cornet in Tophet. Then I saw about two feet of
the snake come up through a crack, between the
old man’s legs. He opened his eyes just then and
also saw the snake, and promptly took a back
somersault on top of the dog. The dog limped
away yelping ; the ancient put his foot through
the concertina as he scrambled up, and the con-
certina yelped. Then he cursed fluently at me for
not standing where he told me. In the general
confusion the snake left.

“ Vulcan ” : The public should jump hard
ontheN.S.W. Land Commission’s sliding scale
of allowances to witnesses. Value of a witness,
as a witness, doesn’t lie in the position he : holds or
the work he performs, but in what he has to say
on the matter in hand ; and in giving members of
the professions a guinea per day (with an extra ss.
to a guinea per day for country witnesses), whilst
it-runs down the scale to from ss. to 7s. 6d. (with
from 2s. to 3s. extra for country witnesses) for
artisans, &c., the authorities show a touching re-
gard for the top-hat and boiled-shirt institution.
Fees of from 2s. Gd. to 10s. 6d. (with 2s. to 6s.
for country witnesses) for women, “according to
station in life,” are a still worse disgrace. Mrs.
Pure Merino, earning nothing at home, loses
nothing by being absent, but Mrs. Cocky does a
man’s and woman’s work combined during her
day, and her absence costs the selection so much
extra.
“S.R.”: “Chip” (B. 25/5/’O5) is*riglit
about the old blunderbuss. It was a terrific
weapon to kill anybody with. The special bullet
used in the Snider by the British troops against
the Zulus was hollow. When it struck an object
it spread out. It made a ghastly wound. I
bought an old Snider and 500 cartridges once.
Spending my holidays on a friend’s selection, I
assisted his son (an expert kangaroo shooter and
skinner) at a week’s ’roo shooting. I was a pretty
good shot and ran his bag pretty close. But the
language he used when he examined some of the
’roes was terrific. Asked me if I couldn’t fetch a
blooming battery while I was about it. Soon
after I joined a rifle club. I did much good shoot-
ing with my Martini-Enfield. With afile I snicked
a cross well into the top of each bullet. This made
it a decent dum-dum ; its hitting power was then
nearly equal to a '44 Winchester. As a handy
sporting rifle the Winchester is the daddy of all
weapons.

“Cronje”: Saw yesterday a string of
caterpillars about a foot long steering in a north-
easterly direction, as if making for a Government
farm. They were peculiarly marked, with two
long spikes sticking out of the
sides of the head, like horns, and a
row of black spots right down the
back to the tail. I thought to try
an experiment, so I killed the
leader. The next in command
took the lead and on they went
like a great Chinese procession.
Then I killed that self-elected
leader. The whole army swung
round till they came to the second
last one, and they gathered round
him or her—all the while emitting
a hissing sound, as if in consul-
tation. After a while they moved
back to places. Then one grisly
fellow took up the white grub’s
burden, but he seemed a canny
general and one capable of taking
in a situation, for he sent out
two scouts on either side to clear
the way. Then the procession
humped its back and pushed on.
Has any Bulletin reader noted this species ?

“D.P.” : carcase-dressing (Bulletin,
5/25/’O5). A carcase that is being dressed for
freezing passes through several hands, and is sawn
from tail-joint to neck (crosscut with two men),
which makes it impossible for one man to complete
the task in the time mentioned. Eight minutes
would be nearer the mark, even in the case of a
small ox. Still, some phenomenal slaughtering
records have been established—in the public-houses
on pay-Saturdays.
**• * *
“Tuna”: “ Icorner ” (B. 23/2/’O5) is
right, “ T.P.” notwithstanding. Only where he
comes much in contact with tourists and travellers
does the Maori to some extent follow civilised
usages. Elsewhere you have the same old thing—
Maori oven, fleas, dirt, and sitting on the ground
to eat. “ T.P.” is correct about the greenstone
deposit in the South Island. Owing to the
natives’ crude way of treating the large pieces,
much is wasted. They build a huge fire round
the block of stone, and, after 48 hours, deluge it
with water; result, a host of small pieces full of
flaws.
“ F.C.” on Bunny as a livelihood :
Rabbit-trapping' isn’t all beer and skittle3—or even
plain beer—but hundreds of those unemployed who hang
round the cities, chasing the elusive job, might do worse
than take it up for a while. It doesn’t cost a great deal
to start at the game. You must have a mate, better two,
and better still if four go together. There is no fun in
setting traps all day, carrying bunnies a mile or more to
the camp, and coming home cold and tired, and perhaps
Wet, to cook yourself a meal. Mostly, then, you don’t
cook it; you take a snack of something cold and turn in—
and that isn’t good. If there are four of you, you want
four dozen trap 3 (Tonks’) for each of the work-
ing three you may manage five dozen after a
time. Tents, of course, blankets, etc., also an
axe, a tomahawk and three “ setters ” —carpenters’
adzes will do. If you can get three fairish rnokes
do so ; they will hump the traps for you when shifting
ground—perhaps a mile, perhaps five. And you must
have one decent horse, and a light, two-wheeled cart to
take the bunnies, strung in couples on sticks across the
cart, to the railway. To find where to go—thickly-rab-
bited ground—write to anybody you know. If you know
nobody, write (prepaying reply) to a station-master or
any other railway man ; they are obliging, and, besides
knowing if bunnies are thick, they know if anybody else
is trapping in their neighborhood. Get where rabbits are
thickest and as neat as you can to a railway station—
never mind about townships and stores. Yoii take a fort-
night’s or a'month’s supply with you, and when that runs
low you can send a P. 0.0., or one of your rabbit cheques,
to any city wholesale grocer, and he’ll send all you want
to your railway station. But don’t write to any farmer
to find if bunnies pervade the landscape. His ideas and
yours as to the frequency of rabbits are certain to be
totally at variance. If he has ten acres of growing crop
and has seen one rabbit, he is morally certain to tell you
they are as thick as thieves. He will be most pleased to
see you—but he won’t give you grass for your horses.
About horse-feed, you must do the best you can. If there
is any grass about at all—and there won’t be many bunnies
if there isn’t—your one “ carting ” horse will keep himself
in condition most of the year on the roadside. If there
is open land anywhere, you are all right; if there isn’t,
you may have to pay—if you have three prads, most likely
Gd. a week per head. Get near a creek—but don’t get on
it. Get on a slope, halfway down to the creek. Out
gutters round your tents; make “flies” of bags ; and
clean your rabbits on the creek and below your camp (this
will attract fish if the creek boasts any).
Before you start, having- noticed your nearest station,
go to the Fish-market and interview one or two auc-
tioneers to see if they have any rabbit-boxes up that way.
I f they haven’t, get them to send up half-a-dozen. If you
want more, send a note down, they’ll send as many as you
can fill. Also, you’ll get your cheque a day or two after
the bunnies reach the city. You’ve got to trust these
rabbit-auctioneers —they are possibly as great rogues as
the butter-agents, but it can’t be helped. On many
stations export-agents meet the trains regularly and buy
all bunnies offering—paying cash. I don’t suppose they
are any more honest or philanthropic than the “auc-
tioneers.”
When I went trapping, 12 years ago, I knew absolutely
nothing about the game, and my mate but little. He
went to fill iii an unemployed winter, I to regain my
health—l couldn't walk a mile without resting—so we
didn’t consider the thickness of bunnies when we chose
the locality. There was no export trade in bunnies then,
the demand being consequently very restricted, yet we
lived well, had books, papers, and tobacco, kept our-
selves in clothes and boots (the heaviest item), and
cleared about 10s. a week apiece with rabbits averaging
.‘lid. a pair. With experience, something approaching
heavily-rabbited ground and the exporters offering Gd. a
pair, a man should clear, above expenses, from 255. to
355. a week.
A few memos.:—Don’t get second-hand traps. •' Get the
best even if you have to pay 10s. a dozen for them, though
you ought, to get them for 7s. Gd. Take some quinine
with you in case you get wet and chilled. If none of you
can set a trap, .you’ll have to get some farmer’s son to
show you. Best time to start is March, but any time is
good ; you’ll have a job from December to February
keeping" the bunnies from flies, but your own savvy and
the experience of others will suggest what is best to do.
Also in the spring' the young bunnies are a nuisance, but
that can’t be helped—there is no rabbit-trap known that
differentiates between full-grown bunnies and kittens,
and, anyhow, young rabbits, just reaching maturity, are
the best eating, and bring as good a price as big- bucks.
Always go round your traps morning and evening—early
evening, too, if bunnies are thick—and re-set every trap
sprung; and don’t send rabbits away the day they are
killed unless the weather is very cold. Let them hang
over night to stiffen, then they’ll reach the market or the
freezing-chambers stiff. If they don’t get a chance to
stiffen they’ll sweat, and be condemned, and then you’ll
be the freight out of pocket. Don’t waste or destroy any
skins : the hat factories will take all you can bring them.
And if you don’t trap more than a dozen bunnies the first
week don’t lose heart. Also keep shifting your traps.

John C.: (1) What would be the use of passing
a law that no agricultural machinery may be imported
when the same effect can be got by a sufficiently heavy
tariff? (2) No, your economics are wrong. If an article
costs £25. to be made abroad, and a duty of £25 meets it
in Australia, it by no means follows that a similar article
locally-made will cost £5O. It might, indeed, follow that
it would cost less than £25, because the local manufac-
turer, assured of his market and able to gauge exactly its
extent, could produce with great economy. He might,
to give a supposititious case which could be matched with
many actual experiences, produce axes at 3s. if the
local tariff so protected his home market as to give
him a certainty of demand for 50,000 axes a year,
whilst he couldn’t possibly compete, even at a
higher price, with foreign competition, which would
only allow him to sell 20,000 axes in the year....

“ D’Harum ” writes :
lie “ Exile” on water-finding by means of a bent piece
of wire. The old gentleman who showed him the device
was not its originator ; it has been known to many people
for a very long time, I imagine. However, I have never
seen it done with bent copper wire; I have used, and
have seen other people do likewise, a prong from a live
tree with the sap still fresh in ,it. Otherwise the system
of water-finding I know corresponds with “Exile’s.”
Does anybody know a gobd whisky-finding device ?

“Xerxes” : Re native cats (“Jab.,”B.
11/5/’O5). There are large cats (Dasyures) and a
small kind. The large ones, generally called
“ tiger-catS,” will eat the whole of the fowl they
kill; but the smaller species only makes a punc-
ture near the head and sucks the blood. The latter
kind are of two colors—white-spotted grey (like
the tiger-cat), and white-spotted black. Are these
distinct species ? [Both the larger and the smaller
n iti ve-cats are Dasyures (D. maoulatus and I),
viverrirms), and the color-difference in the smaller
kind is merely one of variety, young ones of both
colors being often found, in the same litter.]

POLITICAL
POINTS
The alternative to Arbitration : “ Gatling
machine guns have been issued to the Chicago
militia for use in the event of serious strike riots.”
After Reid had been barracking against
Socialism for an hour at Newtown (Sydney), the
other night, an agonised voice rang out above the
din, “ George, say something ! ”
A Jap interrupted some derogatory re-
marks on his nation at a Sydney hotel dining
table the other day by blandly stating that in ten
years a Jap would be Chief-Justice of Austral:*.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers, who says
that he could induce 100 men to settle on the land
with four addresses, would be better employed in
reckoning how many he shut out from agricul-
tural settlement by the improvement lease provi-
sions in his Land Act.
W. E. Johnson, M.H.R. (N.S.W.), who
says that “Socialistic ideas are incompatible with
our religious beliefs and our family life, ” signed,
in 1894, the most solemn Labor League pledge
ever devised, and bound himself to uphold an
absolutely Socialistic platform.
Watson got on to the “point” of the
“ black ” planter when he said at Cairns, “ There
has been no concerted effort made to bring white
laborers to the North.” If the planters had done
as much to secure white labor as they did to get
the nigger, the difficulty would have been over-
come ere this.
The white population of Salmon River
Valley (British Columbia) has decided to resist by
force the introduction of Japanese labor. The
Canadian Government insists, for “Imperial
reasons,” on allowing the free entry of Japanese
into the Dominion, and it seems as though a civil
war would be the harvest of that decision.
Premier Carruthers, the Reform hero,
whose salvation ticket included Reduction of
Ministers, not only leads Moo Brunker (and his
halo) into the Ministry, thereby raising that body
to about the highest number on record, but invests
with Ministerial power a man his constituents
rejected at the polls. All in the name of the
People’s Party.
THINGS AREN’T ALWAYS WHAT THEY
SAY THEY ARE.
Bent, the Blower, seems to have done everything from
acting to cabbage-gardening: but in his Empire speech
he said he had never been to England.
LITTLE BOY FROM BRIGHTON: “ Hang it, he ain’t
the tin god I thought he teas, after all.”
The frenzied statements that the Labor
party is seeking to uproot religion and obliterate
the marriage tie, are so sedulously circulated that
a prominent Anglican (Sydney) clergyman, with
more millinery than sense, has taken it upon him-
self to write to the papers suggesting that the
church should expel all Sunday-school teachers
who are avowed Laborites.
Apropos the Sydney daily papers’ horror
at the interruptions during William M‘Linen’s
recent anti-Socialistic meeting, what about the
same papers’ circular a while ago to the news-
agents, when the Laborites wanted to establish a
new paper ? Under pain of boycott these virtuous
daily papers ordered the agents not to give
Labor even a noisy hearing—they weren’t to let
it have any hearing at all.
That old and trusty friend of the Tory
Sress, “High Authority,” has been stating up
Forth that the Commonwealth Act for Tommy
Tanna’s importation is illegal, insofar that it
infringes on the common law rights of the indi-
vidual, of whatever color, to reside in any part of
the Empire, once he is there. But the Privy
Council tried its venerable front tooth on that
same question long ago, and the Chinaman who
had got into Victoria was thrown out again
on his pigtail, and fell heavily in the exterior
saying “ Whaffor?”
Our friend Jonathan,
from the United States, has
issued a bitter complaint
about “Australian competi-
tion in the Philippines,”
where this country does
about twopenn’orth of trade
per annum—the trade, in
fact, has declined since 1901
—and he wants this com-
merce stopped. It is diffi-
cult to please Jonathan.
He sells us about £5,000,000
worth of goods per annum,
and takes about £2,500,0 0
worth, and demands the dif-
ference in cash. He puts
45 per cent, duty on the
Australian agricultural im-
plement and charges this
duty on the real value ; we
let in most of his agricultu-
ral implements free and
charge the rest 12| per cent,
and allow him to fix the
value that the 12£ per cent,
shall be charged on, and he
fixes it so low that our duty
on Jonathan’s agricultural
implement pans out at about
5 per cent. He has annexed
this implement business
from us, and has taken our
tobacco trade, and is reach-
ing out for other things.
And when this mild, quiet country tries to sell a
load of wool or a chunk of wood to his colony,
Jonathan rages over the landscape like a grizzly
b’ar painted red.
Lascar stokers in the Orient mail service
have been replaced by members of the Federated
Seamen’s Union of Britain—one of the rules of
which is that every member must be a British
subject. The Labor party really seems to be
about the only effective friend that the Boy of the
Bulldog Breed has. He gets a lot of inhar-
monious singing from the Tory person ; but it’s
only the Radical who troubles to get him a job.
“ Until they could change human
nature,” said Joseph Carruthers, scraping his
mild little second-fiddle to G. H. Reid’s lead,
“socialism would be an impossibility, an imprac-
tibility.” Then Joseph went to a socialistic
telephone and ordered supper for 11.45, and
stepped over a socialistic gutter and got into
a socialistic tram, and rode home on a socialistic
train. And the telephone and the gutter and the
tram and the train all winked at Joseph violently.
There is some doubt as to whether the
evidence given before N.S.W. Lands Commission
has the same right to publication as evidence in a
law court, and someone seems to have drawn the
attention of the press to this with a club. For a
while the Sydney Telegraph gleefully printed
the Lands Commission teg-* , '’i2B»y in full. Then
CamlTa 'cold, cold day when it merely said
this: —
The witness then made a series of charges against a
certain politician.
Closer settlement in Victoria under Bent’s
paternal direction is a slow-going reform, anyhow.
Thusfar, nobody has been settled. A first estate
of 23,000 acres, purchased early in March, is ex-
pected to be ready for selection by the end of
August, and meanwhile the applicants for farms
don’t appear deeply impressed by the prospect of
what they are likely to get. On the other hand,
the vendors of the estates are praising and magni-
fying Bent’s name. Their settlement was highly
satisfactory.
Female card-box makers’ union in Sydney
has died while waiting for the dormant Arbitration
tribunal to hear its appeal. Sydney Labor Council
has been informed that employers promptly
reverted to the old sweating conditions, which had
been slightly ameliorated by the formation of the
union. Once more girls earning 3s. to 7s. weekly
are subjected to heavy fines for minor offences.
To be 15 minutes late in arrival now costs the same
old shilling penalty—from one to two days’ pay
gone in one vile swoop.
Sydney Telegraph, in its report of Half-
Premier Reid’s Queensland tour, told how the fat
gentleman was banqueted at Childers, in the
sugar region, and one of the toasts put down on
the list was “Success to the Sugar Industry.”
But the girthy politician got his eye on this pro-
posal to wish good-luck to an Australian industry,
and demanded the removal of the toast from the
list, and, as the Telegraph proudly expressed it,
“the scheme was foiled”—just as if the sugar
industry was the miscreant in the melodrama.
Stated by John Blackman, representative
of the Cooerwull Woollen Mills, before the Tariff
Commission at Sydney (7/6/’O5), that whenever an
article was produced by Australian manufacturers
patterns were forwarded immediately by retailors
to English buyers, whereupon copies came out
made of inferior stuff, which swamped the market.
He declared himself to be a Freetrader, but he
asked for increased duties nevertheless, because
under Freetrade the mills were gradually closing
up. The same old Freetrader !
That down-trodden person Premier Bent
has been ill-used again. At Bendigo, the reporters
thought they heard Bent make a long and violent
attack on the Victorian Labor members, in which
attack he said, among other things, that they
spent half their time in Parliament playing bil-
liards, and neglected their duties. Bent’s after
explanation that he didn’t even allude to the pre-
sent set of Labor members was a pretty loud dis-
claimer. A man may repudiate a sentence, but it
isn’t every man who has the cheek to repudiate
half a column or so.
Victorian Premier Bent’s latest notion
is to sell the 3G3 acres comprising the big Kew
lunatic asylum grounds, and with the proceeds
build an up-to-date asylum elsewhere. But he
seems to have forgotten that this land is virtually
sold already. The late unlamented Gillies long
ago reckoned he could get a million for it, so he
borrowed the million on the strength of his
expectations, and promptly splashed the coin
around. Now anything that can be got from the
sale or lease of the land should go to wipe up
Duncan’s splash.
A REVEREND ANAUCIIIST.
Rev. M‘Cann (Melbourne), at a Temperance Thanksgiving for closed
hotels, said if he were the Premier he would close all superfluous hotels at
once without compensation.
THE CONFIDCATIOR (savagely): “If I had my way I'd take your
blooming loaf” (snarl), “and you and your brats wouldn ’t
get a bite, see!” (Another snarl.)
SMALL VOICE (from rear): “Hold tight, dtddy!”
Thus the Age, Melbourne
Any Ministerial interposition would smack of Socialism,
and Socialism of every kind we know, is equivalent to
political damnation. This is the kind of clap-trap which
is preached from decade to decade in order ... to keep
the noses of the producers hard on the grindstone.
Old man Syme’s paper just now promises to be a
tower of strength to the cause of progress. Instead
of retrograding it grows more Radical as David
grows older.
“Two-Seas”: The number of questions
that are not put by the Lands Commission is
a noble tribute to the power of bluff and bluster
exhibited by some of the unwilling witnesses.
One question that should be put to all land agents
is : “ How many eases have you had during the
past three years in which your fee has been £lOO
or more, and particulars of same?” That “bill of
costs” in the Sims case shows how difficult it is
to total a genuine charge of £lOO, even when
assisted by such an item as “ Carrutbers £25.”
The usual deputation —heaven only knows
how often that deputation has turned up—lately
applied to the N.S.W. Government for work, and
quite a number have been put on at a railway de-
viation job. A railway deviation works out like
this
(1) A railway is built out of loans.
(2) It is decided that on one section of it a bad route
was selected, so a certain number of miles of line on a
better route are built out of loans.
(3) When the new line is finished the old one is torn up
loans.
(4) i\o aßefitpt is made to pay off any of these loans.
In Victoria, under the regime of Bent,
pointsmen occasionally have to work 18 hosrc '">h
of the 24. At a cabin where three men are em-
ployed, it sometimes happens that one falls sick ;
then the department stops the sick man’s pay and
generally makes the other two do his work with-
out extra remuneration. The same sort of economy
pervades the service, and occasionally the man
who has been on duty for 12 or 18 hours loses his
grip of things. There was a big smash the other
day, and when the bill comes in it will require a
good deal more economy of the Bent kind to make
up for it.
Thomas Bent, referring to the Butter Com-
mission’s report, says he has a Secret Commis-
sions Bill on the stocks which goes even further
than Lord Russell’s English Act, and that “it
was arranged at the Hobart Conference that I
should draft such a Bill and send copies to the
other Premiers with a view to joint action by the
Parliaments of the six States.” Bent’s idea of
progress is that his Bill should be discussed at
great length by 600 legislators in 12 different
Houses. Yet didn’t we federate so as to have one
Parliament to deal with matters that concern the
whole of the Commonwealth ?
The Westralian State Government, at the
prompting of the ’Groper fruit-growers, is making
mincemeat of one clause of the Constitution. On
the pretence of only blocking diseased fruit, it
makes it almost impossible to get in fruit grown
in other States ; and when it is admitted, the price
has to be put up enormously to make good the
loss through scores of cases having been destroyed,
with the result that the local Groper gets a huge
inter-State Protection never contemplated by the
Constitution. Chaffey Baker, President of the
Senate, who is interested in the S. Australian fruit
business, told Premier Daglish the other day that,
just to see what would happen, one case of apples
and one of pears was examined by two experts
before being shipped, every fruit was overhauled,
and nothing far., tly suspicious admitted. Yet
those two cases were condemned. And the humor
of the situation is that the Westralian fruit-area is
simply crawling with disease.
The results of the South Australian elec-
tions made the Melbourne Argus very angry;
in fact, that usually mild-tempered old lady
almost lost control of herself on the occasion,
and hit out with her dishcloth with a fury that
was rather surprising. The fact that the S.A.
Labor party got several M’s.P. from the neigh-
boring States and the Commonwealth Parliament
to help it was what chiefly raised the dander of
the Argus, and it got off remarks like these :
A contest anywhere to which he (the Labor member) is
invited is as welcome as pay-day. .
He (the unfortunate Labor member again) is almost
bursting with unexpended loquacity.
The States and Commonwealth pay the men (still tne
Labor members) liberal salaries and transport them to any
place where they may happen to be required, free of cost.
One effect of the practice of turning a horde of du e -
gaged politicians (this refers, of course, to Labor mem De l
into the field at every election will be to embitter the
feeling between the State and Federal Parliaments, which
is not too cordial at present.
They (Labor members, once more) employ tbemseßes
as peripatetic spouters and touters m party interest .
And all this waving of the enraged cotton umbre a
because the Argus’s friend, John Darling, was
seventh on the list in his constituency.
This moan appeared in S.M. Herald’s
leading article of June 7 :
William M‘Millan, at the Protestant Hall a week or so
ago, said that money was still leaving the country.
It is, William M‘Linen, it is. On a rough esti-
mate £2,800,000 a year is leaving N.S.W. alone to
pay interest on foreign public debt, and a million
or two a year to pay interest on foreign private
debt, and somewhere about £12,000,000 a year to
pay for foreign imported goods, most of which
could be locally produced. Mr. Importer M‘Linen
never spoke more truly than when he remarked
that money was still leaving the country.
The Tasmanian Collector of Customs states
that the increase in that State’s industries
which was reported in 1903 has stopped, and that
1904 was a year of slump. One clothing factory,
employing 20 hands, has closed; likewise three
boot factories (100 hands), three soap and candle
factories (35 hands), and two confectionery fac-
tories ; while there is depression in almost every
other industry, save the heavily-protected beer
factories. When this dismal report came out
Sydney Telegraph braced itself for a great effort,
and started its leading article thus :
A stronger argument for fiscal peace than any that has
been presented to the Tariff Commission for reviving the
tariff fight is contained in the reports from the Tasma-
nian and Westraiian Collectors of Customs on the effects
of the Federal tariff in their States.
The Japanese are said to have “ mentioned
their terms or some of them. They want the
Chinese town of Port Arthur, and the Chinese
territory of Liao-tung. They want Russia to
evacuate Manchuria, and as Japan demands pos-
session for itself of the Manchurian railway right
up to Harbin, it probably proposes to hold the
territory also—the railway without the territory it
runs through would be of little use. Also it
demands Korea, so apparently China is to get
back nothing of what it lost to Russia, and is also,
by way of a fresh robbery, to lose its shadowy
rights as suzerain of the Land of the Morning
Calm. After these things are fixed up, the Jap
wants Saghalien and an indemnity. If Russia
should acceed to these terms it will want money
badly, and presumably it will begin, by way of
raising the wind, to oollect the indemnity that
Turkey has owed it for the last 27 years. Then
there will be more trouble.
Vic. Premier Bent has made a stupid
attempt to excuse his action in insisting that
a printer in the Government office who happened
to be a member of the Political Labor Council
should either leave the Council or leave his billet.
“It would be a nice thing,” says the political
hobbledehoy from Brighton, “to have a member
of the Political Labor Council among the printers,
talking politics instead of doing his work.” But
when Bent howled that the man must either give
up his printing job or his connection with the
Political Labor Council, he didn’t accuse him of
proselytising in business hours. Bent came down,
on him simply and solely because he was a con-
spicuous Labor man. Kow that he has aban-
doned the P.L.C. he is allowed to remain in tLo
service, though his views haven’t changed, and he
is as likely as he was before to talk politics in
working hours. In fact, he is more likely to do it
now than he was before. If anything can confirm
him in his views, it is likely to be his recent
experience of the petty silly tyranny of a yelling
lout of the Bent class.
THE SOCIALISTIC CAT.
A cat can look at a king;
But, O, a cat of that size
Were quite a different thing
In the sight of Oeorgie's eyes.
State Premier Carruthers at Ryde
(N.S.W.), June?: —
He never said the Labor Party was a socialistic party.
He had read and studied Socialism but he could not give
the Labor Party credit for practising or even preaching it.
The only place where it would be practicable or possible
would be Heaven.
On this basis why not call the Anti-socialist
party “Anti-Christ” for short? Then comes
this item (Sydney Telegraph, June 10): —
In the course of an address on Socialism to a meeting of
the Women’s Liberal League at North Sydney the other
evening, Mrs. Osborne said that there were many different
kinds of Socialism, but they were all alike, inasmuch as
the object was to repress individuality and to increase the
power of the State. The lecturer quoted Milton’s line :
“ To disenthrone the King of Heaven, we war, if war we
must,” in support of the theory that Satan was the first
Socialist.
This is a fearsome discrepancy and almost amounts
to a statement that Satan hangs out in Heaven as
a regular thing.
When colds and influenza rage,
They add to life a dismal page,
They make us all look twice our age—
And scatter death around.
‘Tis then we find a friend so sure,
In William Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.
Which, always certain, always pure,
Will save us many a pound.*

CURED BY CUTICURA
IN TWO WEEKS
Mrs. George J. Steese, of 701 Coburn St.,
Akron, Ohio, tells in the following letter
of another of those remarkable cures of
torturing, disfiguring skin htimours daily
made by Cuticura Remedies, after phy-
sicians, and all else had failed: “I feel
it my duty to parents of other poor suf-
fering babies to tell you what Cuticura
has done for my little daughter. She
broke out all over her body with a hu-
mour, and we used everything recom-
mended, but without results. I called in
three doctors, they all claimed they
could help her, but she continued to
grow worse. Her body was a mass of sores,
her little face was being eaten away, and
her ear 9 looked as if they would drop off.
Neighbours advised me to get Cuticura
Soap and Ointment, and before I had
used half of the cake of Cuticura Soap
and the box of Cuticura Ointment, the
sores had all healed, and my little one’s
face and body was as clear as a new-born
babe’s. I would not be without it again
if it cost a fortune instead of the small
sum it cost us to cure our baby, after
spending much money on doctors and
medicines without any benefit whatever.”
