The Assassin of Public Opinion.
The Tory press of Australia seems to be un-
animously of opinion that an anti-Labor policy is
the best advertisement-canvasser. And the
campaign against arbitration is consequent on this
belief. Everyone knows how virulently that
has been carried on. There is no trick that has not
been practised in the way of suppression, distor-
tion, mis-statement, and even of deliberate
prevarication, concerning the proceedings of
the Arbitration Courts in N.S. Wales. Westralia,
and even of Maoriland. To such an extent has a
corrupt and contemptible anti-arbitration cam-
paign been carried on that Justice Cohen, on at
least three occasions, made lengthy and detailed
protests from the bench of the N.S.W. Arbitra-
tion Court against the unscrupulous manner in
which the local press had striven to prejudice
public opinion with regard to the decisions and
methods of the court over which he presided. As
for Maoriland, the Australian daily papers
have insisted that the arbitration courts of
that State were always congested with busi-
ness, that their decisions gave satisfaction to
no one—neither to employers, employees, nor to
the general public. But within a week or so of
the date on which these words were used by an
Australian daily, its M.L. correspondent was
permitted to inform its readers that Justice
Chapman had suspended the latest session of the
Arbitration Court at Auckland because there were
neither breaches of awards nor industrial disputes
submitted for its consideration and settlement.
Therefore, in a city which the Auckland Evening
Post once styled ‘ ‘ the storm centre of our industrial
world,” there is, again to quote that paper, “an
entire absence of friction and heart-burning.” The
M.L. paper, usually suspected of Tory proclivities,
is also honest enough to give the arbitration laws
credit for the change. But the journals of
most Australian capitals devote daily much time
to the poisoning of the wells of truth, until
it seems about time that some steps were taken to
suppress this constant belying of the country’s
laws and public institutions. It has been said
that the assassination of public opinion is a more
grievous crime than the assassination of a human
being. Why then should the hired stabber, whose
dagger is a fountain-pen, be longer permitted
impunity ? Why should it be considered wrong
to slander an individual while it is allowable to
defame a law, its administration, and the results
arising therefrom ? Is there to be protection from
the defamer for everyone, no matter how humble,
but none for the public conscience, the most
delicate, the most susceptible of all ? It is time
that something were done to make press-lying on
public subjects a less profitable occupation by pro-
vision for the punishment of any journal whicn is
found guilty of habitually endeavoring to mislead
and prejudice public opinion—the very soul of
the nation.
Bull Cohen and his French and Belgian brethren,
who own most of the Westralian mines, haven t
yet, in their thirst for dividends, introduced the
Chow, but, according to evidence given to the
Federal Old Age Pensions Commission when it was
in the West, they are doing the next worst thing—
they are ruthlessly sacking older miners who are
even a trifle past their bullockmg prime. One
witness swore that men who were showing streaks
of grey were getting into the habit of using dyes ;
and there is plenty of evidence that the mines make
men grey very rapidly. It looks as though Cohen,
Cohenstein, Coiienloup and Co. aren’t content
with simply rifling Australia of its gold, and leav-
ing the refuse in the tailing-dumps to pay the
deficit with ; but they also use up Australia’s
most virile human material, and when it shows
signs of wear they throw that also out on the
refuse dump. This is what comes of “bringing
capital into the country which means handing
the country over for exploitation to a foreigner,
who looks at it, not as the home of a nation, but
simply as a source of dividends.
*
During a debate in the House of Lords on Eng-
land’s system of Chinese slavery in S’Africa—
The Archbishop ot Canterbury admitted that the
Chinese were being accorded fair treatment, but hoped
that the moral aspect would be adequately considered.
Possibly the Archbishop is not to be blamed too
severely. He is human, and he is paid to be a
smug pillar of hypocrisy, not a priest and a teacher.
But the reek of his cant is none the less disgust-
ing. What consideration for morals can there be
in a system which herds scores of thousands of
men, away from their wives and families, in com-
pounds for several years at a stretch ? The Chinese
slave compounds at Johannesburg must be moral
hells, and the Archbishop must know that they
are and can be nothing else than hells.
Sachse, Vic. Minister of Education, wants
to establish museums in country centres, using
State school children as collectors. > Also, “ the
early colonists are fast disappearing,’ says Sachse,
and it is, in his view, desirable “to obtain well-
written interviews with them, and have them
framed and placed in State schools. Sachse
shows some discernment in coupling this idea
with that of a museum of curiosities. He must
have conceived the scheme while looking on Ben
Goddard, of Kyabram.
Rep. Bonython, of the Adelaide Adver-
tiser, is between two devils. One of them wants
his paper, and the other wants his seat. Ihe
’Tiser’s wobblesomeness led to the Labontes pro-
moting a rival daily, and the project is still moving
on ; while the ’Tiser’s belated, but in the end
vigorous, fight for franchise reform so riled the b.
Australian Tory party that now John Darling has
declared that he will run for Bonython s seat next
Federal election. But he had better not. He
ought to remember what happened to his political
chum, Sudden Solomon, who is now glad to grope
after a small State seat again in the back parts ot
S. Australia.
For a proof that the Sydney Telegraph
is merely a subsidised organ of the importing
party, compare the way it treats the exporter of
raw material and the exporter of manufactured
machinery. The Telegraph has always professed
an amazing desire to see this country build up
a vast exporting business To encourage the
people to tear raw material out of the earth and
off the face thereof, it has advocated unremunera-
tive railway rates, the right of the employer to
sweat his employees, and the absolute necessity
for the cheapest of cheap labor. All manner of
State assistance should be given to the „ poor
farmer,” the “backbone of the country. Ihe
State should build cool stores for his bullsgrease
and subsidise foreign shipping lines that they
might carry his wool cheap. But when the
Harvester-making industry comes abng-an
industry which pays wages on which men can
rear families and be creditable oitizens-andasks
not for assistance like the “poor farmer, but for
defence against illegal and unfair competition,
what a change on the face of Hie Ogre in King-
street Then the man who wants to build up an
export trade is an “ insatiable Protectionist, and
something between a public enemy and a pm-
jam.

Medical men evidently believe in Cleanliness
being the Handmaiden of Health (not to mention godli-
ness) judging from their enthusiastic attendance at Daly’s
Turkish Baths, Eoyal Arcade, Melbourne. And they like
their little joke as well as their little wash. Quoth one of
their prominent humorists the other day, midst a chorus
of approval, “ We send as many of our patients to you as
we possibly can, Daly, to have their diseases dissolved for
the time being ; but we don’t want to permanently injure
our practices in the interests of our fellow man.*
Bad Breath..
The Tongue is the Index.
Your breath is foul, and you do not perceive It
yourself. Like the person who is always among
the oils and varnishes, and gets used to what is to
many people a most obnoxious smell. It is most
disagreeable to those with whom you come in con-
tact. Look at your tongue in the looking-glass
every night and morning, and if it is at all dis-
colored take a couple of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills. They will remove the objectionable matter
from the stomach, regulate the system, and
sweeten the breath. One or two of these pills
taken two or three times a week will ensure a well-
regulated system, fortified against all diseases.
They cleanse the stomach, put life in the liver,
a move to the bowels and thereby are a positive
and permanent cure for constipation, biliousness,
sick headache, piles, pimples, boils, skin diseases,
and all disorders arising from imperfect digestion
and impure blood. Packed in amber bottles, and
sold throughout Australasia.*

It is good news to Commonwealth smokers that
“ Marsuma ” cigars have come here. Marsumas are made
of East Indian leaf, which is said by many experts to be
far superior to Havanna, and have the advantage of
being far more economical to smoke. The Havanna
cigar, says one authority, is living on a past reputation,
the Marsuma is making a present fame. Such judges as
Pinero, Conan Doyle, Henry Irving, and our own Edmund
Barton speak highly of the Marsuma.*

SPORTING NOTIONS
The Maoriland team of British Association
footballers will officially arrive in Sydney to-day,
and get down to serious business on Saturday, the
17th, when they will meet a Metropolitan team, at Epping.
Altogether, 11 matches will be played, three of which will
be inter-State—those on June 24, July 1, and July 22. As
the British Association people in Sydney play their style
of game better than any other footballers of that city,
these, matches ought to be worth seeing. It is possible
that in this campaign the Cornstalks will be successful,
and Maoriland may go back home with its dejected tail
dragging in the water.
• • • •
Of the Maoriland “ Soccer” football team which
landed in N.S.W. this week only five members played
against N.S.W. in the tests last season, when the Corn-
stalks were in Maoriland. All the rest are young players
who have come to the top since. The visitors will find
the difference between their own and the Sydney grounds
very much against them at first, but by the end of the
tour they should be reasonably accustomed to the harder
surface. Also, as they get hardened, the N.S.W. surfaces
will be getting softer.
• • * •
In Sydney, Pyrmont, Glebe and Rozelle head
the “ Soccer” competition with 10 points each, and
Ultimo is pressing close behind with nine points. The
once-powerful Granville club is at the bottom of the list,
with six matches played and lost; a fearful example of
how the mighty get blown away when the door is left
open. The team that is to arrive from Maoriland on the
15th was, just before departing, beaten by a Wellington
team by five to three. Which is generally the thing that
happens to the team that is going abroad. It seems to
chance that way, so that if the departing team loses on
its tour—“ Well, it was only a second-rate team ” ; and if
it wins—“ Whoop ! see what we can do even when we
have left our best players behind.”
# * * *
Under Australian rules the Y.M.O.A. won its
sixth consecutive match, North Sydney being the
victim this time. It vvas not quite the defeat inflicted
on Paddington a short while ago ; some remnants of the
vanquished were to be found among the ruins this time,
the score being 61 to 51 points. First quarter saw the
Good Young Men go ahead, but the'Northmen pulled up
so well during the second that the third quarter started
even. It didn’t long continue so, as the Good Young
Men got in some beautiful kicking and marking, and
went ahead with a rush. The North Sydneys took charge
in the last quarter, and scored at a great pace, but time
ran out before they could overtake the Good Young
Men, and the Y.M.O.A. had won its hardest match.
Paddington underwent a severe banging at the boots of
Sydney, which beat Neild’s suburb by 50 to 20. The
downfall of the day was West Sydney’s obliteration by
Newtown. Newtown ended with 121 points to West
Sydney’s 17, and when the ruin is so bad as that detail is
almost unnecessary.
* * * *
That Hebrew gentleman who wrote in Holy-
Writ that any race was liable to boil over, nearly had his
remarks endorsed in the Balmain-Glebe Rugby match at
Sydney University Oval on Saturday. Glebe was the
team that had, so far, not lost a game Balmain
had won a game, although drawing a bye gave
it credit for two wins on paper. Naturally it was
expected that Glebe would make dead meat of the water-
siders. And then the Scripture came nigh to being ful-
filled, for Balmain forwards played a clever game, and
smothered and bustled the enemy’s back division. At the
very first, George Pile’s suburb rushed Glebe down on to
its line, and a try was scored, and a great hush of surprise
fell on the people and deprived the barracker of the
power of yelling. Eventually Glebe pulled itself together,
and won by 6 to 3 ; but it was a tough fight, with the
issue always in the balance.
• * « *
The powerful-legged Rugbyites who roam
through the sand dunes of South Sydney met the wild
hillmen from North Shore on Saturday, and although the
hillmen secured the first score, the others had all the best
of it. Gradually they wore the North Shore men down,
and then ran over them, to the tune of 21 to 8. The
losing team seemed to have gone stale and to be played
out; and someone blundered by failing to replace worn-
out forwards with young players. Also, the South men
were aided by a player with the fearsome name of Remen-
schneider, which was itself almost equal to ten points.
University went down to Newtown by 14 to 3. It is now
becoming evident that the formation of the Sydney Dis-
trict Club has taken the sap out of the once mighty
Eastern Suburbs team. The unfortunate Easterns have
lost the whole six matches they have played, and are the
disgusted possessors of the only duck egg on the list.
* * * *
Melbourne Football Club —of the Yic. League-
moves to put large numbered tickets on the backs of the
players, so that the casual visitor may know who is who,
and little Lizzie may the more easily identify her boy. So
far the other clubs don’t see it and the League refuses
permission. If the League is wise it will continue in that
frame of mind. If the player with the extra-hard
head and heavy boot knows he is being watched indi-
vidually by persons who can identify him, he may
play to the gallery by putting on an extra spurt now and
then ; but in sport, as in politics, the individual but selfish
player who makes brilliant dashes is not the one who wins
games for his side. Co-operation beats individual play
every time. In an ideal game no one should be identifi-
able, save only the captains—and, of course, the referee.
He can be identified by the road-metal thrown at him.
* * * *
“ Critic ” : Football must be a very uninterest-
ing game in the Wimmera district of Victoria. Comment-
ing on the recent closely-contested match between
Horsham and Stawell, the Horsham Times says: “The
Horsham brass band was on the ground, and greatly
helped to break the monotony of the afternoon by playing
selections.” And this band recently entered for a c mpe-
tition and got sixth place. (There were six competitors.)
* * * *
Nothing unusual is transpiring in Victorian
football as far as the play itself is concerned, Fitzroy being
still undefeated and Collingwood having lost but one
game—to ’Roy aforesaid ; but the barrackers are getting
more willing every week. Fines of 40s. apiece, inflicted
by the Carlton beaks on a couple of “ supporters ” who
expressed their disapproval of an umpire too forcibly,
failed to urge these ill-conducted persons to more decorous
behavior; for last Saturday, at half-time, in the Associ-
ation match —Richmond v. Ports —a howling mob of semi-
lunatics swarmed across the low picket fence and climbed
all over umpire O’Loghlen before police or players could
grasp their intention. He was rescued, and refereed to the
finish, but had to be escorted on and off the ground. The
game was exceptionally close, Ports winning by 29 points to
27, and there was every excuse for excitement, but none
whatever for 200 alleged men attacking one soli-
tary umpire. For some years there has been bad blood
between these teams, and last time writer saw them play
a dozen mounted and a score of foot police were there to
restrain the barrackers. Association umpires get £1 Is.
per game—and they earn it.
» * * *
Of the 17 Rugbyites chosen to represent N.S.W.
against Queensland, eight are from the country ; in fact,
the back division is a backblox division.
* * * *
“ B.” : Private and public accounts received
from England describe the abominably cold weather in
which the Australian team was called upon to play its
first matches. It is perhaps to this that many dropped
catches may be ascribed. The Sydney press writers who
dispense the daily “ comments on the situation,” are very
severe on the failings of the fieldsmen, but probably they
don’t know what it is like when a frozen man tries to
catch a frozen ball in Greenland. But the bowling
averages of the Australians have a despairful look. At
the time rain stopped play in the Marylebone match on
the first day, Laver had taken 5S wickets—only one less
than Cotter, Howell, .Noble, and Hopkins combined, and
Laver only went to England to manage the tour, while
the other four went to bowl the English batsmen out, and
send J. Bull’s wicket into the centre of next week.
» * * *
Hirst, the Yorkshireman, seems to have got a
leg which may keep him out of all the test matches. The
wonder is that he has been able to do so much work these
last few years. His long run to the wicket, when bowl-
ing, includes a queer hop and a step, and his delivery of
the ball is of the laborious, back-spraining order. Nothing
of the free, easy, wiry cricketer about Hirst.
« * * *
Ringrose, the Yorkshire bowler, looks a very
valuable discovery for the Englishmen. Last season he
played only nine times for the county, and his bowling
was not of much account. Evidently he had no reputa-
tion when Yorkshire met the Australians in May, for
whilst the visitors were knocking up a long score,
Ringrose got very little show. His average was two wickets
for 36 first innings, and in Yorkshire’s second innings
he bowled but three balls. Since then he has probably
been coming on, for jn the return match he started the
Yorkshire attack, and justified his promotion by giving
the Australians “ fits.” Maybe he is merely a new
puzzle in bowlers rather than a fixed star, in which case
he will cease to be dangerous when he loses his freshness ;
but thusfar he has done great things. Nine wickets for
76 on a batsman’s wicket is about the hottest perform-
ance an Australian Eleven has ever experienced in
England.
* * * *
“B.B.” : R. A. Duff, up to and including the
Marylebone match, is the only Australian batsman who
has not missed an innings thus far in the present English
tour. He had batted 20 times, and Noble and Darling
came next with 17.
• • • *
Marmont and his full-brother Merrion are two
of the most interesting entries for the Y.R.C. Grand
Nat. Hurdle. Will the handicapper estimate Marmont’s
chance on the old fellow’s top “ form ”of two years ago,
or reckon him to be a little on the down grade ? And will
Merrion rank as the poor hurdle-race performer of yore,
or the comparatively respectable flat-racer who won a
minor event at Williamstown a few months back ?
* * « *
First pedigreed thoroughbred mare sent out to
Australia was Manto, landed at Sydney by Mr. T. Icely in
1825. A large number of noted Australian racers, includ-
ing Trenton, are descended from her. Several thorough-
bred mares were sent out prior to this by the British
Government, but their pedigrees never arrived.
* * * *
Crack jockey Hewitt turned up not long since
to ride at the Oamaru (M.L.) meeting, but wasn’t allowed
to mount until 3 p.m. At that exact hour and minute a
three months’ disqualification, imposed at Dunedin,
expired. They are painfully accurate at Oamaru.
* * * *
More than half the entrants for A. J.C. Epsom
Handicap are four and five years old —the most successful
ages in the history of the race. First Metropolitan was
run nearly 40 years ago, and since then no more than
three three-vear-olds and two aged horses have succeeded.
* * * *
As usual, Scobie’s (Ballarat) stable is more
strongly represented than any other in Melbourne Cup.
It sends 10 horses. J. Wilson, junior, ranks next with
eight.
* * * *
Entries for Melbourne and Caulfield Cups,
closed last week, reveal a shortage on previous years’
numbers. The 144 for Melbourne Cup is less than for
any year since 1899, and the 145 for Caulfield is three less
than the number entered in 1901, which was the smallest
entry for seven years. What this decline is due to Turf-
dom is unable to say.
* * • *
The trotting gent is in a class by himself. When
he makes up his mind to commit a robbery—that is to
say, when the whole of him agrees that it is Borndead’s
turn to win to-day—only accident or lunacy will frus-
trate him. It was partly accident and partly lunacy
that occurred the other day. There were ten starters
in a handicap trot. Betting opened with the usual
false favorite. Punters have an idea that it is good
business to wait for the favorite at the “ death-knock,”
and then go in. So it came that the usual rush just pre-
vious to the start sent out one an odds-on chance. Now,
the cunning crowd was not on this favorite, but the one
solitary trier in the race (Borndead’s horse), being ridden
in a wretched fashion, came round the turn for home
running third, with the favorite (going as slowly as it
dared) second, and Nothing-in-particular first. The case
was a desperate one, and two mannikins came to a simul-
taneous decision. The one on the favorite (second) de-
cided to turn his horse across the course towards the fence,
and thus lose as much ground as possible. The more
reckless one on Nothing-in-particular (first) decided to
slew his horse across the course, collide with the favorite,
and put them both out of the running, so that No. 3
(Borndead’s horse) must win. They both slewed simul-
taneously. The result was that the second horse wasn’t
there for the first to collide with, but the third one (Born-
dead’s horse) was. They went down (the rider of No. 3
never expected such treatment, and the thing was too
realistic), and the favorite simply had to win. And the
language destroyed the grass for three miles round.
* » * *
“J.M‘K.”: Re racehorses’ names. Very com-
mon are attempts to combine names of sire and dam,
which very rarely blend well. If the reversed-name fiend
should strike any of War God’s stock, we’ll probably have
“ Raw Dog,” and “ Spotted,” “ Tinned,” and “ Dry ” ditto.
Noticeable, by the way, that nags above the common
have mostly plain, pronounceable names.
* * * *
Women punters are becoming so numerous on
the S. Australian courses that the clubs are seriously con-
sidering the establishment of separate totalisators for the
exclusive use of the Sex which wears the beautiful hat
and can never make up its mind. But women don’t want
separate totes—they want to be on the machine where the
most money is invested, and totalisators patronised by
women alone would pay microscopic dividends. In
N.S.VV. and Victoria the mild bookmaker snorts wildly
when the woman punter approaches him. She has a
vague idea that he is a remnant, and tries to get him at a
reduction, and she bargains till Isaac feels as if he wanted
to go away behind the grand stand and be a dangerous
lunatic.
* • » *
Late English files disclose that the devil’s num-
ber, 13, was much associated with Vedas’ win of the
2000 Guineas. Vedas was No. 13 in the book ; rider drew
13th position ; and the colt’s starting price, 11 to 2,
totalled 13. Also, it was Vedas’ 13th race, and his was
the 13th preliminary canter past the stand. Further,
that day 13 years previously the colt’s trainer, W. T.
Robinson, rode a 2000 Guineas winner, Bona Vista, the
first and only time.
* * * #
English horse-trainer J. Cannon has, in 31
years, prepared 1240 winners.
♦ * * *
“Phoenix”: Spielers, dealers, stealers, bottle-
ohs, ladies of all ages ; also young girls and boys—most
of them ragged, and all dirty. The scummiest, rummiest
crowd, perhaps, in creation, gather on a vacant plot
outside Randwick racecourse. These people do not
chaff, banter, or joke ; there is nothing humorous about
them; all pushing and hustling in solemn, terrible
earnestness to get information before investing from a
“tray-bit” to a “deener” on that noble animal, the
horse. This is the “ outer ” —the outer-outer—and for
sheer blasphemy it is second to no place on earth. On
the fringe, the two-up school is busy. “ Heads a deener ! ”
“ Tails a sprat 1 ” “ ’Ere yer are ; tails a tray-bit 1 ” The
coins are spun, and then the losers perform. “Lord
stiffen yer, yer cow ! Why didn’t yer spin ’em ’igher ?
Now then, get back and give a bloke a filthy charnce.”
Said a “ lady ”to another, as they passed me : “I backed
Deemster for a bob, an’ ’ad a sprat for a saviour on St.
Leonards.” The “ sport of kings ” •
* * * *
The liveliest sport that Melbourne has seen
since the roaring days of old was provided, by the Weber-
Woods wrestle, at the Athenaeum Hall, last week. The
rival merits of the men had been discussed in the local
law court, when Woods sued Hackenschmidt for wages,
or something, and this match was to decide whether the
Russian had over-rated his young pupil Weber. The
question was soon decided. Weber slung Woods to the
floor and flattened him out in half-a-minute. When they
presently closed for the second bout (the match was for
best two out of three), Woods attempted a strangling
hold. Weber raised his hand in protest, and the referee
ordered a break-away. Immediately Weber repeated his
previous cross-buttock, and slung Woods fairly and
squarely upon his two shoulders, but the fall was some-
what off the mat, and Woods was ordered to place himself
in the centre of the stage, with Weber in charge of him.
The Englishman contrived to wriggle, and put his stum-
mick where his back had been, but, after some six
minutes of experimental work, Weber got a half-Nelson
hold, and levered the prostrate one over upon his two
shoulders. Within seven minutes, all told, the Australian
had taken three falls out of the “ champion of the British
navy,” though only two of them counted. Then Buttan
Singh sent up a challenge to wrestle Weber for £25, where-
upon the youngster threw off the coat he had slipped over
him, and said, “ I’ll wrestle him now— put him up.”
Buttan wasn’t taking any sporting offers of that sort.
The crowd chaffed the astonished nigger, he hung his
black head, and slunk out of the hall with his spokes-
man in tow.
* » *■ *
The lost Victorian mace has a less scandalous
parallel in the English Football Cup, a cheap but much-
prized trophy, open to the whole kingdom. Ten years
ago it disappeared from a shop window in Birmingham,
where it was being exhibited by the local club that had
won it. It was never heard of again.
• • • *
Old Jack Graham’s attempt to lay down
the laws for the Weber-Woods wrestling match was appar-
ently actuated by mixed motives. Graham was
bound to preach the obsolete doctrine of strangle in
order to justify the decision he gave at Wirth’s circus last
year, when, as referee, he permitted Buttan Singh to
squeeze the neck of young Weber until blood came from
his ears and he dropped insensible from the black man’s
grip. It was after hearing the account of the Buttan-
Weber contest on the morning of his arrival in Melbourne
that Hackenschmidt got the notion of converting the raw
young Australian into a better sort of champion than
black Buttan. The first notable result of Hack’s sports-
manlike idea was Weber’s ridiculously easy defeat of
Woods t’other night. It only remains for the youngster
to take his revenge out of the heathen who was allowed
to choke him at Wirth’s circus. As regards Jack
Graham’s apologies for strangling holds, the friends of
the veteran point out that he learned the game in the
old school, and is now speaking by the book —the old
school-book. He would let both men do “ all they know.”
Well, if Weber and Buttan Singh wrestled all they knew
next week, the Hindu population of Melbourne would
probably be reduced by one.
* * * *
The Geelong (Vic.) police lately tried to put an
end to the alleged “ sport ” of coursing rabbits with fox-
terriers in an enclosed paddock. They proceeded under
sec. 28 of the Vermin Destruction Act, which forbids the
wilful setting loose of bunny. The plea that defeated the
prosecution—that the rabbits were not set loose, as they
had absolutely no chance of escape—was in reality the
strongest condemnation of the practice from a sporting
point of view. The P.M. opined the Vermin Destruction
Act was not intended to meet such a case, and he was
probably right; but if the Cruelty to Animals Act doesn’t
cover the difficulty, it certainly should be made to. If the
object is to kill bunny, a gun or a waddy is speedier and
less painful. The assurance that he has no chance of
escape reduces the alleged “ sport ” to a level with the
practice of drowning, kittens in a tub.
♦ * m *
The cabled result of the Roberts-Stevenson
billiard match throws no light on the question whether
the younger artist was “ waiting for a wager ” during the
first half of the game, when Roberts gained more than
half the 2000 points he had conceded. Stevenson scored
faster than the old champion in the second stage, and
finally won by,1520 points— i.e., he made 16,000 whilst
Roberts was making 16,480. This leaves the grey-haired
man still at the top of the tree “ for figures,” but he has
been there, or thereabouts, for 35 years, and the chances
are that Stevenson will beat him on level terms when they
meet in a straight-out championship match. The strain
upon their nerves will be greater than it, was in the recent
game, and the young nerve ought to outstay the old.
Roberts, considering his age, is a more amazing player to-
day than ever he was, but he isn’t miraculous.
■* * * ■*
Maoriland has fixed a cold, thoughtful eye on
the bookmaker who lays totalisator odds. At Auckland
a penciller and his client were hauled up on six
charges, and the Rhadamanthus fined the bookmaker £lO
on each of the six imputations, and smote the client to
the extent of £2O. Then he announced that in future
he would deal out imprisonment in addition to a
fine. So there is horror in Israel, and the hair of Jacob is
perpendicular.
* * * #
Sydney baseballers are working up a mild milk-
and-water excitement. Woollahra and Paddington con-
tinue on their course unbeaten, and even at kiss-in-the-
ring there are thrills when two teams that have gone
nearly the whole round unbeaten meet to decide which
shall embrace Polly. The two teams before-mentioned
now lead the competition with 12 points each, and the
nearest rivals are Petersham and Chatswood, toiling far
in the rear with 4 points apiece.
* * * *
The N.S.W. inter-State lacrosse practising team
consists of seven players from Balmain, three from Bur-
wood, two from Marrickville, and four from North Sydney.
* # * *
It is pleasant to note that the pigeon-slaying
“sport” is coming into disfavor in England. Tony
Hurlingham Club has eliminated the barbarity from its
programme, after 37 years butchery, and the lesser fry,
who must slavishly follow the aristocratic lead or die,
will probably not be long in doing likewise.
* * * #
Balmain, Sydney’s most waterlocked suburb,
is forming a sailing club open to owners of 16 and 18
footers. It would give sailing a fillip if the electorate
system should be adopted, and Our Beautiful would have
to be enlarged to find room for the craft.
* * * *
The motor-journey from Melbourne to Ade-
laide has been made by Russell Grimwade, in 40 hours 50
minutes. Part of the way there is no regular road, and
the tracks along the Coorong were wet and greasy. The
run has twice been made in the opposite direction in a
trifle over 38 hours.
* * * *
To its credit, the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen
again biffed out Larry Corbett’s request to have
his disqualification lifted. This ought to about end the
appeals of Corbett, but a rider who has the “ face ” to
apply for consideration after the relation of the Corbett-
O’Brien case is not likely to be crushed by any feeling of
false modesty.
• * * »
Effete Britain came out on top when ancient
cyclist Sid Jenkins beat American Kramer in a semi-final
at Paris recently. The Briton “ jumped ” fully 200yds.
from home, and when Kramer settled down to catch him
the old man kept going at such a pace that the
Yankee found the contract too large. All the same,
it was a ease of tactics and not speed ; when Jenkins shot
over the line Kramer was only a wheel be hind him.
Saturday last saw the “road-racing season”
officially opened in N.S. W. by the Marrickville club’s ten
mile spin. Wheelers turned up in force, no less than 500
being present, as well as motor-bikers and people in cars.
Out of 106 entries there were 93 starters, and, although
the road was in excellent condition, falls were frequent.
Baker, of the Northern Suburbs, won the event, his
actual riding time for the ten miles being 30min. 12sec.,
while fastest time was put up by P. R. Ray, of Curzon
Park club, who hunted himself over the distance in
2Snnn. 58sec.
• * * •
That the chairman and secretary of the Federal
Cycling Council should occupy exactly the same position
on the Council of the Victorian League of Wheelmen is
not only an anomaly, but is utterly wrong. The C uncil
should be in all things absolutely independent of any of
the Leagues, and a man can no more conscientiously rep-
resent two cycling bodies than he can love each of
three wives best. Whenever the Victorian League comes
into conflict with the Federal Council, the secretary or
chairman must throw over the one or the other. The
E resent arrangement means simply that the Victorian
eague has undue weight in the Council.
* » * *
Ken Lewis, the well-known Victorian racing
cyclist, claims to have discovered—what many like men
have dreamed of—a solution that, stored between inner-
tube and tyre, will automatically seal up punctures before
the air can escape.
* * « *
The Fennessy-Farley battle at Queen’s Hall,
Melbourne, last week, appears to have been very savage.
At the gong they sprang at one another, and Farley
was sent to the boards five times. The second round
saw the tables turned somewhat, Farley managing
to batter Fennessy to the floor. Then all regard
for the M.Q. rules was abandoned, and the bruisers
held each other and bashed furiously-, wrestled, fell over
the ropes, punched wildly at each other as they fell,
punched wildly on the floor, and punched wildly while
getting up. The audience lost its head and yelled till its
jaw went lame, and the noise prevented the referee’s
voice being heard, so he went into the ring and per-
sonally tore the men apart as if they were
wet blotting paper. Meanwhile the audience fought
among itself, and gave the bruisers a well-
earned spell. The third round saw Fennessy riot-
ously aggressive, and Farley went down persistently.
Another free fight in the hall and a wild and deafening
dispute between the seconds and the referee caused them
to go to their corners till something like order was re-
stored. When the noise died down Farley announced he
was “ satisfied.” Fennessy was declared winner, and was
immediately challenged by Squires.
• * * *
The Whitelaw-Andrews disturbance at Sydney
National Sporting Club last week supplied what looked
like 17 vigorous rounds. Whitelaw was apparently in
dead earnest—rhe was on to Andrews right through,
and in return brother Montague stood in close and hit
with all his might. Andrews had the longer reach and
the superior skill, but Whitelaw was hard as a Christian
money-lender’s heart, and, disregarding Monty’s attacks,
he waded in and struck blows which looked as if they
ought to have killed. And yet it took 17 rounds of
battering, during which Brother Montague was harried
round the ring like a piece of brown paper in a whirl-
wind, before Andrews’ corner hoisted the towel of
unconditional surrender.

Premier Carruthers (N.S.W.) is develop-
ing a capacity for discovering huge, mysterious
undertakings, which threaten to break out at any
moment and provide work for 1,371.429 unem-
ployed. Recently he discovered two in one week.
One is a dark and secret co., that is bringing in
“over £400,000 for investment in developing the
shale industry,” and the other, he “learns, ’ will
work up the rabbit industry, and make a feature
of utilising the by-products, “on an enormous
scale,” at Forbes (N.S.W.). At the same time
Carruthers says that “ capital is being driven out
of this country ”by “socialistic legislation, and
simultaneously he asserts that Socialism is a good
thing that is only possible in Heaven.
Speaking to the people at Ryde (N.S.W.)
Premier Carruthers defended himself in these
words :
He did not object to criticism. There were people who
thought he was going about his work in the wrong way.
“An old dog for a hard road.” Some of those people who
wanted to teach him how to run the show had not been
as many minutes in politics as he had been years.
Theoretically this may be all right, but even a
good theory can be carried too far. When the
dog grows so old that he can’t keep up, and the
people in the waggon can just see him panting on
the rear horizon, they generally think it time to
get a young, fresh dog. And when the dog dis-
appears altogether, and begins to send collect
telegrams from places many miles back asking the
Van of Progress to wait for him, then he becomes
quite superfluous.
Melbourne Age, re our late Hempire and
Prince of Wales mafficks: —
Reasonable people may be excused for feeling, as they
hung out their domestic supply of bunting for the second
time within two weeks, there was a danger of over-doing
the thing. It is better to foster national sentiment by
such universal celebrations as Labor Day and Agricultural
Day, or a harvest thanksgiving festival, than by merely
sectional holidays or birthdays.
The yarn, fathered by G. H. Reid, and
published and republished ad nauseam by the Tory
press during the mail squabble, that the Orient
00. demanded an increased subsidy owing to the
white labor clause in the Postal Act, was given th«
lie direct at the co.’s annual meeting in London
(29/4/’O5). Frederick Green, president of the co.,
S The idea that they had asked for an increased subsidy
owing to this obligation was erroneous. They had simply
wanted a larger subsidy because they had come to the
conclusion that what they had been receiving was not
sufficient. There was very little difference in the cost of
employing white or black crews ; for more black men had
to be employed as they were not so strong as white men,
and the increased number compensated for the higher pay
given to white crews.
Which statement also bursts up that other lie,
patiently and sedulously asserted by Australian
nigger-worshippers, that stoking is a black man s
job. According to Mr. Green —and he ought to
know—the white man is stronger and better fitted
for it than the Lascar. And, as the Orient ships
now carry one-third the old number of stokers, it
seems that the white man is three times as well-
fitted for the job as the Lascar.

« J ” • During the See regime in N.S. W.,
A. was Chief Clerk of the Dog-Tax Department
and Registrar of Goat Licenses at a salary of £ooo
per annum. On the accession to power of the
Carruthers Ministry he was relieved of the chief
clerkship and appointed Registrar of Goat licenses
only, at the same salary, while B. was made Chief
Clerk at £5OO per annum, and C, and D. were ap-
pointed as assistants at £220 and respectively.
B C and D. are now recommended for a joint
Skry increase oi £l6O Thus, the work which
was formerly carried out by A., at a cost of £5OO
annually, is now performed by A., with the assist-
ance of B„ the help of C and with D as an aux-
iliary, at a total annual cost of £l4OO, and a
potential future cost of £1560 But let not he
Deformers be judged too hastily. , ’ ,
Government not increased the work of the loac.s
maintenance men as an earnest of its economica
intentions ?
At a time when the Westralian Govern-
ment is trying all it knows to buy out the Midland
Railway Co.—a concern which, many years
ago, grabbed some two million acres of prime
agricultural land in return for building, for .its
own sole profit, about 250 miles of a single-ane
railway—Bigjohn is going about the country
advocating the construction of more railways
by private enterprise. It will cost £1,000,000
to buy the Midland Co. out, and the railway is
not worth half of it. Settlement has been
retarded in that part of the country for years,
simply because the co. charged prohibitive prices
for homestead lots, and then sat down watching
other parts of the State being developed. Bigjohn
favors a line from Albany to Bridgetown, through
fine karri and jarrah forests all the, way, and says
that no difficulty Would be experienced in getting
someone to build the line if timber and some land
were given. But if the State builds the line it
will have the timber and the land as well as the
railway.

Thousands Owe
Their Lives to the
i Skill of This
| Wonderful Doctor.
I ♦
Seems to Possess Strange
and Mysterious Power over
Disease.
Offers His Services Free to
the Sick and Afflicted,
Distance no Barrier .
For the past three years accounts have come from
America of the most astonishing nature, regarding
marvellous cures that were being performed daily by the
famous physician and scientist, Dr. James W. Kidd.
At first these stories were received with considerable
doubt, but there continued to come reports of men and
fdh
women being restored to health after years of suffering.
Chronic and so-called incurable invalids were brought
back to perfect health. There seemed no affliction or
disease but what he might cure. Thousands of patients
were treated by the doctor until his name and the fame
of his marvellous remedies spread all over the civilized
world. Investigation has proved every assertion made by
Dr. Kidd. Some of the stories told and letters received
from grateful patients seem to indicate that this man’s
remedies possess a mysterious pow<»s 2~i£ase. No
matteLhow. tub case, how chronic, or how long
standing, what other men have said or failed to do. These
are the cases he especially solicits, because they give him
a chance to prove his superior ability.
When asked the secret of his success, the doctor
modestly answers that it is partly due to the wonderful
remedies which he uses and partly to the careful attention
given each case. He treats patients all over the civilized
world by mail, and seems equally as successful as though
they were present in his office. His greatest ambition is
to give every sick, afflicted and suffering man and woman
in the world a chance to try his wonderful treatment. In
an interview recently the doctor said: “I believe that
I, have discovered a way to relieve most of the suffering
from sickness and disease in the world, and I believe that
it is my duty as far as possible to make this fact known to
all. For this reason, lam offering to send a free treat-
ment to every applicant, rich or poor, no matter where
they live, no matter what their disease. lam not a rich
man. I cannot afford to always give my medicine away,
but I am going to continue as long as possible or until
every afflicted person has had a chance to try and satisfy
themselves without expending one penny.” Do you
mean that you will send treatment absolutely free to every
one who applies? was asked. “That is exactly what I
mean. Any sick or afflicted person who will write to me
and describe their condition may have a trial of this treat-
ment entirely free. There are no conditions, no restric-
tion. Distance is no barrier. I cure as readily thousands
of miles away as in my office. A letter does just as well
as a personal visit.”
The above statement appeared in all the leading
American papers and from there to the English press. As
a result Dr. Kidd’s mail has been flooded with applications
for free treatment. All have been answered promptly,
but still they continue to come. For the benefit of his
thousands of patients in the British Empire the doctor has
been compelled to establish an office in London. To
secure a free treatment, it is only necessary to address Dr.
James W. Kidd, 486 Saracen Buildings, Snow Hill, London,
E. 0., being sure to describe your case. Remember that
the doctor treats all diseases from the simplest and
easiest cured to the so-called incurable and chronic
afflictions. There is no disease that he may not cure.
In view of. Dr. Kidd’s marvellous success, his standing
among America’s most famous physicians, his reputation
for honesty and integrity, this offer affords a remarkable
opportunity to all who are sick and in need of medical
help. It means a chance to get well. It means the
opinion and advice of one of the world’s most famous and
successful physicians on your case absolutely free.

BUCKLEY & NUNN LIMITED, «€lbourN€.
ti Moreing and Co. intend to have a
shy ” at the new Dolcoath tin mine, Zeehan way (Tas.).
This is the property that shone out in th<* action between
Adams, a local journalist, and Trevis Moore, in which
Adams claimed an interest in the co. The penman won,
and if B.M. decide to put up cash there will be one more
ink-slinger safe. Hertzig has been round with a pickaxe
and a sample-bag at the mine, on behalf of the firm, and
great things are expected locally. Lode tin is a doubtful
quantity, and Heemskirk and Dundas mines have not
built up a reputation like. John Moffatt’s V.ulcan, up
North. Still, a little boodle might be worse employed
than in suggested by Hertzig to his principals.
There is not much frill about the directors of the
North Mt. Farrell (Tas.). All they were getting was 15s.
a week among six, and they wanted a rise to £75 per six
months. No one grudges them a living wage, much less
a little froth on the top of the pewter; they deserve
reward if only because of the model little balance-sheet
and report they turn out. They have also gone
‘ joint-and-several ’’ for the co.—a thing a man only does
when he believes in a mine; and they have put
the co. on the perch among the few profit-earning mines
of little Tassy. Costs last half-year worked out at 21s.
2id. per ton of crudes (including ss. Id. for development),
against 21s. 6Jd. for the preceding half-year; and net
profits were 7s. 3Jd., against Is. Bid. Mine to date has
yielded 96,583 tons, of the average value of £8 7s. 9d.
Directors want to bring in water from the Lake at the
top of Mt. Farrell to work the plant for half the year, and
so bring costs down.
* • * #
At No. 10 level of Great Fingall (W.A.), reef
splits 590 ft. north of shaft, but both branches are in pay
ore, hanging wall branch being worth 22dwt. for 6ft. wide
and 100 ft. long, footwall branch being worth 20dwt. for
Bft. wide and 160 ft. long. No. 11 north was barren for
173 ft., but for next 600 ft. was wide and payable. Develop-
ments are so far ahead of mill, and costs are now so low
that no anxiety is warranted at present. Capital expen-
diture is completed, so divs. must be big. Pay shoot
pitches north, therefore every subsequent level will have
further to drive through barren ground, of which bears
will make all capital possible.

Westralia is still able to provide fancy
Latest reported is from Invincible Extended, Siberia—
-8340z. from 13 tons. Mine is situated in Wealth of

* * * *
Northern Blocks Syndicate (W.A.) was formed
m May, 1903, with a capital of 350,000 shares of Is. each
to prospect three outside blocks of Associated Northern!
This prospecting failed, and work was suspended with
half the capital intact. Three months ago concern took
six months’oplion on Great Leviathan, six miles south of
Southern Cross. Kenny, its prospector, got very rich
stone near surface, but was not able to tackle deep sink-
ing. At 160 ft. reef has been cut, 4ft. wiab, worth 35dwt.
Drive continues in good ore, and, if next three months’
prospecting confirms this, syndicate will take over the
property. In addition, prospecting of Medindie Hill with
diamond drill has been resumed. This hill is east of
Brown Hill, and in direct line and adjoining Brown Hill
Extended’s eastern lode on north.
♦ * * *
Hainault, Kalgoorlie, has been doing well
lately, but output is being pushed for all it is worth. A
year ago reserves were given as 140,000 tons of 7dwt. ore
and returns practically confirm this. In April grade of
ore increased to Sdwt., and profits jumped from £l3OO to
£l9OO. Developments didn’t foreshadow this increase
and many believe that mine is being forced to let those
speculators who hold bulk of scrip unload. Co.’s financial
year closes on May 31, and it is said that Is. div. will
shortly be declared. May profits may run up to £2500 ;
but scrippers had better be vary, as unloading by insiders
is bound to follow any rise.
* * * *
The mostly-Adelaide people who were going to
float into great riches on a stream of oil in Taranaki
(M.L.) have given up the idea. They bored 2000 ft. or so
(which was a good deal deeper than their expert told them
they would need to go), got the usual “ indications,” and
then came to the same conclusion as scores of others who
have tried the game—that there wasn’t any money in it
Still the local (New Plymouth) people haven’t lost all
hope, and, with £ for £ help from the Government pro-
pose to probe a bit further, and settle the matter still
more definitely.

Quarterly divs. of half-a-crown are paid, and a bonus of
2s. per share on present 495,907 shares has been added the
last two years. Profit in 1903 was £364,944; in 1904
£370,153 (inclusive of £4264 interest). Twelve months ago

duced by heavy writings-off to £16,301; but there is a

tralia has Bendigo’s £1,244,790, J. Bull has had practically
the whole of Waihi’s £1,176,191. Yet some strange citi-
zens call the Waihi business “ bringing capital into the
country."

* * « «
H. Y. L. Brown, the cautious S. Aus. State
geologist, has had a look at the new tinfield near Port
Darwin (N.T.), where Hang Gong and some other Chinese
parties have made a comfortable rise. Tin country covers
about 5 sq. miles ; and deepest workings are about 20ft.
Tin ore is in granite dykes, which have shed some of their
metal. Brown says the dykes are irregular and the tin
patchy ; “ nevertheless I am of opinion that some at least
continue tin-bearing in depth, and large quantities of tin
are likely to be obtained from this area.” He hasn’t often
written anything more definite and emphatic than that.
Yet, for all the good it is to Australia, most of the Hang
Gong field might almost as well be in China.
Two more Boer war heroes have gone to
gaol in Melbourne. Judge Molesworth called it
robbery and cowardly assault on an elderly man,
and said something about “18 months.” How
many farm-burners are out of gaol just now ?
A Sydney hat manufacturer, who went
before the Tariff Commission and asked for more
Protection, said he knew skilled hatters who, not
finding work at their own trade, were now wharf-
lumping. Australia won’t give them work making
hats ; it only gives them the job of dragging out
of the foreign ship some of the £427,876 worth of
foreign hats and caps which were imported last
year. And the Argus and the Telegraph, and
the rest of the Freetrade crowd who howled them-
selves hoarse because six hatters were kept wait-
mg in a comfortable ship for a day or two while
their boss filled in some necessary forms, don’t
raise even half a howl when the same hatters are
kept waiting for weary years on the wharves. In
fact, it is the Argus and the D.T. and the Free-
trade crowd who keep them waiting. ’
Is This Like Tours?
Man Describes the Horrors op
Stomach Troubles ” and How He was Cured.
Millions of men and women are to-day suffering
the tortures of pain from various diseases, but
there are few among them all who can and do
describe correctly the symptoms of their disorders.
m.L W inni e I s a l of so recent date as December
Jth, 1904, from Thomas Frederick Watts, in which
he describes so clearly what he felt that anyone
who has similar symptoms can tell at once what
™ probabilities are of securing benefit from
Mother Seigel’s Syrup, the same remedy that Mr.
v\ atts used. Read the letter for yourself :
t £ two y ears , an d ten months back since
i first fell ill. I went right off ray food and was
unable to sleep. My strength ebbed away until I
became pitiably weak. The little food I ate
punished me dreadfully, and I was never free from
pam and aches. My tongue was furred and my
throat so sore that it hurt me to swallow even a
mouthful of water. I thought my heart was
aiiected, as it fluttered and beat most irregularly
and was subject to spasms of intense pain. I
became so thin and feeble that I could scarcely
walk, and finally had to give up my employment.
I was then attending the Sydney Hospital as an
out-door patient, and was being treated forindiges-
tion and general debility. I also had a gargle for
my throat. But in spite of all they could do for
me at the hospital I grew steadily worse, and
began to give up all hopes of ever getting right
a & ain- I had been unable to work for ten weeks,
when a fellow-patient, who was being treated at
the hospital for injuries received accidentally,
strongly urged me to try Seigel’s Syrup. Though
1 really thought it .useless, I bought a bottle on
my way home. To my delight, after taking it for
a tew days only, I began to pick up rapidly. The
soreness left my throat, my heart-action became
regular, and I could eat without fear. I kept on
taking the medicine, and was soon back at my
work and thoroughly cured.”
Mr. Watts has lived at 168, Henderson-road,
Alexandria, Sydney, since 1890, and is an
Australian by birth, and well and favorably
known m the community where he resides.*

“II exham ” : Professional musician O’Brien, writing to
a Sydney daily, says the Bavarian band engaged at
Wave Hey tother day could not play “ Gor Save,” &c.
Must lie mistake. The air of the official Sauerkraut Im-
perial hymn is the same as that of “ Gor Save,” as is also
the Swiss national air, and a Bavarian band that didn’t
know Kaiser William’s hymn would have its trombone
confiscated for lese majesty.
Premier Bent, of Victoria, in his role of comic singer at
Ballarat, yelled in an outrageous voice a couple of Dick
Thatcher s old-time melodies. Thatcher (oft mentioned
in The Bulletin) was a singer of the ’so’s, and a great
favorite on the goldfields, particularly at Bendigo. He
wrote.verses.on topical events to faniiliar airs, and sang
“ 111 a Volce quite unlike Bent’s. It was characteristic
of Thomas to select “ The Bathing in Bullock Creek ” for
rendition before a mixed audience: The song relates an
incident which cast a gloom and a disrespectful feeling
overthe city long, long ago. It is descriptive of a picnic
of theatricals and important Bendigonians to Bullock
Creek. The men wandered off, leaving the ladies by
themselves, but rushed back on hearing terrible screams.
Then they found that the ladies had taken a dip in the
waters of the creek, and were covered with voracious
leeches. It is a very nude melody after that. Thatcher
described the. incident in song the same evening to a
strictly male audience. Bent, the Blunderbore of Aus-
tralian politics, sang the same shrieking ditty to an audi-
ence of eminently respectable fathers and mothers, and
their unmarried daughters, in the Princess Theatre.

or two after, all the stage hands engaged upon Belasco’s
new play Sweet Kitty Bellairs” suddenly left the
theatre, for no stated reason. Next day they were
employed by the Syndicate Theatres. This happened at
Washington. A fortnight later, upon returning to New
York to produce his play, Belasco found that the ropes in
his fly-gallery had been fouled, knotted, twisted, snarled
and tangled. Again he was deserted by his trusted

Transpired in Queensland Supreme Court
the other day that it has been oustomary by some
employers of Kanaka labor to deduct from their
wages the pay for each day that they are laid-up
by sickness or accident. And the poor nigger’s
pay varies from £6 to about £l2 per year.
“J. A.C.” : Re the alleged cleanliness of
the Japanese. The authoress of “Unbeaten
Tracks in Japan.” wrote thus, 25 years ago,
about the population of some remote inland
districts:—
These people wear no linen, and their clothes, which
are seldom washed, are constantly worn, night and day,
as long as they will hold together. They seal up their
houses as hermetically as they can at night, and herd
together in numbers in one sleeping-room, With its
atihdsphferb vitiated, to begin with, by charcoal and
tobacco fumes, huddled in their dirty garments, in
wadded quilts which are kept during the day in close
Clipboards, and are seldom washed from one year’s end to
the other. The tatami (mats) beneath a tolerably fair
exterior swarm with insect life, and are receptacles of
dust, organic matter, etc. The hair, which is loaded with
oil and bandoline, is dressed once a week, or less often in
these districts, and it is unnecessary to enter into any
details regarding the distressing results. The persons of
the people, especially of the children, are infested with
vermin, and one fruitful source of skin sores is the
irritation arising from this cause.
“ C.C.H.” : Have come to the conclusion
that small men are greater swearers than big men.
My theory is : A big fellow need only say, “Go
and do so-and-so,” and it is dono ; a little chap has
to say, “ Go and do so-and-so, damn you ! ” and
perhaps it isn’t done even then.
“ Wilhelmina S.” : A culchawed insurance
clerk gave me the information that those in
“business” left their employment at five ; those
at “ work ” left at six. This throws some light on
the difference between the gentleman who has
just accepted a position and the fellow who has
got a job.
Two or three secret murder societies have
lately introduced a gory element of romance into
the life of the Italian colony in San Francisco.
When the amputated head of a signor is taken to
the morgue, the trouble is to get any of its rela-
tives and friends to swear to it. They just look
and shudder, and say nothing, lest the chairman
of the association that decreed the murder should
mark the “informer” for slaughter. A Sicilian
who was chopped to pieces in San Francisco, the
other week, is said to have paid the penalty o.
having raised a subscription for burying a previous
signor who was murdered. Seems that the
mangled corpse of anybody whose name ended in
an “ o” while he was alive is a matter to be care-
fully ignored.

A spell in a private hospital is coming' to be entered up
as a regular part of a star engagement in Sydney.
Nance O’Neil, it is reported, is bringing a complete com-
pany with her. The last time the lady was in Australasia,
a locally-recruited member of the company got the
horrors on the way over from Maoriland, and, with a shirt
clothing his thinness, and a razor in his hand, chased the
ample lady into hysterics. Perhaps that incident has
given her a bias against Australian actors.
“Mao B.": Was present one night at a literary-
medico’s house when Carrie Haase electrified a small
audience by her wonderful impersonation of Lady
Macbeth. It was the sleep-walking scene, and Carrie’s
mother afterwards remarked to me that she could not
realise that Carrie was her daughter ; never thought she
could be so terrible. Wilson Barrett had a very high
opinion of Miss Haase’s ability.
At New York, recently, Louise Thorndyke Boucicault
was married to YV. G. Cheney. He is her third husband.
Dion Boucicault the elder met Louise Thorndyke in
Australia, and, after obtaining an American divorce from
his first wife (Agnes Robertson), married her. Bouci-
cault’s first wife would not recognise the divorce, but
was unsuccessful in an action against him, and also in
contesting his will. After Boucicault died, Louise Thorn-
dyke married again, but obtained a divorce last year.
George IV., as Prince Regent, and Mrs. Fitzherbert,
appear with astonishing vraisemblance in Mack’s Tom
Moore play. George married Mrs. Fitzherbert, according
to the rites cl' her church, the Roman Catholic. The
news got abroad and made a frightful row. George put
up Fox to deny it, in the House of Commons. He gave
Fox his word of honor that the marriage had never taken
place. George IV.’s mistresses included Ladies Conyng-
ham, Jersey, and Hertford. He worshipped his old
clothes, and never would part with any. The whole
wondrous collection remained after his demise. In the
pockets there was £lO,OOO scattered over almost innumer-
able pocket-books which he had forgotten.
The plot of a new playlet staged in London, the other
week, is an amusing example of artificial dramatic effect.
An ambitious artist is promised the hand of his best girl
if he can get a picture accepted at the Royal Academy.
He chooses for his subject “ The Outcast,” and takes his
boozy brother Bob as the ideal model. Bob has a capital
outcast appearance, and the great work is getting along
nicely until Mrs. Bob begins to reform her husband. Next
time he sits he is a sober, decent-looking model, so his
brother lures him back to the bottle in the interests of
art, restores him to the ranks of picturesque outcasts,
and completes the portrait with a few brilliant strokes of
his inspired paint-brush. Then the moral climax. Indig-
nant Mrs. Bob destroys the picture, and the best girl
discards her loving artist in disgust at his conduct.
Probably she fears that if she were to marry him he would
incite her to beer and put her on canvas as “ A Fallen
Angel,” or murder her to get, a good corpse model. The
cockney audience is said to have been deeply impressed
by this play. ’Arry and ’Arriet accept it as a painful
reflection on the depravity of Art.
“0.K.”: The speech that Rickards delivers to his “ kind
patrons ” from the stage when he is about to make another
of his periodical trips to Europe, Asia, Africa, and
America in search of “the latest novelties, ladies and
gentlemen,” is, to my way of thinking, an excellently
harmonious utterance. It blends Rickards, the old-time
coster impersonator, with. Rickards, the manager (past
and present), and lays them like flattering unction to the
soul of the kind patrons. The style of the speech hasn’t
varied in the last 20 years, but long practice has given it
more ease, fluency, and point than of yore, without
diminishing its warmth of ’eart. It is a speech that
prompts one to exclaim, “ Good old ’Arry ! ” Heard him
again the other night at Melbourne Opera House, and
noted how he struck right ’ome to the audience’s ’eart—
the “very bottom, of its ’eart” so to speak—by an-
nouncing that for the future he would engage only ex-
pensive turns abroad, having come to the conclusion that
he could find plenty of bright young local artists who
would please his kind patrons better than second and
third rate performers such as he had previously imported
by the half-dozen for make-weight purposes.

Bulletin hasn’t the least idea what it is about, but it

partly an Impression. Most of all, it seems to be a Tall
Pale Howl: —“ Cobb’s coach was flying through the city
to-day by way of advt. for a show—the St. Vincent’s Hos-
pital Fete. Cabbage Tree Ned was up, and his quartette
of spankers galloped like those of Stein’s fire-escape.
Inside were G. V. Brooke, Coppin, Joey Gougenheim,
Fanny Oathcart, Charley Young, and the managers of the
Bank of Australasia, Bank of Victoria, Colonial Bank,
and Bank of Elegance, each with a thousand sovereigns.
The Escort galloped around. ‘ Hey up ! ’ shouted Cabbage-
Tree Ned. Away went the coach out at the north end of
Elizabeth-street. At Keilor the panting horses were
taken out, and a much inferior-looking team substituted,
but rare ’uns to go ! The lame one turned out the best of
all. Soon they came to the Corduroy. Bump went a
bank manager’s cranium through the oilskin top, and he
only laughed. However, he didn’t think it so funny
when they passed Velvet Ned and three other Garibaldi-
shirted horsemen, in the Black Forest.”
“Yarrab”: “The Pierrot” (B. 18/5/’O5) is rather con-
fined in his knowledge of things theatrical when speaking
of William Anderson, lessee and manager of Sydney
Palace. W.A. is no neophyte as an entrepreneur. When
only a fledgling he was connected with the Princess
Theatre, Bendigo ; and fully 12 years ago he managed
that' versatile trio, Gourlay, Walton and Shine. Then
followed the successful tours of George Leitch, Grattan
Riggs, George Darrell, Alfred Dampier, Maggie Moore and
other well-known stars through all the principal Victorian
towns. Eleven years ago he took a pantomime company
(curiously enough called “ Sinbad the Sailor,” and iden-
tical in title with the one he is now running through
Groperland) on a tour through Victoria—his troupe
consisting of 60 people. Nine years ago we find him, in
conjunction with Charles Holloway, lessee of the old
Alexandra in Melbourne, and just after that time he
figured in a managerial capacity with George Rignold,
when the latter successfully toured Australia. In 1899
he opened—John F. Sheridan being the star—at Sydney
Lyceum ; and, at the same time, he and Holloway were
running a company at the Melbourne Royal. Since then
his ventures have been many and varied ; and to-day he
is simultaneously running companies in New South Wales,
Maoriland, Victoria and Westralia. Surely such a record
can hardly be spanned by the space of “ about five
years.”
Sydney Zoological Society does not advance with the
societies in other States, and occasionally has to sell an
elephant or a lion to raise money to buy old horse for the
other animals. The trouble is, of course, finance ; but
without improving the gardens it is difficult to see how
the society will earn more, and, barring the acquisition of
a few cheap bears and ostriches, the show seems to have
declined instead of improved of late years. The society
wants more land and an experienced theatrical man—one
who will have every animal in the grounds capable of it
saddled up, the monkeys schooled to play cricket, and
each beast trained to do its turn. Matinee days and gala
nights would draw crowds; and the Polar bear in his
famous high dive, the chimpanzee troupe of acrobats in
their swing act, and the ship of the desert at sixpence a
ride might pull the finances out of the mud in no time.
The blackballing of Ellen Terry for the Lyceum
gromen’s) Club has caused Mrs. Craigie (“ John Oliver
obbes”) to resign her membership. The ostensible reason
for the blackballing was that Ellen Terry had not written
anything, but Mrs. Craigie asserts that she has published
several articles on Shakspeare, while some of the black-
balling cats have “ merely written a paragraph or so for
a fashion paper.” John Oliver delivered this burst of
scorn from the lofty pinnacle of a dozen novels and half-
a-dozen plays, with the limelight full on—for she loves it
as dearly as any actress,
A crowd of supers is generally as queer a conglomera-
tion of human atoms as fortune can gather together. It
often includes ex-bank managers, solicitors, remittance
men, disqualified jockeys, sailors, and an odd mixture of
all trades, generally Wasters. There are a few stage-
struck enthusiasts with aspirations for a theatrical career.
There is a well-known old super in Sydney who was 15
years ago a highly-esteemed bank manager, and several
who have established record times in getting through
money. ; j
At Balmain (N.S. W.) it cost the council
£BOO more for scavenging by contract during 1904
than in either of two previous years under day
labor. Balmain is the shabby, overcrowded suburb
which returns Bill Wilks to the Federal and S. J.
Law to the State Parliament because they are
Orangemen, and it is said that the local council is
also run on strict sectarian lines. If so, no won-
der the ratepayers suffer. And they deserve to
suffer. Any fool who votes a man into an Aus-
tralian public body because of the Boyne Water
or the Walls of Derry, is such a mischievous fool
as to merit any sort of misfortune.
Mr. Frederic Manning, of Sydney, has
been writing to London Outlook, combating the
Stinking Fish cry:—
I would protest against a paragraph in your paper on
the subject of Australian mails, in which you refer, pre-
sumably to the Australian Labor party, as “ the forces of
political larrikinism.” Larrikins we certainly have, too
abundantly, among our politicians, and I am neither a
partisan nor an apologist of the Labor party; but in
common-fairness to them I should like to point out to you
that they have never allowed the prospect of personal
gain to influence them, and that they are the only political
organisation in Australia of which that can be said.

Restoration to Health.
(By “Sano.”)
One of the supremest joys of life is the experience of full-blooded health after one has suffered
bodily pain for a lengthy period, especially when the illness has been of such a nature that nearly all
hope of recovery has been abandoned. In these days of strict attention to hygiene, diet, and physical
culture, the maintenance of good health has been reduced to a science, and it is comparatively easy to
remain well and fit when once in proper form, whilst there are a large number of disorders that afflict
humanity which now can be avoided, or readily cured, if the origin of such disorders is understood.
It is quite common, still, to regard Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Sciatica, Gravel, and
Stone, Bladder Troubles, Biliousness, Indigestion, Jaundice, Sick Heahache, Anaemia, General
Debility, and most blood disorders as being of a specific nature, each calling for independent treat-
ment, when, in fact, all of these disorders have their origin in an inefficient action of the kidneys and
liver. The importance of the work which nature has apportioned to the kidneys and liver will be
more fully realised by a short account of their respective functions.
The kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains
in weight of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from the third of an ounce to
nearly an ounce. If the kidneys are working freely and healthily all this solid matter leaves the body
dissolved in the urine, but if, through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their work
properly, a quantity of these urinary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins,
contaminating the whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning such as Rheumatism,
Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, Gravel, Stone and Bladder
Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are healthy is to place some urine, passed
the first thing of a morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until next morning. If it is then
cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural color, or has particles floating about in it,
the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must immediately be taken to restore their vigor, or
Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many manifestations of uric poisoning will result.
The liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made
from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The liver takes
sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it
to the blood, as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble,
into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles which have
lived their life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from some
form of biliary poisoning such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia, Jaundice, Sick Headache, General
Debility, and Blood Disorders.
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys and that done by
the liver, that where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys, the liver becomes
affected in sympathy and vice versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this
close union of the labor of those vital organs which resulted in the discovery of the medicine
now known throughout the world as Warner’s Safe Cure. Certain medical men, knowing
what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine could be found which would act s peeifically on
both the kidneys and the liver, devoted themselves to an exhaustive search for such a medium, and
their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in compounding a medicine which possesses
the required quality in the fullest degree. Warner’s Safe Cure exhibits a marvellous healing action in
all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys and liver, and restoring them, as it is able to
do,- to health and activity, it, of necessity, cures all complaints due to the retention in the system of
urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the kidneys and liver naturally eliminates the
poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons cease. Cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure
are permanent simply because they are natural. [9].
