Sixty Miles Away.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE little clock clicked on apace, and yet the
rain fell still;
And from my warm, thick pillow I could
hear
The dreary drops drop endlessly upon the wooden
sill
And the wet gum-boughs shuddering with fear ;
While ever in my mind I saw the wrinkled,
shallow pool,
Though sixty miles by hill and dale away,
Where round and round the body of the poor, self-
slaughtered fool
Swirled in the windy, yellow-water spray.
And then, again, the urgent mouth, the breath,
the gypsy hair _ f -
Of her who, sleeping at my side, did lean
Her body’s bribe for all this sin and merciless
despair—
“Ah, God! What penance, say, can make me
clean ? ”
And still the rain fell down, and still the wind, all
big and wroth,
Stormed in the chimney like an angry sprite—
When on my cheek there crept from off the
counterpane a moth
In search of company and warmth and light.
Anon. I shook the horrid thing to earth, and
heard it fall
Upon the carpet near the great white bed,
Whence soon it, whirring, rose and fastened at the
plastered wall
But scarce a hand’s breadth from the woman’s
head.
It was the kind of Moth which Death has marked
out for his own,
And through my veins there came a sudden sense
Of murder to be done. Fate at my feet his gage
: had thrown.
And no escape lay anyway from hence.
And once again I saw, though sixty miles by hill
and dale away.
By hill and dale, the wrinkled shallow pool
Where round and round, backward and fore, be-
spent by spuming water-spray,
There swirled the poor unwept self-slaughtered
fool.
The rest was certain : Guided by the Moth, now
at her throat,
Charr’d black upon the silver of her skin,
I sped her spirit, scarlet, to old Charon’s wherry-
boat, 1 ' ■;
And gave Corruption easy entrance in. ’ ,
The little cldck clicked, on apace, and yet the rain
felt still,
A nil from my warm, thick pillow I could lica/r
The dreary drops drop endlessly upon the wooden
sill—
And the wet gum-boughs shuddering with fear.
N.S.W.
HUGH McCRAE.
Dingoes I Have Met.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f SPHERE be many sorts of dingoes, the
black, the spotted, the brindle, the yellow;
but all live and work the same way—by
cunning. The plain and forest dingoes hunt in
small packs, to master the big game there; the
scrub dingoes hunt in pairs, the game only afford-
ing a meal for two; the town dingo singly, so that
none shall share the spoil.
I met my first dingo one morning in the creek.
We had all wagged it from school to go swimming,
gleaning from Aunt’s sorghum and melon patch on
the way. A big log spanned the swimming hole,
and made a slippery bridge when the creek was
up. As we sunned ourselves under it after a
hearty lunch of melon and “ sugar, ” a big, gaunt
dingo trotted oilt of the' bush and paused in the
log-middle to look round. He was yellow, with a
black back and a ferocious head. We all yelled
together, thinking how funny it would be to see
him go crashing into the bush on the other side.
He made one scared bound, slipped on the log,
and next minute was snapping savagely amongst
us. A couple of the smallest kids got trampled
into the mud in the struggle; then the weaker-
minded broke back to the school and told on us.
Heavens! how the teacher belted us next
morning. ‘ ... v - i •
Then Aunt sent Bill up to the selection to live,
and she also sent some of her pet poddies up to
be weaned. One day when I was there, wag-
ging it as usual, we heard the calves’ blare; a big
bitch dingo had the apple of Aunt’s eye down, and
was trying to drag it into the bush for her pups.
Bill let go his old musket at her. When the
smoke cleared away, the bitch was speeding into
the bush, and aunt’s prize poddy was kicking its
last on the ground. Bill got there first and stood
looking thoughtfully at it, his foot on its forehead.
I could see no bite 3. Bill thought the fright must
have killed it.
“ There she is again,” I shouted excitedly. Bill
turned round sharply, forgetting to keep his foot
still. There was a neat bullet-hole in the calf’s
forehead.
“Lor!” said Bill, in a dazed way, “the bullet
must ’a glarnced hoff. I alius heerd as" they
dingoes were tough.”
We buried it under a peach tree in the garden.
I had Bill under the whip after that.
A little afterwards I invested ss. in a half-bred ;
a selector near by, Who was breeding pups for the
scalp-bounty, sold him to me. This bitch was--
something of a kangaroo dog and something of a
collie ; he assured me the pup would be a ringer,
the father being my old friend of the log.
I called the pup Tiger. He turned out a great
cattle-dog, though he insisted on going away
bunting every now and again. I had lost' him
for a couple of days this way, when I went over to
gee a selector whose place fronted our new road.
The unemployed were then joyfully skimming it
o.ver (on borrowed money). It lay between Camp-
belltown and Bulli. .
Yarning in the hut (the selector was sixty and
I was ninje), swopping opinions about Rarkes, we
heard the patter of feet outside. Before we could
get to the door, the unemployed’s road-boss had
darted in upon us like a hunted devil, knocking
my host into the fire. It took three nips of
“ still ” whisky to bring him round ; then he told
his story. He was walking down the road by
himself, when he was suddenly bailed up by a
ferocious brindle dingo as big as a mastiff. After
a life and death struggle, weaponless, he had
strangled it barehanded, then, feeling faint, he
had run down to us.
He had barely finished when we heard some-
thing else pattering towards the hut; next
moment a large yellow dog, with pricked ears, hove
in sight.
“ It's’im again,” yelled the road-boss, and fell
under the bunk. The dog trotted into the hut,
and lay down sleepily at my feet. It was good
old Tiger.
He was a demon to fight, and had such a repu-
tation that no dog in Liverpool or Campbelltown
would tackle,him. Many a Sunday morning did
he uphold his supremacy in the backyards there ;
I became quite a noted character as “ th’ nipper
wot owns th’ yaller fightin’ dorg.” Next to Aunt’s
game fowls, which Bill used to bring in muffled in
a soojee-bag, the steels clinking in his pocket, and
fight against other fanciers’ birds during church-
time, I was easily the most notable character in
the district. I lost Tiger two years later. I saw
my friend, the dingo-breeder, going into town with
a bundle of scalps, and noticed one amongst them
marvellously like Tiger’s.
The next one I struck was in Lower George-
street. I was 14, and wanted to buy a beagle-
hound (greyhounds had gone out). No. one in
Liverpool or Campbelltown was complete without
a few in those days, and I wanted to be in the
fashion. With los. of hard silver I boldly adver-
tised for “ choice bloodhound or beagle bitch, must
be young,” and was snapped up by Lower George-
street. Went down with a light heart and a
strong chain in my pocket.
The fat old dealer eyed me up and down, and
then led out a blear-eyed, rusty old thing with no
teeth.
“Pretty as paint,” he said, heartily. “Ain’t
she a beauty?”
“Pretty old,” I answered, looking at her gums.
“What do you want for her?”
“ Only twenty-five bob,” he purred, as if giving
her away.
“I’ll give you eighteeenpence,” I said, readily.
This disgusted him.
“ Bah,” he snarled, kicking the old thing to the
back of the shop again, where the smells Were
strongest, “you don’ wanter buy a dog; you
wanter steal one.”
“Well,” I retorted, getting near the door, “if
I couldn't steal a better one than that I’d drown
myself.”
So, we parted.
I Worked for a dingo for a while. He had a
briissplate up, “ J. Skinnem, Financier,” and a
beautiful ad. in the papers, offering to lend money
without security, as he was a Christian gentleman,
arid didn’t want any prpfit. The constant inrush
of the sold-up widows and orphans got on my
nerves, so I left him and went selecting in the
Warrio scrub.
• ■ Thedirstr’sefection I took up was full of dingoes.
They used to howl round the hut at night, and
creep into the beef-cask if I left the door open. I
shot several in self-defence. After that they left
me in peace for a couple of months, till one night
I heard the. familiar crunching of bones outside.
Peering through a orack in the slabs, I saw a
dingo, pure white he was, fleckless. Made up my
mind to catch him, and left scraps outside for the
purpose. He got quite tame, but as soon as the
hut door creaked would disappear like white mist,
to show himself against the moon on a tangled
network of charred logs 50 yards away.
Nothing would induce him to come inside the
hut and be caught, so at last I got full of him.
“ Come in here, you coward,” I shouted, angrily,
in dingo language. “ I won’t hurt you, you fool. ”
“No fear,” he answered, from the logs; “I
don’t like the look of that rope you’ve got. You
might sell me to the Zoo., or something equally
horrible. I had a brother got caught that way
once ; took him three years to get loose again. If
you’ll come out and sit on this log, I don’t mind
having a yarn with you.”
Being an albino, he told me, was not all fun.
‘ ‘ You’re so noticeable, ” he explained. * ‘ Liable
to get shot any minute. Then the turkeys and
paddymelons can spot you so easily, sneaking on
them ; and if you go near a hut, the fools always
try to catch you for a curiosity. If I were a
brindle now ” he sighed enviously.
We got quite friendly, though he would never
come inside the hut. He stopped with me for a
couple of months, and used to keep me posted
about the best places for turkeys and flock-pigeons ;
then he disappeared suddenly, and I never knew
what became of him.
I left this selection, and took one nearer human
beings, where I saw cedar-getters once a week.
The dingoes were scarcer, the only troublesome
one being a big brindle brute with flop ears. His
mother had apparently been chased (and caught)
by a mastiff. This beggar used to stalk me about
the scrub in the day-time, and howl dolorously
round the hut at night, just outside the palings.
He would escort me home from scrub-falling every
night, just beyond rifle-shot, on the off-chance of
my getting bushed. (All ways are one in the
scrub when the sun goes down.) One of the
cedar-getters had a dog with a great reputation
for dingo-killing; so I borrowed him for the
brindle, who was getting on my hqjves.
Jim Doolan lent him readily;"he was a big
nondescript brute, with an unearthly appetite.
“ This’s th’ way I ketches’m,” confided Jim to
me “Th’ol’ dorg runs’bout four chains ’ead ’o
me; then er dingo nips outer th’ scrub at ’im,
thinkin’ e’s lorst. Th’ ol’ dorg hollers ’n ketches
holt, ’n I run up wid a broken bullick bow in me
’and, the dingo watchin’ me all th’ time ’n not th’
dorg. Then Raowdy’s got ’is grip ’n we kill ’im
atween us. I sell th’ skelp fer fifteen bob t’ a’
Armidale cove, ’oo gits a paownd f’r it there. They
aint no baownty in this districk.”
I kept Rowdy for a few days, but the brindle
was too 'subtle; so, fearing a famine, I gave
him back again. About a week after Jim passed
in great distress.
“ That blasted brindle o’ yourn ’as killed
Raowdy,” he informed me. “ 01’ Raowdy wuz
playin’ th’ game ’bout a mile back, ’n Mister
Brindle jump outer th’ scrub ’n cops ’im be th’
throat afore ’p could yelp. I never heered no
noise, ’n’ w’en I come up there’s Brindle a-worryin’
the larst kick outer Raowdy. I wood’n’ a took
ten paown’ f’ that dorg. Y’ orter pay me f’ ’im, I
reckon, seein’ uz it wuz yore dingo as killed ’im.”
I left the scrub after that and came to Sydney,
owing the Ideal storekeeper'a. few pounds, which
I promised to pay him when convenient. He got
impatient in about a fortnight, and sooled a fierce
black collector-dingo on to me for payment.
This one wrote first in a pained way : “Would
you please settle the little account ? ” Treated this
with the contempt it deserved ; besides, I had no
spare money. Gpt another in a few days : “ Will
proceed against ypu if account not settled.” Wrote
to the storekeeper in a friendly tope, advising him
to. have nothing tq do with such offal. Got p
frantic yelp next (expect the hope of commission
was fading): “Will take proceedings imme-
diately.” Wrote again kindly to the creature,
advising him to take a box of Beeeham’s pills.
Reply came by return post (letter fairly smelt
of sulphur): “ Will issue summons for you.”
Replied through the week. Told him (still
kindly) that I was just fixing up my horses and
gear for a trip out to the Gulf with cattle, but if
he would meet me at the mouth of a certain gully
in these ranges, about dusk, I would settle up in
full with him. Silence ; dingo evidently winded.
Stills are barely paying now, and it’s hard to
earn an honest penny ; but a horse-planter told me
yesterday that scalps are worth £5 each in the
Burragorang valley, and £1 for pups.
Am going to look at a dingo slut fqr sale at
Bankstown to-morrow.
FALDEB.
A. E. Morgans, ‘ he Westralian who is find-
ing the money for the Essendon-Fleupngton (Vic.)
tramway, is accumulating numerous irons in the
fire. A year or two ago Morgans was exclusively
a mining man. To-d jy he partly owns a news-
paper, draws rent from much property, is direct-
ing the tramway scheme aforesaid, and wants to
build a railway as well. The railway—Port Hed-
land to Marble Bar—is the dream of Morgans’
life, and Premier Daglish seems inclined to assist.

A Song of Work.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE sing too much of sports and such — too
little of toil and deeds ;
Too much of love and stars above, and
winds in the whispering reeds.
Lot’s sing new songs of the toiling throngs—let’s
chant of hammer and saw ;
Let’s chant of life and steam and strife and the
foundry’s blazing maw !
Sing ho for Work—for the smoke-stack’s murk—
for the crash of iron and steel;
Huzza for the war where the pistons soar and
crank-shafts thrust and reel!
For the world’s great heart is beating here, ’midst
clamor of steel and steam ;
Man rules the earth with his strength and skill—
aye, man is a god supreme !
Aha for the crash where the forges flash, and the
anvils clank and clang;
Huzza for the beat of the iron feet where the
Nasmyths bounce and bang !
For the whirring drills and the roaring mills—for
the shafting’s rolling song—
Huzza for the gods and the piston-rods and the
workers stern and strong !
For I love the night where the Titans fight, and
the gloom is blotched with flame ;
Aye, I feel a king when the crossheads swing and
growl in their iron game ;
Fit kingdom this, where the forges hiss, and the
red slag drips like blood ;
O, I love grim Graft, and I love eaoh shaft, and I
love Work’s mighty thud 1
Let’s whine no more—let us smelt black ore—let’s
grapple at last with Fate;
Let’s march like men in the ranks again—let’s
tramp with a swinging gait !
Let us take our stand with the nations grand—let
us show that we’re not played out;
Let’s live, let’s act —let us deal with fact —have
done with drivel and doubt.
Have done with dreams and the misty themes—
have done with the dread of death ;
Let’s work like Hell for an age-long spell—let’s
laugh with our parting breath !
Nor prate nor preach, but rise and reach for the
nearest task at hand ;
bet’s toil, let’s sweat—let’s fight on yet for the
sake of our own dear land !
For we sing too much of sports and such — too little
of toil and deeds ;
Too much of lover and stars above, and winds in the
whispering reeds.
Lets sing new songs of the toiling throngs — let’s
chant of hammer and saw;
Aye, chant of life with its steam and strife and the
foundry's blazing maw 1
Sing ho for Work—for the smoke-stack’s murk—
for the crash of iron and steel;
Huzza for the war where the pistons roar and the
crankshafts thrust and reel I
Set the world's great heart a-beating here, midst
clamor of steel and steam ;
Let's shake the hills with ou/r iron wills—for Man is
a god supreme.
A.
GRANT HERVER.
In the Cretin Country.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WENT to Varallo (in the Italian Alps),
by way of Stressa, to Orta over the Monte
Motterone, an unnecessary mountain of
5000 ft. high, and returned to Novara from Varallo
by railway. The sanctuary of Varallo is very
prodigal in the matter of chapels—there are 46
attached to the Church of the Sanctuary, which
has been a place of great resort by pilgrims since
good Bishop Borromeo visited it in 1584. But it
was certain, under any circumstances, to have
been popular with pilgrims, because Varallo is
1480 ft. above sea level, and the Sanctuary another
600 ft. above Varallo. It is a queer fact that pil-
grimages were never made to sanctuaries on a plain
and within comfortable travel of acity. Sanctuaries
were always built on the most difficult mountains
to be found.
At Varallo, again, I was struck by the greater
cleanliness of the Northern towns, and by the
absence of beggars. Notices prohibiting begging
are displayed at every street corner. But the
courtly Tuscan habit has disappeared, and the soft
voices and the luxurious climate and vegetation of
the South are re-placed by Swiss, German, and
Tyrolean harshness.
It was the day before Good Friday, and Varallo
had its usual Easter procession of the Crucifixion.
In Genoa Christ is represented in these proces-
sions by a man. In Varallo the prettiest youth
in the town had been selected as the Christ. His
hands and feet were painted to represent the
bleeding wounds of the stigmata—anticipating
the denouement, for he bore his cross, and the
procession represented the events immediately
following the scourging. His face was also bloody
and his brows were crowned with thorns.
His disciples followed, represented by grades of
youthful handsomeness, according to the rank
tradition assigns them in the Church. Lastly
Judas, a horror of a man ; a dwarf with a matted
beard and yellow teeth, and a goitre to his breast-
bone. The representation was repulsive to the
verge of beastliness.
» * * •
I left early next morning for the “mines.” The
road ascended steeply for all the distance. I
climbed seven miles of wall, which greatly taxed
the strength that was just returning to me after
three months’ illness. The road is peppered with
well-painted shrines. A favorite subject is St.
Peter with his foot upon a stuffed lion.
Although the day was Good Friday, the steep
cobbled mountain paths were dotted with peasant
girls carrying, in the great baskets that extended
from above their heads to below their hips, bushes,
manure, firewood or wine barrels. They were
newly-washed girls, too, and in festa dress—a
chemisette and a zouave jacket, short skirt and
grey stockings, and boots of undressed leather—
but they worked all the same.
All the time I was in Valsesia, I saw no other
beasts of burden in the country. Centuries of
labor-had greatly uglified all types. Only once
did we meet a pretty girl, and the Corporale’s one ’
eye wandered and revolved as he looked at her.
She was in the company of a woman laden with
half a cart-load of hay, who was the official
portatrice of the Commune. When we resumed
our climb the one-eyed shift-boss said, “Bella
ragazza ! Tutto sangue e busto ” (a beautiful girl
—■all blood and bosom).
A cold wind blew off the snow and down the
gorge we struggled to ascend, and necessitated an
overcoat, so that, between the half-freezing of the
wind and the perspiration induced by weakness
and exercise, over an hour passed before we
stood at the neck of the gorge.
And then for a moment, it was worth the
struggle. Ahead was a snowy little tableland
chequered by peach-trees in full bloom. Immedi-
ately behind them, the background of the snow-
covered mountain threw back the color of the
peach-bloom and exaggerated its depth by the
reflection.
It was necessary to climb this mountain at its
most precipitous point. Arrived at the summit,
I looked over into Switzerland, but drew back
again quickly, for fear the Swiss should see me and
puckalow me.
Now that you are locked in by the mountains,
tread lightly, for you are on unholy ground. This
is the home of the deadly jodeller, and the land
of the poisonous “ tira lira,” and of the father of
all such as say or sing “ Lu-lu-liety,” or cry
“Milk-ho !” with variations.
The general air of unintelligence in the sodden
faces of the people we passed at these heights
was very striking. Stupid cretins carrying horse
burdens ; pigmy and misshapen men, wry in the
face and twisted in the limbs; men with long,
curling chins, splay lips, and shaking mouths,
yellow-tusked and matted of beard, came down
these little paths and muttered to themselves as
they walked. There were also very undersized
women with Chinese beards, who bore their horse-
burdens through the leafless chestnut groves and
gibbered to themselves ; horrible old beldames
who sprayed themselves with rheum as they
talked ; and many half-naked, beastly, and goi-
trous dwarfs.
This is the chamber of horrors of rural Europe,
without a doubt. Some constituent in the water
is alleged to be responsible for this idiot asylum,
but there must be other very serious contributing
causes.
My idea of the origin of these people of the
Upper Valsesia is this : Two lunatics—-one male
and one female—escaped from their asylum, and
she said : “ Where shall we go ? ”
Replied the man : “We must getaway from
these madmen who put us in that asylum. They
live on the plains, so let us go to the mountains.”
“ I love the mountains,” said the lady lunatic.
So they went there and multiplied exceedingly,
and all the people who live in mountains are mad,
even unto this day.
The festa dresses. of these girls of Valsesia d©
not vary much in form in the many communes.
The bodices are usually well-fitting—the skirts
generally appear to have been cut out by a drunken
tailor with a squaring axe. Thus the effect is
always to make them hulky-waisted—and in some
cases the waist is carried so high that it is a sur-
vival of the Directory period, and in others it is
so' bulky in addition t© its height as to be more
than a suggestion of the dresses of the “ Bals a la
Victirne” after the fall of Robespierre—every
fashionable citizeness pretending to be in an
interesting condition, “by means of mere pillows
and stuffing,” says Montgaillard.
The “mine” was not a mine, but merely a
gratuitous hole in the earth, as usual, so I left the
snow, and the goitrous cretins, and the bearded
ladies who gibbered under the chestnuts, and the
railway took me to Novara and thence to Milan.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Moving of De Jersey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOON after we were married we went to
live in a row of dove-cots in Jay-street, North
Sydney. Ours was No. 9—“ The Nest.”
No. 11 was ‘ 1 The Nook.” I made the acquaintance
of “The Nook” by borrowing his saw. Their
name was De Jersey—superior people, but affable.
They lived in Jay-street because of. the lovely view
across the vacant land opposite. “The Nook” is
small, of course, and only 18s. 6d., “butsreally,
you know, the aspect is delightful.” We had
barely settled down in “ The Nest ” when a jerry-
builder started to run up a row of pigeon-boxes
opposite, and ran them up with amazing rapidity.
The gentility of the dove-cots was seriously threat-
ened while the view from their windows was wiped
out. Emphatic was the wrath of De Jersey, and
his scorn was withering.
“ What decent people,” he demanded from the
verandah to the unheeding jerry-builder, “can
live here with those places there ? Look at them !
Cribs ! Just cribs ! Look at that hall, made out
©f a packing-case; and the rooms the size of a
mousetrap! Where are the Health Department
people ? Can’t someone stop the man ? Who is
he building them for ? Does he know any pigmies
to let them to ? Where’s our beautiful view ?
Aren’t people’s rights to be respected ? Well,
one thing’s flat. Out we go, pretty quick ! ”
One day my wife- ran out to meet me just as
the houses opposite were being finished. “ Jim,
dear ; I’ve been over the Houses opposite. They
are really not half bad. Such quaint little corner
fireplaces with the sweetest mantelpieces. And
such a cunning glassed-in verandah at the back.
It’s an extra room, really; and they have more
land than these. .And, Jim, dear, the rooms are
not really much smaller. And only 135., Jim.
And, dear, Mr. Dodson has built another row at
Crow’s Nest. Just the same, and he says he will
let us have one for 12s. 6d. on a lease. Crow’s
Nest is not really such a very poor neighborhood,
after all, and you know it is nearer the boat than
we are here.”
She finally played on my feelings with th#
saving of 6s. a week. “But what will I say to
De Jersey ?He will be just horrified.” I weakly
remonstrated. The thought of telling that scion
of an ancient stock that I was going to live in a
13s. house owned by that worm, the jerry-builder,
was grievous.
“ Oh, tell him we can’t stand the houses opposite;
and, besides, we want to live in Mosman to be
near mother.”
I’m a poor liar, but De Jersey didn’t cross-
examine me. He was full of his own plans.
“ I’ve bought a place at Killara,” he said.
“ Killara’s rather far up, and, of course, expen-
sive ; but the people are decent, and there are the
golf links. And besides” (glaring at the offend-
ing Dobson, busily fixing “ Murielville ”on the
gable of No. 24 opposite) “ you can’t be out
there.”
They moved first, and my wife and I waved a
long farewell as their pantechnicon toiled up the
hill. It came for us a week later. Getting into
Rawson Villa was a squeeze. After a vain,
glorious struggle with a curtain pole, I was again
forced into making the acquaintance of next door
by asking him for a loan of his saw. Climbing on
the fence, I discovered Northcote Villa painfully-
beating a carpet spread over a clothes-line. A
towel was wrapped round his head.
“ Could you very kindly oblige ”
I stopped, for under the towel I had recognised
De Jersey.
